Disaster costs skyrocket in Canada: report: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/disaster-costs-skyrocket-canada-report/docview/433566871/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada - ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts -has skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says the Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report. "The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate. It suggests a disaster-mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Disaster+costs+skyrocket+in+Canada%3A+report&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=03841294&amp;title=The+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-04&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at 10 times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada - ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts -has skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says the Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at more than $1.1 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January 1998.
Cost to Insurers Soared
In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters.
Over the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's soaring disaster costs, which are increasingly borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests man-made climate change may be the reason.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said computer models show that as Earth's atmosphere warms, its ability to soak up moisture increases. That means, he said, that more moisture will be available in the atmosphere to fall as rain or snow.
"It's still very difficult to sort out what the major causes of the enormous increase in disaster losses are, but climate change is almost certainly a factor," Bruce said yesterday.
Bruce was one of the Canadian representatives on a United Nations scientific panel that studied global climate change and its potential effects on the world's weather systems. The panel's 1995 report became the foundation for the Kyoto agreement, which commits the world's industrialized countries, including Canada, to greenhouse-gas reductions over the next decade.
The three primary greenhouse gases - carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide - are produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline.
The EPC report, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
The report also predicts:
n Extremes of the hydrological cycle, floods and droughts, will become more frequent, with heavy storms increasing in frequency by more than 50 per cent. What are now 100-year floods, like the Manitoba's Red River flood of 1997, could become 50-year occurrences.
n Annual lightning strikes could increase by 44 per cent, damaging as much as half of Canada's boreal forest over the next 50 years.
The report argues that since weather-related disasters are likely to become more frequent - and more expensive to taxpayers - it makes sense to launch a national emergency preparedness plan.
The federal government paid out more in 1998 for disaster assistance - nearly $1.3 billion - than it did in the previous 15 years combined. Last year, Ottawa paid out another $312 million in disaster assistance.
Bruce said the federal government now leaves disaster-prevention work entirely to the provinces, even though it continues to write large cheques in the aftermath of storms and floods.
A federal program to limit flood damage was abandoned during the deficit-inspired budget cuts of the mid-'90s.
"The need to encourage timely, cost-effective means to save lives, reduce property damage and limit disaster costs has never been more apparent," the EPC report concludes.
Tighten Building Codes
It suggests a disaster-mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.
The reports also said Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.
Illustration MARJE FLETCHER, SOUTHAM NEWS GRAPHICS / Financial losses from severe weather escalating in Canada ;

Disaster costs skyrocket in Canada, report finds: Financial losses from severe weather increase 10 times faster than rate of economic growth: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/disaster-costs-skyrocket-canada-report-finds/docview/358259703/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes. "The need to encourage timely, cost effective means to save lives, reduce property damage and limit disaster costs has never been more apparent, particularly with the prospect of extreme weather events becoming more frequent and severe due to climate change," the EPC report concludes. It suggests a disaster mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.

Full text: OTTAWA - Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at 10 times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts --have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests man-made climate change may be the reason behind the more frequent and costly storm episodes.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said computer models show that as the earth's atmosphere warms, its ability to soak up moisture increases. It means, he said, that more moisture will be available in the atmosphere to fall as rain or snow.
"It's still very difficult to sort out what the major causes of the enormous increase in disaster losses are, but climate change is almost certainly a factor," Bruce said Monday.
Bruce was one of the Canadian representatives on a United Nations scientific panel that studied global climate change and its potential effects on the world's weather systems.
The panel's 1995 report became the foundation for the Kyoto agreement, which commits the world's industrialized countries, including Canada, to greenhouse gas reductions over the next decade.
The three primary greenhouse gases -- carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide -- are produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline.
The EPC report, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
THE REPORT ALSO PREDICTS:
- Extremes of the hydrological cycle, floods and droughts, will become more frequent with heavy storms increasing in frequency by more than 50 per cent. It means what are now 100-year floods, like the Manitoba's Red River flood of 1997, could become 50-year occurrences.
- Annual lightning strikes could increase by 44 per cent, damaging as much as half of Canada's boreal forest over the next 50 years.
The report argues that since weather-related disasters are likely to become more frequent --and more expensive to taxpayers -- it only makes sense to launch a national emergency preparedness plan.
The federal government paid out more in 1998 for disaster assistance -- $1.2 billion -- than in the previous 15 years combined. Last year, Ottawa paid out another $312 million in disaster assistance.
Bruce said the federal government now leaves disaster prevention work entirely to the provinces even though it continues to write large cheques in the aftermath of storms and floods.
A federal program to reduce flood damage was abandoned during the deficit-inspired budget cuts of the mid-'90s.
"The need to encourage timely, cost effective means to save lives, reduce property damage and limit disaster costs has never been more apparent, particularly with the prospect of extreme weather events becoming more frequent and severe due to climate change," the EPC report concludes.
It suggests a disaster mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.
The reports also said Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.
"It is tragic to note," the report said, "that the failure to be able to cope at this level causes many, if not most, of the casualties in the immediate aftermath of a disaster."

Disaster costs skyrocket in Canada: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/disaster-costs-skyrocket-canada/docview/352473356/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts --have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report. Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes. It suggests a disaster mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Disaster+costs+skyrocket+in+Canada&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=11979941&amp;title=North+Bay+Nugget&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-04&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA -- Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at 10 times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts --have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests man-made climate change may be the reason behind the more frequent and costly storm episodes.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said computer models show that as the earth's atmosphere warms, its ability to soak up moisture increases. It means, he said, that more moisture will be available in the atmosphere to fall as rain or snow.
"It's still very difficult to sort out what the major causes of the enormous increase in disaster losses are, but climate change is almost certainly a factor," Bruce said Monday.
Bruce was one of the Canadian representatives on a United Nations scientific panel that studied global climate change and its potential effects on the world's weather systems. The panel's 1995 report became the foundation for the Kyoto agreement, which commits the world's industrialized countries, including Canada, to greenhouse gas reductions over the next decade.
The three primary greenhouse gases -- carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide -- are produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline.
The EPC report, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
The report also predicts:
- Extremes of the hydrological cycle, floods and droughts, will become more frequent with heavy storms increasing in frequency by more than 50 per cent. It means what are now 100-year floods, like the Manitoba's Red River flood of 1997, could become 50-year occurrences.
- Annual lightning strikes could increase by 44 per cent, damaging as much as half of Canada's boreal forest over the next 50 years.
Emergency plan
The report argues that since weather-related disasters are likely to become more frequent --and more expensive to taxpayers -- it only makes sense to launch a national emergency preparedness plan.
The federal government paid out more in 1998 for disaster assistance -- $1.2 billion --than in the previous 15 years combined. Last year, Ottawa paid out another $312 million in disaster assistance.
Bruce said the federal government now leaves disaster prevention work entirely to the provinces even though it continues to write large cheques in the aftermath of storms and floods.
A federal program to reduce flood damage was abandoned during the deficit-inspired budget cuts of the mid-'90s.
"The need to encourage timely, cost effective means to save lives, reduce property damage and limit disaster costs has never been more apparent, particularly with the prospect of extreme weather events becoming more frequent and severe due to climate change," the EPC report concludes.
It suggests a disaster mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.
The reports also said Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.
"It is tragic to note," the report said, "that the failure to be able to cope at this level causes many, if not most, of the casualties in the immediate aftermath of a disaster."

People: Bruce, James

Disaster costs skyrocketing: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/disaster-costs-skyrocketing/docview/345809161/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters.During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes. "The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Disaster+costs+skyrocketing&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=08373744&amp;title=Expositor&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-04&amp;spage=A.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA - Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at ten times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts --have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20 to 30 times over, says Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters.During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests man-made climate change may be the reason behind the more frequent and costly storm episodes.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said computer models show that as the earth's atmosphere warms, its ability to soak up moisture increases. It means, he said, that more moisture will be available in the atmosphere to fall as rain or snow.
"It's still very difficult to sort out what the major causes of the enormous increase in disaster losses are, but climate change is almost certainly a factor," Bruce said Monday.
Bruce was one of the Canadian representatives on a United Nations scientific panel that studied global climate change and its potential effects on the world's weather systems. The panel's 1995 report became the foundation for the Kyoto agreement, which commits the world's industrialized countries, including Canada, to greenhouse gas reductions over the next decade.
The three primary greenhouse gases --carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide -- are produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline.
The EPCreport, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
The report also predicts, extremes of the hydrological cycle, floods and droughts, will become more frequent with heavy storms increasing in frequency by more than 50 per cent. It means what are now 100-year floods, like the Manitoba's Red River flood of 1997, could become 50-year occurrences.

People: Bruce, James

National disaster plan advocated as costs soar: Extreme weather increasing in frequency, severity: [National Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/national-disaster-plan-advocated-as-costs-soar/docview/329567234/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45-billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39- million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes. "The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climatically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, titled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=National+disaster+plan+advocated+as+costs+soar%3A+Extreme+weather+increasing+in+frequency%2C+severity&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-04&amp;spage=A.4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA - Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at 10 times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts -- have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, says the Emergency Preparedness Canada (EPC) report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45-billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec in January, 1998. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39- million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for the country's rapidly rising disaster costs, which are increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climatically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, titled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests man-made climate change may be one factor behind the more frequent and costly storm episodes.
The EPC report, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
The report argues that since weather-related disasters are likely to become more frequent --and more expensive to taxpayers -- it only makes sense to launch a national emergency preparedness plan.
The federal government paid out more in 1998 for disaster assistance -- $1.2 billion --than in the previous 15 years combined. Last year, Ottawa paid out another $312-million in disaster assistance.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said the federal government now leaves disaster prevention work entirely to the provinces even though it continues to write large cheques in the aftermath of storms and floods.
A federal program to reduce flood damage was abandoned during the deficit-inspired budget cuts of the mid-'90s.
"The need to encourage timely, cost effective means to save lives, reduce property damage and limit disaster costs has never been more apparent, particularly with the prospect of extreme weather events becoming more frequent and severe due to climate change," the EPC report concludes.
It suggests a disaster mitigation strategy should identify potential flood plains and restrict development on them; develop building codes that recognize bridges and buildings will have to bear heavier snow loads and withstand higher winds; and encourage municipalities to build storm sewers that can handle increased rainfalls.
The reports also said Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.

Full text:   Financial losses from severe weather events in Canada have increased at 10 times the rate of the country's economic growth since the mid-'80s, says a study prepared for the federal disaster agency.
The cost of weather-related disasters in Canada -- ice storms, windstorms, floods and droughts -- have skyrocketed over the past 15 years, rising 20-to-30-fold, says the Emergency Preparedness Canada report.
Insured losses peaked in 1998 at $1.45 billion, largely due to the ice storm that devastated parts of Ontario and Quebec that January. In 1984, Canada's insurance industry paid out only $39 million in claims as a result of weather-related disasters. During the past 15 years, Canada's economic output has doubled, but neither the increasing value of Canada's economic infrastructure nor inflation can account for rapidly rising disaster costs, increasingly being borne by the federal government, the report concludes.
"The much more rapidly increasing rate of loss from climactically related disasters such as floods, storms and droughts may indeed suggest that these latter hazards may be increasing in frequency and severity," says the study, entitled Disaster Mitigation and Preparedness in a Changing Climate.
The report suggests that man-made climate change may be the reason behind the more frequent and costly storm episodes.
James Bruce, one of the country's leading climate scientists and an author of the study, said computer models show that as the Earth's atmosphere warms, its ability to soak up moisture increases. It means, he said, that more moisture will be available in the atmosphere to fall as rain or snow.
"It's still very difficult to sort out what the major causes of the enormous increase in disaster losses are, but climate change is almost certainly a factor," Bruce said Monday.
He was one of the Canadian representatives on a United Nations scientific panel that studied global climate change and its potential effects on the world's weather systems. The panel's 1995 report became the foundation for the Kyoto agreement which commits the world's industrialized countries to greenhouse gas reductions over the next decade.
The three primary greenhouse gases, carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide, are produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline.
The EPC report, which calls on Ottawa to spend money now to mitigate future storm-related damages, notes that climate models predict Canada will experience shorter, warmer winters marked by more intense storms that are likely to produce more flooding.
The report also predicts:
- Extremes of the hydrological cycle, floods and droughts, will become more frequent with heavy storms increasing in frequency by more than 50 per cent. It means what are now 100-year floods, such as Manitoba's Red River flood of 1997, could become 50-year occurrences.
- Annual lightning strikes could increase by 44 per cent, damaging as much as half of Canada's boreal forest over the next 50 years.
The report says that it makes sense to launch a national emergency preparedness plan and that Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.

The report also said Canadian families should be encouraged to prepare for weather disasters by equipping themselves with enough supplies to survive for 48 hours on their own.
"It is tragic to note," the report said, "that the failure to be able to cope at this level causes many, if not most, of the casualties in the immediate aftermath of a disaster."

Disaster bill tops $80 billion in '99: [5 Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/disaster-bill-tops-80-billion-99/docview/313463864/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: A total of 702 major natural disasters such as earthquakes and hurricanes occurred worldwide in 1999, exceeding the 700 recorded in 1998, according to Munich Re. Total economic losses amounted to some $80 billion in 1999, the company said. This figure has been exceeded only twice before -- in 1998 ($93 billion) and 1995 ($180 billion, as the result of the earthquake in Kobe, Japan). The insured losses of about $18 billion were the third highest in this century following 1992 ($26 billion, the result of Hurricane Andrew in Florida) and 1994 ($21 billion from the earthquake in Northridge/Los Angeles).

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Disaster+bill+tops+%2480+billion+in+%2799&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Journal+of+Commerce&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-03&amp;spage=8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   CATASTROPHE LOSSES
A total of 702 major natural disasters such as earthquakes and hurricanes occurred worldwide in 1999, exceeding the 700 recorded in 1998, according to Munich Re.
Total economic losses amounted to some $80 billion in 1999, the company said. This figure has been exceeded only twice before -- in 1998 ($93 billion) and 1995 ($180 billion, as the result of the earthquake in Kobe, Japan).
The insured losses of about $18 billion were the third highest in this century following 1992 ($26 billion, the result of Hurricane Andrew in Florida) and 1994 ($21 billion from the earthquake in Northridge/Los Angeles).
Losses from the violent storms that swept Europe in the last week of December have not been included in these figures. Consequently the total economic and insured losses will rise considerably once the damage has been evaluated.
During 1999, there were numerous spectacular large catastrophes -- earthquakes in Colombia, Turkey, Greece, and Taiwan; avalanches in the Alps; floods in central Europe and south-eastern Asia; tornadoes in Oklahoma and the hailstorm in Sydney, Australia; hurricanes and cyclones in the United States, Japan, and India.
Hurricanes accounted for 70 percent of insured damage, followed by earthquakes at 18 percent, floods at 6 percent, and other disasters including forest fires and volcano eruptions at 6 percent, it said.
Munich Re said the frequency of natural disasters has been increasing in the 1990s and that its research team sees "no chance of a decrease . . . for the foreseeable time." It said the rapidly growing world population, urbanization and climate change were partly to blame for the increase in disasters, and also pointed out the vulnerability of modern industrial society to catastrophes. Munich Re's Geoscience Research Group (comprising 14 scientists under its head, Dr. Gerhard Berz) has been recording catastrophe data on a global scale for more than 25 years.
Credit: Journal of Commerce Staff

Is our weather getting worse, or does it just seem that way?: [Final Edition]

Author: Bueckert, Dennis

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/is-our-weather-getting-worse-does-just-seem-that/docview/345809882/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to? Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995. Photo: CP archives photo / A tornado touches down in Edmonton in this July 31, 1987 photo, causing over a quarter of a billion dollars damage and killing 27 people. ; Photo: CP Photo / Pedestrians make their way past downed trees as an ice storm ripped through Montreal in this Jan. 6, 1998 photo. The ice storm left parts of Quebec and Ontario without electricity for days as ice- covered trees crashed down on power lines. ;

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Is+our+weather+getting+worse%2C+or+does+it+just+seem+that+way%3F&amp;author=Bueckert%2C+Dennis&amp;issn=08373744&amp;title=Expositor&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-01&amp;spage=MA.19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: TORONTO - Gordon Toole remembers the day it was so cold that spit froze before it hit the ground.
Toole was one of about 20 men working at a military airport at Snag, Yukon, when the temperature plunged to the deepest low ever recorded in North America.
"You can't breathe through your nostrils in air that temperature, it's almost as though they're blocked or frozen," he says from Snag, about 320 kilometres northwest of Yellowknife. "You have to breath through your mouth but they're shallow breaths.
"You cannot take deep breaths because they just catch in your throat, you're gasping."
Toole recalls the crystal clarity of sounds. He could distinctly hear the barking of dogs in a village some five kilometres away. An airplane overhead was deafening.
FROZE IN THE AIR
As an experiment, the men flipped a saucer of water into the air at waist height: it landed on the ground in hard pellets.
The temperature was so low it couldn't be recorded on a mercury thermometer. Even in an alcohol thermometer, the liquid sank below the lowest temperature mark, settling into the bulb at the bottom.
They marked the point with a small file -- a pen wouldn't work -- and sent the thermometer to Toronto. Days later the reading was confirmed: --62.2C.
The record set at Snag on Feb. 3, 1947, still stands. For Canadians who seemingly never get tired of talking about the weather, it's a classic tale, especially since there were no casualties and no real damage.
Recent years have seen many weather extremes that were not so benign.
Few residents of Eastern Canada have forgotten the ice storm in 1998, when millions shivered in darkened houses or sought refuge in crowded public shelters.
TOWNS SUBMERGED
Few residents of Manitoba have forgotten the flood of 1997, when the Red River turned into a vast sea, driving some 28,000 people from their homes and submerging whole towns.
The Saguenay Flood of 1996 caused massive damage, as did the Calgary hail storm of 1997. Unpredictable Prairie weather has helped reduce Canada's breadbasket to a basket case as the year 2000 nears.
The Insurance Bureau of Canada estimates that insurance payments for extreme weather events across the country have been doubling every five to 10 years. Payouts last year reached $1.5 billion.
"The weather in Canada has been frightful," says Paul Kovaks, a spokesman for the bureau.
Munich Re of Munich, Germany, one of the world's biggest reinsurance companies, estimates global losses from weather-related natural disasters have increased to $90 billion annually from $7 billion to $8 billion annually in the 1980s.
Part of the rising toll results from population growth -- there's simply more property to be damaged, and more of it is built in risky areas like flood plains and coastlines.
Paradoxically, modern technology can also increase vulnerability to extreme weather since vital energy and communications systems across large areas are intertwined.
"The disaster is the event plus our vulnerability," says federal climatologist Roger Street. "Higher dependence on networked energy, higher dependence on telecommunications, makes us more vulnerable."
Climatologists would like to come up with an extreme-weather index so they could measure the apparent rise in instability. One of the problems is they can't agree on how to define an extreme weather event.
The great ice storm, for example, did not involve record-setting precipitation. It resulted from multiple factors, none of which would in itself be ranked as highly unusual; it was the combination that was unusual.
The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area.
Climatologist David Phillips says in his recent book Blame it on the Weather that El Nino has become the scapegoat for everything from whale beachings in China to increased crime in Saskatoon.
EL NINO GETTING STRONGER
But using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to?
Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.
Is El Nino a messenger for something much bigger?
Few credible scientists dispute that the climate is warming: the finding has been confirmed by countless studies. There has been great debate about whether the trend is due to natural variability or human influence. Now that debate also seems to be settled.
Over the past century the average temperature of earth has risen by close to one degree Celsius. Now the big controversy concerns how fast future warming will occur and how it will affect different regions.
COMPUTER SIMULATION
Much of the research is done with computer models simulating the atmosphere -- programs so complex that they push existing hardware to its limits. The models have many warts but they're getting better.
"There's some uncertainty, but I feel much safer, much more confident making a weather forecast for the year 2090 than I do an economic or a demographic or a political kind of forecast," says Phillips of the federal Environment Department's atmospheric environment service.
For example, the models show a balmy future for Toronto: "What Kentucky has now is what in fact Toronto will inherit in terms of its climate probably a hundred years from now.
"We've warmed up Toronto by maybe 0.6 of a degree and now we're talking about warming in the summertime of four to five degrees, five to nine degrees in the winter. There's going to be really a leap forward in terms of the climate."
Canadians may welcome the prospect of milder winters, but what about the prospect of more frequent and severe droughts, floods and storms?
"It is extreme events that will be the biggest impact on Canadians from climate change," says Gordon McBean, assistant deputy to the federal environment minister.
At the 1997 Kyoto climate conference, Canada pledged to reduce its greenhouse emissions by six per cent from 1990 levels by 2008- 2012. But since then, emissions have continued to rise, and there is still no plan for reversing the trend.
REQUIRES CHANGES
Cutting emissions would require major changes in the way Canadians use energy, and would have an impact on virtually every industry. Disruptions in the near term might be high.
"The cost of action to reduce the risk is today, and the cost of inaction is in the future and hence there's a tendency for discounting the costs of inaction," says Henry Hengeveld, a senior adviser on climate change in the Environment Department.
The fossil fuel industry and the Alberta government have denounced the Kyoto commitment as unrealistic, and skeptics continue to find platforms for their views.
Hengeveld, for one, is not discouraged. He expects that the weather itself will settle the quarrel.
"If we are right in our science, and I think we are, nature will remind us from time to time. One of the things that got this on the agenda in 1988 was the extremely hot, dry summer we had that year. I think this is going to happen from time to time again."
Illustration Photo: CP archives photo / A tornado touches down in Edmonton in this July 31, 1987 photo, causing over a quarter of a billion dollars damage and killing 27 people. ; Photo: CP Photo / Pedestrians make their way past downed trees as an ice storm ripped through Montreal in this Jan. 6, 1998 photo. The ice storm left parts of Quebec and Ontario without electricity for days as ice- covered trees crashed down on power lines. ;

'70s brings change and growth: [Final Edition]

Author: Porter, Thomas

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/70s-brings-change-growth/docview/344980033/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The 1970s were a period of great growth for the city of Prince Albert said local retailer Herschel Davidner. In his recollection, the city's business community evolved with the arrival of new people. Aside from changes to downtown, [Davidner] said Prince Albert experienced a period of significant growth during the seventies, after seeing the development of the pulp mill and the burning of the last bonded debt in the '60s. "The pulp mill had a huge impact on the city of Prince Albert," said Davidner.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%2770s+brings+change+and+growth&amp;author=Porter%2C+Thomas&amp;issn=08395381&amp;title=Prince+Albert+Daily+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-01&amp;spage=29&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Approaching 2000
The 1970s were a period of great growth for the city of Prince Albert said local retailer Herschel Davidner. In his recollection, the city's business community evolved with the arrival of new people.
"The downtown used to be a vibrant, thriving retail centre. There were no vacancies," said Davidner. "When the South Hill Mall was built in '72, the retail space in the city was effectively doubled."
It was the beginning of change in the retail sector, said Davidner, one that spelled the end for the downtown shopper.
"When they were talking about building the thing (the mall), I asked myself `Do they expect to have twice the sales with twice the space?"' said Davidner.
Many businesses took to the mall, leaving empty storefronts in the downtown core. Despite the changing face of the city's center, Davidner's Clothing and Western Wear enjoyed favourable sales throughout the '70s.
"We noticed lower sales totals than the previous year for about a year after the mall came in," said Davidner. "Sales gradually returned allowing us to expand our location at the time."
Some retailers didn't weather the climate change as well, said Davidner, with some moving into the new mall and others disappearing all together.
"We used to have three shoe stores on Central Avenue and three high quality menswear retailers," said Davidner. "You can see what's happened to the downtown area. Things have changed and efforts to revitalize have had limited success."
"You didn't see lawyers and insurance agencies on the ground floor," said Davidner. "Second Avenue has become the main drag now and downtown locations that were once prime business locations are going for less money."
Aside from changes to downtown, Davidner said Prince Albert experienced a period of significant growth during the seventies, after seeing the development of the pulp mill and the burning of the last bonded debt in the '60s.
"The pulp mill had a huge impact on the city of Prince Albert," said Davidner.
"The mill poured money into the development of this city and provided jobs for countless people. They have been, and continue to be, a great community member."
With an increase in population brought on by new jobs and new money, existing buildings became crowded and translated into a need for a additional space.
Carlton Comprehensive High School was built onto the Technical High School on Sixth Avenue East in the mid-1970s, said Bill Smiley of the Prince Albert Historical Society, along with other familiar landmarks of today.
"A second police station was built where the Northcote Manor stands in 1974," said Smiley. "The new fire hall was also built on 15th Street East."
The J.M. Cuelenaere Public Library took shape in 1972 shortly after the construction of the Comuniplex and new subdivisions like Crescent Heights, said Smiley.
"There was a lot of new development in the 1970s," said Smiley. "The city was growing rapidly and needed new facilities like schools, high rises and the mall."
Former Herald managing editor Wayne Roznowsky, who worked in the sports department at the time, recalls the decade as devoid of economic worry; a time when employment was plentiful.
"If you didn't like the job you had, you just went and found another one," said Roznowsky. "It was a time of great optimism, you didn't need to worry about what you did or didn't get out of university."
He recounted the opening of the mammoth Carlton Comprehensive as the day the high school dynamic changed for good.
"It (Carlton) pretty much absorbed four other schools and brought the total from five to two; St. Mary and Carlton," he said.
Illustration Photo: Herald file photos / BOOM TOWN -- The 1970s will be remebered by many Prince Albert residents as a time of great growth and change. With low interest rates and a growing population, construction and development was on the rise. With a new mall, schools and housing erupting from the ground, the city skyline would be forever changed. ;

Leading Article: Dawn of a new millennium We have the power. Do we have the wisdom?: [1]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leading-article-dawn-new-millennium-we-have-power/docview/245471424/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: There have been billions of dawns since earth was first formed. But, according to the calendar most of us share, with today's dawn the human race enters the third millennium. We may feel awe and humility, and even surprise. For more than two generations we have survived our power to destroy all life while slowly engineering the planet's asphyxiation. Somehow we have made it. The sceptics will say that today's date is only a number; the pedants insist it is not even the right one. But for everyone else, just as the dawn marks the start of a new day, and January 1 announces the start of a new year, so today's dawn opens a new chapter in the human story. We do not wake this morning to a blank slate: there is something deathly in that idea. We wake instead aware of the long past we leave behind. But the turn of the millennium requires us to pause a moment, turn around, and ready ourselves for the future. What will it be? Can we possibly imagine it? The perspective of the lens in space is denied us. The century just gone is too close to disentangle its miracles from its horrors. Even the still beauty of the image is a lie because its gleaming perfection masks the potential for catastrophe. But a millennium offers renewal. `To make an end is to make a beginning," wrote Eliot. `The end is where we start from." Still, there is a question that has to be solved first, one that underpins all the others. If, uniquely among all the creatures on earth, our evolution is in our own hands - thanks to our understanding of our genes and our mastery of our physical environment - what will we do with it? How will we ensure it is just? And this, tough as it may seem, is not a question we can leave to the experts. This is one for all of us. For there is a risk here that, as science accelerates ever faster, our human institutions fail to catch up. We have the brainpower to see how our lives could be improved beyond our dreams, to be rid of much pain and hardship, yet we struggle to spread these new riches fairly. So scientists develop a treatment for Aids which gives those in America or western Europe once written off as the living dead a new chance of life, and yet Aids still kills millions in Africa. This is not because our scientific creativity hits some conundrum it cannot solve in Africa: it is because one part of the planet is so much poorer than the rest. We could be about to repeat the mistake with genetic medicine. The rich countries of the north may discover how to abolish a raft of hereditary diseases forever, but in the south (or among the poor anywhere) those genes could continue to kill. If the new millennium sees us eating from the tree of knowledge, we need to work out how we can share out the fruit. The anxiety we may feel about such things might just be alleviated if the west, which has never been richer or economically more self- confident, began to construct a proper programme of third world aid, as well as taking real action on the vehicle and factory emissions, and the pollution of the seas. If we can decode DNA and invent the internet, we should be able to do pretty much do anything. The experience of the past decade in producing global answers to global problems has not been encouraging. Last month's fiasco in Seattle suggested a fatal dislocation between the ambitions of the developed world and the needs of the developing world. Europe has been at peace for more than half a century, but the struggle to establish successful pan-national governmental structures shows the difficulty of reconciling international aspirations with local cultures. The UN security council has so far been unequal to the task of dealing with the problems thrown up after the collapse of the old communist bloc and the consequent erosions of the 19th and 20th century concepts of state sovereignty. The wars in Kosovo, Bosnia, Rwanda and Chechnya all illustrated the international community's inability to intervene quickly and decisively.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leading+Article%3A+Dawn+of+a+new+millennium+We+have+the+power.+Do+we+have+the+wisdom%3F&amp;author=&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-01&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: There have been billions of dawns since earth was first formed. But, according to the calendar most of us share, with today's dawn the human race enters the third millennium. We may feel awe and humility, and even surprise. For more than two generations we have survived our power to destroy all life while slowly engineering the planet's asphyxiation. Somehow we have made it. The sceptics will say that today's date is only a number; the pedants insist it is not even the right one. But for everyone else, just as the dawn marks the start of a new day, and January 1 announces the start of a new year, so today's dawn opens a new chapter in the human story. We do not wake this morning to a blank slate: there is something deathly in that idea. We wake instead aware of the long past we leave behind. But the turn of the millennium requires us to pause a moment, turn around, and ready ourselves for the future. What will it be? Can we possibly imagine it? The perspective of the lens in space is denied us. The century just gone is too close to disentangle its miracles from its horrors. Even the still beauty of the image is a lie because its gleaming perfection masks the potential for catastrophe. But a millennium offers renewal. `To make an end is to make a beginning," wrote Eliot. `The end is where we start from."
Attempting to predict the coming century is more than usually foolish. Even 10 years ago we could not have imagined the internet, the speed of computing (currently doubling every eight or nine months), genetic mapping or cloning. While the mystery of consciousness may elude us for many decades, there are other large areas of our make-up which are about to be revealed. Last month scientists announced the decoding of the first of 23 human chromosomes. It is, as yet, only a rough sketch, but they will be reading the entire genome soon enough, decoding every human characteristic, working out the genes or combinations of genes which are responsible for musicality, schizophrenia, left- handedeness, height, athletic prowess and aggression.
It feels uncannily as though human beings are finally getting to the roots of the tree of knowledge. No wonder today's scientists feel they are living at the most exciting time in history. While Charles Darwin discovered the probability that we evolved by natural selection, we did not understand the mechanics of that change. Soon we will. We are learning from this string of genetic information what life was for our predecessors, how it works for us - and even catch a glimpse of what we might become. In short, we will read the story life is telling us.
This new knowledge will bring untold medical benefits and pose awesome ethical dilemmas. It may be that within two decades traditional surgery will largely be replaced by gene therapy - the injection of chemical messages into diseased or malfunctioning parts of the body. The predisposition to life-threatening diseases in certain patients will also enable doctors to anticipate illness in their patients. The same technology may enable the rich to give their children enormous advantages - enhanced athleticism, height, mathematical ability, the absence of asthma. A scrap of DNA will tell any authority anything they want to know about any person. Who is to determine how this knowledge is used?
It is easy to dwell on our fears. The geniuses of genetics have opened Pandora's box. But people forget the end of the story of Pandora. At the bottom of the box was hope, waiting to break out. Our new ability to eat from the tree of life need not trigger a fall from grace. Take, for example, the fears that the science of genetics would revive the science of eugenics and give intellectual underpinning to crude racism. In fact, advanced genetics teaches us not about the biological origins of our differences, but how strikingly similar we all are to each other. Two groups of chimps found 20 miles apart differ more from each other than one human being does from any other, even those who resemble each other least: our brains are remarkably similar. As we open the book of life during the coming millennium, we should see how much we all share - and how bogus are any notions of racial superiority left over from the last century.
Nor should we blindly fear the prospect of doctors toying with our genes. We need to keep a vigilant eye on what they do, of course, but we can also afford to look forward to a world where diseases such as muscular dystrophy or Huntington's chorea can be tweezed out before they have ruined a life. Would the world really be worse off if those plagues were banished? And if genetics enables human beings to stay alive for longer, and life becomes less of a risk - with less need for the insurance policy of multiple children relied on in the developing world - then perhaps world population might start to fall, easing some of the strain on the planet.
The implications are so vast that we can barely sketch their outline. Where does God, already in retreat after 600 years of modern science, sit in a world where we feel we may one day know the innermost secrets of human existence? Will ours become an entirely godless world? The scientists do not seem to think so. Indeed, some of the most imaginative thinkers speculate that we could eventually discover the concept of right and wrong alongside our genetic instruction manual, that we will realise that the notion of good and evil exists in the universe, as real as the concept of a number: both are real, independent of our perception of them.
Cutting-edge studies of physics - of light, energy and matter - can also feed, rather than choke, our mystical impulses. Now that we can describe the entire physical history of the cosmos for all but the first thousandth of a second, scientists have concluded that, in the words of one, `it looked as if the universe knew we were coming'. Somehow it seemed ready to welcome life. The result is that religion may be different in the new millennium, it may have to speak in a different language, but it will not be dead.
The possibilities are thrilling. Space exploration will continue, reaching beyond our solar system to discover . . . who knows what? Once we have cracked the code for life, will we be able to construct a machine that mirrors it perfectly? Will we crack the riddle of consciousness, so that computers start resembling brains - or will we discover what exactly it is that makes us so different from them? These are just some of the dilemmas that await us.
Still, there is a question that has to be solved first, one that underpins all the others. If, uniquely among all the creatures on earth, our evolution is in our own hands - thanks to our understanding of our genes and our mastery of our physical environment - what will we do with it? How will we ensure it is just? And this, tough as it may seem, is not a question we can leave to the experts. This is one for all of us. For there is a risk here that, as science accelerates ever faster, our human institutions fail to catch up. We have the brainpower to see how our lives could be improved beyond our dreams, to be rid of much pain and hardship, yet we struggle to spread these new riches fairly. So scientists develop a treatment for Aids which gives those in America or western Europe once written off as the living dead a new chance of life, and yet Aids still kills millions in Africa. This is not because our scientific creativity hits some conundrum it cannot solve in Africa: it is because one part of the planet is so much poorer than the rest. We could be about to repeat the mistake with genetic medicine. The rich countries of the north may discover how to abolish a raft of hereditary diseases forever, but in the south (or among the poor anywhere) those genes could continue to kill. If the new millennium sees us eating from the tree of knowledge, we need to work out how we can share out the fruit.
Our starting point is not easy. We live in a world where economic wealth has grown in ways few ever imagined possible - but where it has become concentrated in fewer and fewer hands. Rich countries such as the US are getting fantastically richer, but the poor ones are not just shut out of the new wealth party: they are getting poorer. Life expectancy, so long assumed to be ever- increasing as man and medicine become more sophisticated, is actually falling in the planet's poorest countries. Genetic research may be discovering the secret of making clever babies in London and New York, but in Mali and Malawi children are going blind for lack of basic eye-cream. If science has put humankind's destiny in our own hands, we are getting it horribly wrong.
In the past 50 years we have been so concerned with the individual rights, with self-expression and personal liberation that we have quite forgotten what we are doing to the planet in this binge of self- gratification. Capitalism becomes more global; so does the threat to the environment. What were once local problems of pollution have now merged into a huge general threat to the planet's delicately balanced ecosystem. The oceans rise along with the steady increase in global temperatures; the atmosphere is more polluted than at any stage in human history; and hundreds of plant and animal species are becoming extinct. Our need for fuel, food and living space increases every day and yet we do not have the slightest inkling of how we are going to cope as the world's population leaps from 6bn today to 10-12bn through the next century
From the industrial revolution until a projected date of 2050, we will have obliterated half the species on earth. Whether it is tiny plants, fungi or insects, we are destroying about 27,000 separate creations a year. It is a programme of devastation unlike anything the globe has witnessed before. Climate change is making the problem worse, with arid areas getting more arid and wet ones getting wetter. Land currently used for farming will become unviable, either too dry or with vital top soil washed away. Some geographers predict a kind of runaway meltdown, as global warming becomes a self-fuelling process, galloping ahead beyond our control. The melting of the glaciers will not solve our other looming problem: a world shortage of water, which could see whole swaths of the Middle East and Asia running dry within 40 years, perhaps warring over H20 the way they once battled for oil.
What can we do about this glaring paradox, which has led us to understand ourselves so profoundly and yet still organise our world so badly? Science can offer some help. It may be that a nudge in the direction of genetic modification will eventually be required to avert a food crisis, compensating for the once fertile areas ruined by global warming. Nanotechnology, the use of tiny robots to perform miniature tasks like microsurgery, could also help: the world will consume fewer resources the more adept we become at manipulating matter. The real answer, however, may be globalisation, since even the most highly developed nations seem incapable of addressing the destruction that is going on. Not the globalisation of the world economy, which currently serves to widen the gap between rich and poor, but a new, as yet unformed idea of globalised politics.
HG Wells saw the point a century ago: he realised that modern problems are worldwide, requiring worldwide solutions. Only a kind of politics which weighs up the needs of the entire human race could begin to address the water question, for example, insisting that the water-rich countries use no more than they need, while the water- poor get no less than they need. At the moment we do nothing because the problem seems too large or the cause too obstinate. This is because the growth of the most developed economies depends on the pillaging of natural resources. It is a global problem which will eventually have to be sorted out by powerful global institutions, rather than national governments. This will be the only way to prevent the Brazilians from burning their rainforest, the Americans from polluting the atmosphere with their factories and the British from dumping nuclear waste in the oceans. Each nation bears a responsiblity for the terrible state of the world's environment at the end of the 20th century, but it is way beyond the powers and will of individual nations to right the damage.
The anxiety we may feel about such things might just be alleviated if the west, which has never been richer or economically more self- confident, began to construct a proper programme of third world aid, as well as taking real action on the vehicle and factory emissions, and the pollution of the seas. If we can decode DNA and invent the internet, we should be able to do pretty much do anything. The experience of the past decade in producing global answers to global problems has not been encouraging. Last month's fiasco in Seattle suggested a fatal dislocation between the ambitions of the developed world and the needs of the developing world. Europe has been at peace for more than half a century, but the struggle to establish successful pan-national governmental structures shows the difficulty of reconciling international aspirations with local cultures. The UN security council has so far been unequal to the task of dealing with the problems thrown up after the collapse of the old communist bloc and the consequent erosions of the 19th and 20th century concepts of state sovereignty. The wars in Kosovo, Bosnia, Rwanda and Chechnya all illustrated the international community's inability to intervene quickly and decisively.
But pessimism should be tempered. The emergence of global consumer power, thanks to the internet, could bring a company like Monsanto to its knees. The web is creating new political communities, scattered across the globe, but working together. To hint that all this might one day lead to a global government, democratically elected, sounds laughable now. If anyone had suggested votes for the masses at the beginning of the 19th century they would have been ridiculed, too. But it happened.
We need to pull off a shift as profound as that modern move to democracy, this time at the global level. And yet we must still remain mindful of the local. A challenge for the new century is to keep alive the belief that individuals can shape the world around them, whether it is the planet or their village. The new technology can be a friend or foe in this process - daunting us with information overload or enabling us to reach out and connect with others. We need to make sure the internet and its allied innovations work for us, not against us.
Where might our own, small island fit into all this? The answer could be quite well. Our mother tongue puts us at the forefront of the web, whose first language is English. We are also neatly situated between the old and new worlds, perfectly placed to be the pivot between Europe and America. In the effort to give birth to a new global awareness, we could be in just the right position. We will need to resolve our dilemma over our relationship with the continent once and for all, and we should do that sooner rather than later. That, in turn, may require a new understanding of Britain itself. In the imperial age it was clear what the union of England, Scotland, Wales and later northern Ireland was for. At the start of the new century, we are much less certain. But we need to work out the purpose of the union if we are not to watch it unravel in the coming decades. Until we do, it is hard to see how we can enter into any other arrangement, with Europe or others, with any self-confidence.
So vast changes are ahead of us, perhaps an unending cascade of them. A new relationship between women and men began to emerge in the past century, at least in the developed world. That process of struggle and debate will continue into the new era. But we should embrace it all. For as the scientists will keep telling us, change is in our nature. We are evolutionary creatures, granted a unique ability to ask questions about ourselves. Indeed, it is this very pursuit of understanding which distinguishes us from the animal. We should cherish this fact about ourselves, just as we cherish the intricate beauty of our existence. To quote Eliot once more: `Last year's words belong to last year's language/And next year's words await another voice.' We are blessed to live now: let us make this a blessed century and a blessed new millennium.

Leading Article: Dawn of a new millennium We have the power. Do we have the wisdom?

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leading-article-dawn-new-millennium-we-have-power/docview/245448349/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: There have been billions of dawns since earth was first formed. But, according to the calendar most of us share, with today's dawn the human race enters the third millennium. We may feel awe and humility, and even surprise. For more than two generations we have survived our power to destroy all life while slowly engineering the planet's asphyxiation. Somehow we have made it. The sceptics will say that today's date is only a number; the pedants insist it is not even the right one. But for everyone else, just as the dawn marks the start of a new day, and January 1 announces the start of a new year, so today's dawn opens a new chapter in the human story. We do not wake this morning to a blank slate: there is something deathly in that idea. We wake instead aware of the long past we leave behind. But the turn of the millennium requires us to pause a moment, turn around, and ready ourselves for the future. What will it be? Can we possibly imagine it? The perspective of the lens in space is denied us. The century just gone is too close to disentangle its miracles from its horrors. Even the still beauty of the image is a lie because its gleaming perfection masks the potential for catastrophe. But a millennium offers renewal. `To make an end is to make a beginning," wrote Eliot. `The end is where we start from." Attempting to predict the coming century is more than usually foolish. Even 10 years ago we could not have imagined the internet, the speed of computing (currently doubling every eight or nine months), genetic mapping or cloning.
