As we open the book of life during the coming millennium, we should see how much we all share - and how bogus are any notions of racial superiority left over from the last century.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leading+Article%3A+Dawn+of+a+new+millennium+We+have+the+power.+Do+we+have+the+wisdom%3F&amp;author=&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=2000-01-01&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: There have been billions of dawns since earth was first formed. But, according to the calendar most of us share, with today's dawn the human race enters the third millennium. We may feel awe and humility, and even surprise. For more than two generations we have survived our power to destroy all life while slowly engineering the planet's asphyxiation. Somehow we have made it. The sceptics will say that today's date is only a number; the pedants insist it is not even the right one. But for everyone else, just as the dawn marks the start of a new day, and January 1 announces the start of a new year, so today's dawn opens a new chapter in the human story. We do not wake this morning to a blank slate: there is something deathly in that idea. We wake instead aware of the long past we leave behind. But the turn of the millennium requires us to pause a moment, turn around, and ready ourselves for the future. What will it be? Can we possibly imagine it? The perspective of the lens in space is denied us. The century just gone is too close to disentangle its miracles from its horrors. Even the still beauty of the image is a lie because its gleaming perfection masks the potential for catastrophe. But a millennium offers renewal. `To make an end is to make a beginning," wrote Eliot. `The end is where we start from."
Attempting to predict the coming century is more than usually foolish. Even 10 years ago we could not have imagined the internet, the speed of computing (currently doubling every eight or nine months), genetic mapping or cloning. While the mystery of consciousness may elude us for many decades, there are other large areas of our make-up which are about to be revealed. Last month scientists announced the decoding of the first of 23 human chromosomes. It is, as yet, only a rough sketch, but they will be reading the entire genome soon enough, decoding every human characteristic, working out the genes or combinations of genes which are responsible for musicality, schizophrenia, left- handedeness, height, athletic prowess and aggression.
It feels uncannily as though human beings are finally getting to the roots of the tree of knowledge. No wonder today's scientists feel they are living at the most exciting time in history. While Charles Darwin discovered the probability that we evolved by natural selection, we did not understand the mechanics of that change. Soon we will. We are learning from this string of genetic information what life was for our predecessors, how it works for us - and even catch a glimpse of what we might become. In short, we will read the story life is telling us.
This new knowledge will bring untold medical benefits and pose awesome ethical dilemmas. It may be that within two decades traditional surgery will largely be replaced by gene therapy - the injection of chemical messages into diseased or malfunctioning parts of the body. The predisposition to life-threatening diseases in certain patients will also enable doctors to anticipate illness in their patients. The same technology may enable the rich to give their children enormous advantages - enhanced athleticism, height, mathematical ability, the absence of asthma. A scrap of DNA will tell any authority anything they want to know about any person. Who is to determine how this knowledge is used?
It is easy to dwell on our fears. The geniuses of genetics have opened Pandora's box. But people forget the end of the story of Pandora. At the bottom of the box was hope, waiting to break out. Our new ability to eat from the tree of life need not trigger a fall from grace. Take, for example, the fears that the science of genetics would revive the science of eugenics and give intellectual underpinning to crude racism. In fact, advanced genetics teaches us not about the biological origins of our differences, but how strikingly similar we all are to each other. Two groups of chimps found 20 miles apart differ more from each other than one human being does from any other, even those who resemble each other least: our brains are remarkably similar. As we open the book of life during the coming millennium, we should see how much we all share - and how bogus are any notions of racial superiority left over from the last century.
Nor should we blindly fear the prospect of doctors toying with our genes. We need to keep a vigilant eye on what they do, of course, but we can also afford to look forward to a world where diseases such as muscular dystrophy or Huntington's chorea can be tweezed out before they have ruined a life. Would the world really be worse off if those plagues were banished? And if genetics enables human beings to stay alive for longer, and life becomes less of a risk - with less need for the insurance policy of multiple children relied on in the developing world - then perhaps world population might start to fall, easing some of the strain on the planet.
The implications are so vast that we can barely sketch their outline. Where does God, already in retreat after 600 years of modern science, sit in a world where we feel we may one day know the innermost secrets of human existence? Will ours become an entirely godless world? The scientists do not seem to think so. Indeed, some of the most imaginative thinkers speculate that we could eventually discover the concept of right and wrong alongside our genetic instruction manual, that we will realise that the notion of good and evil exists in the universe, as real as the concept of a number: both are real, independent of our perception of them.
Cutting-edge studies of physics - of light, energy and matter - can also feed, rather than choke, our mystical impulses. Now that we can describe the entire physical history of the cosmos for all but the first thousandth of a second, scientists have concluded that, in the words of one, `it looked as if the universe knew we were coming'. Somehow it seemed ready to welcome life. The result is that religion may be different in the new millennium, it may have to speak in a different language, but it will not be dead.
The possibilities are thrilling. Space exploration will continue, reaching beyond our solar system to discover . . . who knows what? Once we have cracked the code for life, will we be able to construct a machine that mirrors it perfectly? Will we crack the riddle of consciousness, so that computers start resembling brains - or will we discover what exactly it is that makes us so different from them? These are just some of the dilemmas that await us.
Still, there is a question that has to be solved first, one that underpins all the others. If, uniquely among all the creatures on earth, our evolution is in our own hands - thanks to our understanding of our genes and our mastery of our physical environment - what will we do with it? How will we ensure it is just? And this, tough as it may seem, is not a question we can leave to the experts. This is one for all of us. For there is a risk here that, as science accelerates ever faster, our human institutions fail to catch up. We have the brainpower to see how our lives could be improved beyond our dreams, to be rid of much pain and hardship, yet we struggle to spread these new riches fairly. So scientists develop a treatment for Aids which gives those in America or western Europe once written off as the living dead a new chance of life, and yet Aids still kills millions in Africa. This is not because our scientific creativity hits some conundrum it cannot solve in Africa: it is because one part of the planet is so much poorer than the rest. We could be about to repeat the mistake with genetic medicine. The rich countries of the north may discover how to abolish a raft of hereditary diseases forever, but in the south (or among the poor anywhere) those genes could continue to kill. If the new millennium sees us eating from the tree of knowledge, we need to work out how we can share out the fruit.
Our starting point is not easy. We live in a world where economic wealth has grown in ways few ever imagined possible - but where it has become concentrated in fewer and fewer hands. Rich countries such as the US are getting fantastically richer, but the poor ones are not just shut out of the new wealth party: they are getting poorer. Life expectancy, so long assumed to be ever- increasing as man and medicine become more sophisticated, is actually falling in the planet's poorest countries. Genetic research may be discovering the secret of making clever babies in London and New York, but in Mali and Malawi children are going blind for lack of basic eye-cream. If science has put humankind's destiny in our own hands, we are getting it horribly wrong.
In the past 50 years we have been so concerned with the individual rights, with self-expression and personal liberation that we have quite forgotten what we are doing to the planet in this binge of self- gratification. Capitalism becomes more global; so does the threat to the environment. What were once local problems of pollution have now merged into a huge general threat to the planet's delicately balanced ecosystem. The oceans rise along with the steady increase in global temperatures; the atmosphere is more polluted than at any stage in human history; and hundreds of plant and animal species are becoming extinct. Our need for fuel, food and living space increases every day and yet we do not have the slightest inkling of how we are going to cope as the world's population leaps from 6bn today to 10-12bn through the next century
From the industrial revolution until a projected date of 2050, we will have obliterated half the species on earth. Whether it is tiny plants, fungi or insects, we are destroying about 27,000 separate creations a year. It is a programme of devastation unlike anything the globe has witnessed before. Climate change is making the problem worse, with arid areas getting more arid and wet ones getting wetter. Land currently used for farming will become unviable, either too dry or with vital top soil washed away. Some geographers predict a kind of runaway meltdown, as global warming becomes a self-fuelling process, galloping ahead beyond our control. The melting of the glaciers will not solve our other looming problem: a world shortage of water, which could see whole swaths of the Middle East and Asia running dry within 40 years, perhaps warring over H20 the way they once battled for oil.
What can we do about this glaring paradox, which has led us to understand ourselves so profoundly and yet still organise our world so badly? Science can offer some help. It may be that a nudge in the direction of genetic modification will eventually be required to avert a food crisis, compensating for the once fertile areas ruined by global warming. Nanotechnology, the use of tiny robots to perform miniature tasks like microsurgery, could also help: the world will consume fewer resources the more adept we become at manipulating matter. The real answer, however, may be globalisation, since even the most highly developed nations seem incapable of addressing the destruction that is going on. Not the globalisation of the world economy, which currently serves to widen the gap between rich and poor, but a new, as yet unformed idea of globalised politics.
HG Wells saw the point a century ago: he realised that modern problems are worldwide, requiring worldwide solutions. Only a kind of politics which weighs up the needs of the entire human race could begin to address the water question, for example, insisting that the water-rich countries use no more than they need, while the water- poor get no less than they need. At the moment we do nothing because the problem seems too large or the cause too obstinate. This is because the growth of the most developed economies depends on the pillaging of natural resources. It is a global problem which will eventually have to be sorted out by powerful global institutions, rather than national governments. This will be the only way to prevent the Brazilians from burning their rainforest, the Americans from polluting the atmosphere with their factories and the British from dumping nuclear waste in the oceans. Each nation bears a responsiblity for the terrible state of the world's environment at the end of the 20th century, but it is way beyond the powers and will of individual nations to right the damage.
The anxiety we may feel about such things might just be alleviated if the west, which has never been richer or economically more self- confident, began to construct a proper programme of third world aid, as well as taking real action on the vehicle and factory emissions, and the pollution of the seas. If we can decode DNA and invent the internet, we should be able to do pretty much do anything. The experience of the past decade in producing global answers to global problems has not been encouraging. Last month's fiasco in Seattle suggested a fatal dislocation between the ambitions of the developed world and the needs of the developing world. Europe has been at peace for more than half a century, but the struggle to establish successful pan-national governmental structures shows the difficulty of reconciling international aspirations with local cultures. The UN security council has so far been unequal to the task of dealing with the problems thrown up after the collapse of the old communist bloc and the consequent erosions of the 19th and 20th century concepts of state sovereignty. The wars in Kosovo, Bosnia, Rwanda and Chechnya all illustrated the international community's inability to intervene quickly and decisively.
But pessimism should be tempered. The emergence of global consumer power, thanks to the internet, could bring a company like Monsanto to its knees. The web is creating new political communities, scattered across the globe, but working together. To hint that all this might one day lead to a global government, democratically elected, sounds laughable now. If anyone had suggested votes for the masses at the beginning of the 19th century they would have been ridiculed, too. But it happened.
We need to pull off a shift as profound as that modern move to democracy, this time at the global level. And yet we must still remain mindful of the local. A challenge for the new century is to keep alive the belief that individuals can shape the world around them, whether it is the planet or their village. The new technology can be a friend or foe in this process - daunting us with information overload or enabling us to reach out and connect with others. We need to make sure the internet and its allied innovations work for us, not against us.
Where might our own, small island fit into all this? The answer could be quite well. Our mother tongue puts us at the forefront of the web, whose first language is English. We are also neatly situated between the old and new worlds, perfectly placed to be the pivot between Europe and America. In the effort to give birth to a new global awareness, we could be in just the right position. We will need to resolve our dilemma over our relationship with the continent once and for all, and we should do that sooner rather than later. That, in turn, may require a new understanding of Britain itself. In the imperial age it was clear what the union of England, Scotland, Wales and later northern Ireland was for. At the start of the new century, we are much less certain. But we need to work out the purpose of the union if we are not to watch it unravel in the coming decades. Until we do, it is hard to see how we can enter into any other arrangement, with Europe or others, with any self-confidence.
So vast changes are ahead of us, perhaps an unending cascade of them. A new relationship between women and men began to emerge in the past century, at least in the developed world. That process of struggle and debate will continue into the new era. But we should embrace it all. For as the scientists will keep telling us, change is in our nature. We are evolutionary creatures, granted a unique ability to ask questions about ourselves. Indeed, it is this very pursuit of understanding which distinguishes us from the animal. We should cherish this fact about ourselves, just as we cherish the intricate beauty of our existence. To quote Eliot once more: `Last year's words belong to last year's language/And next year's words await another voice.' We are blessed to live now: let us make this a blessed century and a blessed new millennium.

Bradley was Knick, but Gore's the team player: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Lawrence, Jill

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/bradley-was-knick-gores-team-player/docview/408818765/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Bill Bradley was a loner, known for an independence that didn't always go down well at home in New Jersey or in the collegial club that is the U.S. Senate. Al Gore was a team player, a style that worked better as a senator and vice president than as a presidential candidate. * Bradley nearly lost his 1990 bid for a third Senate term from New Jersey to then-newcomer Christie Whitman, who is now the governor. The close call, caused largely by his refusal to take a stand on state tax increases, dissolved Bradley's image as a rising political star heading to greatness. By the time he retired from the Senate in January 1997, he was disillusioned with the political system and out of step with his party's increasingly tough-minded views on welfare. In Congress, Gore was the good student who took on complicated issues and studied them for months. "He was the intellectual," says Rep. Norm Dicks, D-Wash., who has worked with Gore on defense and arms control strategy for 20 years. He says Gore, a link to the Reagan administration for House moderates trying to influence U.S. arms control policy, wrote "draft after draft" of articles for prestigious publications.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Bradley+was+Knick%2C+but+Gore%27s+the+team+player&amp;author=Lawrence%2C+Jill&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-31&amp;spage=17.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   SPECIAL MILLENNIUM EDITION; THE YEARS IN CONGRESS; STYLES; The first primary votes are only a month away. What kind of presidents would the Democratic candidates be? Their contrasting leadership styles offer clues.; See related story: 18A
WASHINGTON
Bill Bradley was a loner, known for an independence that didn't always go down well at home in New Jersey or in the collegial club that is the U.S. Senate. Al Gore was a team player, a style that worked better as a senator and vice president than as a presidential candidate.
The political styles of the two Democrats are as different as their temperaments. Their priorities, legislative skills and relationships all hint at what types of presidents they'd be.
The two do share some political traits. Both are cerebral. Both had fast, stellar starts in Congress. And both have overcome setbacks to make strong White House bids:
* Bradley nearly lost his 1990 bid for a third Senate term from New Jersey to then-newcomer Christie Whitman, who is now the governor. The close call, caused largely by his refusal to take a stand on state tax increases, dissolved Bradley's image as a rising political star heading to greatness. By the time he retired from the Senate in January 1997, he was disillusioned with the political system and out of step with his party's increasingly tough-minded views on welfare.
* Gore clearly enjoys the advantages of incumbency. But as vice president he is naturally overshadowed by President Clinton -- and has been damaged politically by Clinton's scandal-ridden administration. His team spirit, meanwhile, led him to suppress some of his views and express others -- for instance, proclaiming Clinton one of the greatest U.S. presidents right after he'd been impeached.
Different kinds of legislators
In Congress, Gore was the good student who took on complicated issues and studied them for months. "He was the intellectual," says Rep. Norm Dicks, D-Wash., who has worked with Gore on defense and arms control strategy for 20 years. He says Gore, a link to the Reagan administration for House moderates trying to influence U.S. arms control policy, wrote "draft after draft" of articles for prestigious publications.
Gore's strong suit was as a spokesman. In floor speeches, hearings, articles and a book, he drew attention to issues he believed were pressing, including pollution, climate change, organ transplants and problems in the space program.
"In some ways, Gore is more effective working in the glare of the spotlight, and in some ways, Bradley was more effective in the back rooms," says Gregory Wetstone, a senior official at the Natural Resources Defense Council.
Bradley, though he got attention when he wanted it, was more involved in nuts and bolts, especially on fiscal and water policy. "As a legislator, I wanted to be a workhorse, not a show horse. The idea was to get things done, pass balls," he says, recalling his trademark as a New York Knick: passing so others could make baskets.
Like Gore, who subjected himself to 13 months of tutorials with an expert aide to master the nuances of nuclear arms, Bradley was an avid student of policy. He joined the Intelligence Committee and became an authority on the U.S.S.R.
The bi-annual Almanac of American Politics praised both men highly. In 1988, authors Michael Barone and Grant Ujifusa said Gore brought to his work seriousness, close study of facts and "an originality of thought that make him a natural leader." They were even more effusive about Bradley. In 1990, they wrote that many Republicans as well as Democrats "think the country would be lucky" if he became president. "He has demonstrated a special strength of character and self-discipline not often seen in politicians and a piercing insight into issues that goes quickly to the heart of the matter."
Sen. Bob Kerrey, D-Neb., a Bradley backer, says Bradley was influential because "you knew his conclusion would be sound. Many times when he argued, the argument would turn as a consequence of the way he argued it."
Bradley 'on his own'
In the Senate and at home in New Jersey, Bradley kept his own counsel. "He was pretty much a person who did things on his own," says Sen. John Breaux, D-La., a Gore ally who was on the Finance Committee with Bradley.
Some considered Bradley aloof, but others had a more benign take on his reserve. Former senator Alan Simpson, R-Wyo., calls him "a true celebrity" in a glaring spotlight because of his career with the Knicks: "Naturally, you pull back. If you didn't, you'd lose your marbles. I saw him as a shy person."
Bradley says it's important for leaders to have down time. "I don't think you have to be 'on' all the time to be a whole person," he says.
In his final Senate years, Bradley was criticized as disengaged. Some environmentalists say he sat on the sidelines in 1995-96 as Republicans tried to cut back environmental protection laws and agencies. Bradley says he worked to block those moves in committee and in filibusters on the floor.
Bradley also dropped out of a bipartisan group trying to salvage a health-care bill in 1994. He says he left because the others wouldn't agree to a tax break for workers with high-cost health insurance. Breaux, co-leader of the group, says Bradley should have stayed: "If you can't build coalitions and consensus for a policy, how can you be president?"
For Gore, a shaky start
Critics ask a different question of Gore. His campaign has been through a series of shifts as he separates from Clinton and reclaims his political identity. Changes in campaign staff, location, wardrobe and strategy have led some to wonder how he could run the country if he couldn't run a presidential campaign he'd anticipated for years.
Gore says moving his campaign from Washington to Nashville, reducing his staff and trading staged official events for more spontaneous encounters with voters all flowed from his realization that he needed to "establish a clear and direct line of communication with the American people on my own."
Gore says that as he launched his campaign, he was also helping his mother cope with his father's death and "dodging incoming rounds every 30 seconds" as the impeachment process unfolded. "Those aren't justifications for not having perfect pitch politically," he says and then starts to laugh. "I do think that the decision to put the headquarters on K Street (the lawyer-lobbyist heart of Washington) was an atrocious decision." His? "I signed off on it, sure."
It's "probably fair," Gore says, to say the initial setup of his campaign showed "faulty judgment as a politician. But the second half of that has to be I've learned from the experience."
Gore has establishment backing for his candidacy, no surprise given his close ties to party officials and interest groups. Bradley has maintained a distance from the New Jersey and national parties since his first election. "He didn't feel like he really needed the party organization, so he wasn't a part of it," says state Sen. Ray Lesniak, a former state party chairman who supports Gore.
Bradley says he campaigned for 40 Democratic candidates across the country after leaving the Senate and "made sure" a Democrat succeeded him. Lesniak says Bradley campaigned for New Jersey candidates when asked, "but it was not a great effort. It wasn't on a regular basis." When Lesniak chaired the state party in 1992-93, "we really had no interaction at all. There wasn't any hostility there. It was just how it was. That was just his style."
Illustration PHOTO,color,Eileen Blass,USA TODAY; PHOTO,color,Tim Dillon,USA TODAY; Caption: Bradley: Shown in New Hampshire last month, he often worked better out of spotlight, Senate colleagues say. Gore: Known as a good student on legislative issues. Here, he leads a discussion in a Nashville high school in December. Forging alliances: Bradley campaigns Nov. 30 for New Hampshire state Senate candidate Bill Lynch with Lynch's wife, Peggy. Speaking to students: One of Gore's strengths as a senator was speaking about complicated issues he cared about, such as pollution.

Title: Bradley was Knick, but Gore's the team player:   [FINAL Edition ]

Publication title: USA TODAY; McLean, Va.

Pages: 17A

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1999

Publication date: Dec 31, 1999

Section: NEWS

Publisher: USA Today, a division of Gannett Satellite Information Network, Inc.

Place of publication: McLean, Va.

The Wall Street Journal Millennium (A Special Report): Futurology --- Something to Believe In: Yes, technology seems to change almost daily; But some trends are likely to remain in force for a long time

Author: By Thomas Petzinger Jr.

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/wall-street-journal-millennium-special-report/docview/398839084/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr. [Gordon E.] Moore soon established Intel Corp., which went on to ride Moore's Law through years of prosperity. The entire world of today's computing -- plunging prices, hotter applications, the advent of the Internet -- is built on the certainty that less will always buy more. Have no fear. Although he didn't realize it, Mr. Moore was actually describing a trend as old as calculation itself. From the time of the earliest electromechanical calculators at the turn of the century (and by some estimates back to the abacus of ancient Babylonia), calculating speed has been increasing at almost precisely the rate that Moore's Law would indicate. And although very few trends continue unabated, this one will. Every advance in computing ensures a further advance because computers are the main tools by which we design computers. Call this trend Moore's Law II. Consider "DNA computing," which is generating intense research world-wide. (It, too, will play a role in Moore's Law II.) First envisioned by mathematician Leonard Adleman of the University of Southern California, it involves infusing a test tube of DNA with trillions of randomly generated DNA strands whose sequences represent all the possible solutions to a problem.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Wall+Street+Journal+Millennium+%28A+Special+Report%29%3A+Futurology+---+Something+to+Believe+In%3A+Yes%2C+technology+seems+to+change+almost+daily%3B+But+some+trends+are+likely+to+remain+in+force+for+a+long+time&amp;author=By+Thomas+Petzinger+Jr.&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-31&amp;spage=R.12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: In a time of terrific upheaval, are there any technology trends you can count on?
The answer is yes. No fewer than five broad movements in technology are virtually certain to remain in force as far into the future as anyone can see. These trends are secular, not cyclical -- the great movements within which lesser technology trends will rise and fall.
What makes these trends so certain? They're rooted in natural laws and biological drives. Most other trends spring from the vicissitudes of culture; these emerge from physical laws and human nature.
A warning: Not every career, corporation or investor embracing these trends will succeed. Far from it. The history of technology is replete with people who had the right idea but the wrong application; who got there first but were blindsided anyway; who caught the wave but lost their balance.
But while following these trends is no assurance of success, it's probably unwise to bet against them.
Moore's Law II
The best-known law of computing was first articulated in the April 19, 1965, edition of the journal Electronics under the heading "Jamming More Components Onto Integrated Circuits." The author, Gordon E. Moore, noted that from 1959 forward, the density of the transistors per square inch of integrated circuit had doubled every year. (Later, he revised the time period to 18 months).
Mr. Moore soon established Intel Corp., which went on to ride Moore's Law through years of prosperity. The entire world of today's computing -- plunging prices, hotter applications, the advent of the Internet -- is built on the certainty that less will always buy more.
But for how long? Mr. Moore and everyone else acknowledge that the gates and pathways of a chip can never shrink beyond the basic unit of electricity, the electron. Many people are concerned that when Moore's Law hits the wall, the heretofore unstinting increase in calculating speed will plateau, and economic growth along with it.
Have no fear. Although he didn't realize it, Mr. Moore was actually describing a trend as old as calculation itself. From the time of the earliest electromechanical calculators at the turn of the century (and by some estimates back to the abacus of ancient Babylonia), calculating speed has been increasing at almost precisely the rate that Moore's Law would indicate. And although very few trends continue unabated, this one will. Every advance in computing ensures a further advance because computers are the main tools by which we design computers. Call this trend Moore's Law II.
Once chips are packed to the physical limits of surface area, how will computers continue improving? In the near term, by expanding into three dimensions, with electrons skittering up and down as well as side to side. Improvements in chip design may also create advances once transistor density hits the wall.
Another near-term improvement involves lashing together great numbers of small computers in ways that create more than the sum of their parts, a method called "massively parallel" computing.
Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory, Livermore, Calif., already operates such a computer performing two trillion calculations a second. International Business Machines Corp. recently announced a $100 million plan to build a massively parallel machine capable of conducting more than a quadrillion operations a second -- the equivalent of two million of the most powerful desktop PCs operating simultaneously.
In the distance lie quantum computers. Quantum physics describes the duality of the subatomic realm: Sometimes a particle acts like a wave; sometimes a wave acts like a particle. Researchers believe they can harness that duality to act as a bit, which they call quantum bits, or "qubits." Scientists at Los Alamos have used radio waves to activate qubits in simple liquids; already they have succeeded in designing a quantum computer that solves one plus one.
Admittedly that's trifling -- for now. How powerful could a quantum computer be? By some estimates, the subatomic material in a cubic centimeter of salt could execute more instructions than all the computers in the world today.
One consequence of Moore's Law II is the shocking idea that machines may one day outwit humans.
"If I were a hunter -- which I never was much -- I sure as hell would want a smart hunting dog," says Herbert Simon of Carnegie Mellon University, a Nobel laureate in economics and an originator of artificial intelligence some 50 years ago. "So why not with computers? If you have one, let it be smart. We will work out the bargain between us."
Carbon-Silicon Union
Forget the merger of the Web with TV, of phones with computers. The most powerful convergence under way today involves silicon, the substrate of computing, and carbon, the substrate of life. Science author Kevin Kelly calls it the union of "the made and the born." As he puts it, "Machines are becoming biological, and the biological is becoming engineered."
Consider "DNA computing," which is generating intense research world-wide. (It, too, will play a role in Moore's Law II.) First envisioned by mathematician Leonard Adleman of the University of Southern California, it involves infusing a test tube of DNA with trillions of randomly generated DNA strands whose sequences represent all the possible solutions to a problem.
Various chemical techniques eliminate sequences that fail to bind to various partial solutions. The sequence left at the end is the best solution. DNA computing has already solved the famous "traveling salesman" problem, which involves finding the shortest distance for a multicity travel schedule, and is under study as the next wave in encryption and code breaking.
Obstacles abound -- the calculations are error-prone and the DNA proteins fragile -- but by some reckonings a single test tube of genetic material could execute a quadrillion-million instructions per second.
Already in practical use today is the "gene chip." In one application, you unzip a helical strand of DNA containing a defective gene and use an ultraviolet light to etch it onto a glass chip. Then you deposit a patient's DNA on the chip. If it bonds with the etched version, then the sample also contains the defective gene.
"We represent the integration of microelectronics and molecular biology," says Tina Nova, president of Nanogen Inc., a San Diego gene-chip pioneer. She foresees a time when anyone with a sore throat will insert a DNA sample into a home appliance and upload the data for diagnosis.
The made and the born are converging at larger scales as well. A new generation of "smart" prosthetics, using chips that learn the neurology of the user, are sharply reducing the time required to become facile with artificial limbs. Monsanto Corp. is developing plants that produce plastics.
But the most widespread convergence involves the use of life's design principles in the design of computing. One technology involves so-called genetic algorithms, which create random solutions to complex problems, then "breed" the answers until the fittest variant is found. Genetic algorithms are being used to solve complex shop-scheduling problems at Deere & Co., to discover new fuel additives at Purdue University, to create intelligent vehicle controllers at the U.S. Navy, and they are being used in many locations to actually write programs.
And why not? After all, the technology of life has been solving complex problems for more than three billion years.
Intelligence to the Edges
"All intelligence is collective," technology historian George Dyson writes in "Darwin Among the Machines," "...whether that of a billion neurons, a billion microprocessors, or a billion molecules forming a single cell." At the scale of human society, intelligence is devolving from centers and tops and spreading over the widest possible expanse. Technologies that facilitate this migration of intelligence to the edge already rank among the most important of our time.
The Internet, for one, has no intelligent center -- indeed, no center at all. It is pure periphery, yet very smart as a result. Likewise, the mainframe computer, once housed in its tightly sealed glass house and accessible only to a brotherhood of specialized caretakers, has devolved into millions of desktop units, driving intelligence to the edges.
Now, human intelligence is following the path of the machines. In some companies -- DuPont Co. and DaimlerChrysler Corp. are examples -- cross-functional teams and learning networks spring up spontaneously in response to specific problems.
One of the most vivid demonstrations of decentralized human intelligence involves the explosion in "open source" software development. Commercial software developers increasingly place their best work in the public domain, knowing that cadres of programmers will offer ways to improve, refine and debug it.
Sun Microsystems Inc. is giving away the source code for its Jini technology, which permits appliances and computing devices to communicate, to anyone promising to share in whatever improvements they make. In what may be the fastest-growing segment of computing, Web servers run on the open-source Linux operating system, which was developed -- and is still being improved -- by programmers scattered all over the world.
Technologies that restrict the flow of intelligence toward the edges are headed for trouble. Already a backlash is building against popular work-flow systems (generally called "enterprise resource planning," or ERP, systems) that reduce the ability of employees to change how they work.
Picturing Data
Desktop computing exploded with the advent of folders, icons and drag-down menus, those images known collectively as the graphical user interface. It succeeded spectacularly because it transformed massive collections of data into the form most accessible to the human brain: pictures.
We evolved looking at landscapes, objects and each other, not at letters, numbers, and lines of code. Literacy and numeracy succeeded so long as they were simple. The complexities of modern science, society and commerce -- intensified by computers gushing data -- leave us dumbfounded. But computing is now part of the solution as well, thanks to programs that turn data into images, animation, simulations and even games.
Analytical Graphics Inc. of King of Prussia, Pa., sells software that converts imponderable positioning data from satellites into real-time moving pictures seen from any angle or distance. Network administrators of all kinds -- telephone, computer, natural gas, even the back office at the New York Stock Exchange -- watch for patterns and anomalies in data-visualization systems that they could never hope to see in printouts.
In another category, some programs convert inaccessible physical objects to data, and then reconstruct the data into images that are accessible at any scale. Physicians at the Mayo Clinic, for instance, create three-dimensional digital views of a patient's colon, which they can explore from the inside. Engineers at Texaco Inc. look at geological structures deep underground as if they were side-cuts along an interstate highway.
Increasingly, these programs allow executives to play -- and learn -- through "what-if" scenarios, no differently than pilots simulate a variety of flying conditions. TransCanada Pipelines Ltd., one of the largest energy providers in North America, uses complex, data-rich simulations to help natural-gas drillers and shippers coordinate their efforts. Swiss Reinsurance Co., a major reinsurer, uses a graphic simulation called Insurance World to search for risk patterns and to play out the effects of strategies in the marketplace.
Unintended Consequences
Jesse Gelsinger.
His name should rank with Thalidomide, Love Canal and the Ford Pinto as examples of the tragic and unintended consequences that technology can bring. Last September, at age 18, Mr. Gelsinger died in a seemingly uncomplicated procedure in which his liver was injected with genetic material for research on a rare inherited disease.
His disease was no threat to his life. Yet the procedure inexplicably destroyed his lungs in the first death publicly attributed to gene therapy.
At the dawn of the genomics age, it's worth remembering that every technology -- every single one -- bears unintended consequences. Drug makers call them "side effects."
"We can never do merely one thing," the microbiologist Garrett Hardin wrote nearly 40 years ago. "Wishing to kill insects, we may put an end to the singing of birds. Wishing to `get there' faster, we insult our lungs with smog."
But just as technology creates unintended effects, it is our only hope for conquering them. Only technology can widen the field of vision in which we can detect the unintended effects of earlier technologies.
For instance, "now we have models that indicate strongly, very strongly, that the greenhouse effect may well produce large-scale climate change," says Murray Gell-Mann of the Santa Fe Institute, who won the Nobel Prize in physics for his discovery of the subatomic quark. "And the models are getting better and better."
To the extent we're blind to technology's unintended consequences, we can be forgiven.
To the extent we ignore them, we're acting unethically.
---

People, get ready As we enter the new millennium, a second age of enlightenment could be dawning -- one where the little guy fights back against corporate Big Brother

Author: Lasn, Kalle

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/people-get-ready-as-we-enter-new-millennium/docview/384537682/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: As the rolling waves of protesters moved through the downtown core, I changed my mind. These people were passionate, they were having fun, and the signs they carried were sharper than anything I could have thought up. One big placard simply said "WTO" -- except the letters were corporate logos (the golden arches upside-down for "W," the Texaco "T" and the "O"-shaped eye of CBS).

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=People%2C+get+ready+As+we+enter+the+new+millennium%2C+a+second+age+of+enlightenment+could+be+dawning+--+one+where+the+little+guy+fights+back+against+corporate+Big+Brother&amp;author=Lasn%2C+Kalle&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-31&amp;spage=R.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   As we enter the new millennium, a second age of enlightenment could be dawning -- one where the little guy fights back against corporate Big Brother
People, get ready
As we enter the new millennium, a second age of enlightenment could be dawning -- one where the little guy fights back against corporate Big Brother
Friday, December 31, 1999
Special to The Globe and Mail
Vancouver -- To be honest, when I showed up in Seattle last month to watch the WTO circus unfold, I wasn't expecting much from the protesters. I thought they would be the usual rabble-rousing lefty crowd, charged up but not particularly focused, thoughtful or effective.
But as the rolling waves of protesters moved through the downtown core, I changed my mind. These people were passionate, they were having fun, and the signs they carried were sharper than anything I could have thought up. One big placard simply said "WTO" -- except the letters were corporate logos (the golden arches upside-down for "W," the Texaco "T" and the "O"-shaped eye of CBS). It became clear that the vast majority of protesters understood what was fundamentally at stake and what the trade summit really boiled down to: civic culture versus corporate culture. More than that, these folks had found a way to distill the message into potent memes. It's with memes, not bombs or bullets or tear-gas, that the real geopolitical battle of the next century will be fought; and with ground troops like those folks in Seattle, "the people" may just win.
A meme (rhymes with "dream") is a unit of information (a catch-phrase, a concept, a tune, a notion of fashion, philosophy or politics) that leaps from brain to brain. Memes compete with one another and are passed through a population much the same way genes pass through a species. Good strong memes can change minds, alter behaviour, catalyze collective mindshifts and transform cultures. In our information age, whoever has the memes has the power.
For about 40 years, corporations have had the power. They've been beaming their memes into our brains at the rate of about 3,000 marketing messages per day (that includes all the ads you see on TV, on your computer screen, in magazines, on radio, billboards, plus all the brands and logos on buildings, T-shirts, appliances, etc.). This onslaught -- arguably the biggest psychological experiment ever carried out on the human race -- has affected us profoundly. The food we eat, the cars we drive, the way we feel about our bodies, our sexuality, what music we think is cool, have all been shaped by the billions of pro-consumption memes dumped into our collective subconscious daily.
But lately, counter memes -- of the sort seen in Seattle -- have appeared more frequently in the mindscape: fashion billboards "liberated" by creative editors with spray cans; a burping pig on a TV ad chastizing North Americans for consuming too much; a bumper sticker that asks: "Is Economic Progress Killing the Planet?"
The meme warrior's best friend is, of course, the Internet. Last year, the multilateral agreement on investment initiative was killed largely by the spread of anti-MAI sentiment across the Net. Hundreds of thousands of people E-mailed their friends, set up Web sites, held virtual protests and found ways to hack into and jam corporate sites.
A resistance movement against Monsanto Co. and genetically modified foods has gained strength on the Net. The outrage against GM started in the United Kingdom (where folks spooked by mad-cow disease weren't wild about further human experimentation), then spread worldwide. Word memes like "Frankenfoods" and visual memes depicting the bizarre genetic mutations in our future struck a chord. Many months before "The Battle in Seattle," the core issues were hotly debated on the Net.
I believe the next few years will see an intensification of meme warfare throughout our mental environment. Which is to say, not in the sky or on the streets, and not in the forests or around international fishing boundaries as in earlier eras, but in newspapers and magazines, on the radio, on TV and in cyberspace. This global guerrilla information war will be a no-holds barred propaganda battle of competing world views and alternative visions of the future.
The most critical areas will involve the sovereignty of individual citizens and of nations against the sovereignty of corporations. Here are what I find to be the three most potent metamemes currently in the social activist's arsenal:

  

Metameme 1:

  



  

No Corporate "I"

  



  

This is the idea that corporations are not "persons" with constitutional rights and freedoms of their own but legal fictions that we ourselves created and must therefore control.
Are corporations legitimate agents of progress or merely generators of shareholder-profit at the planet's expense? Should transnationals like Monsanto and Time Warner Inc. have the power to challenge the laws of sovereign nations? In the next century, will a handful of giant corporations dominate every industry? Will corporations rule the world? Or is this just a clever lefty pseudo-meme? That's the mind-field we must negotiate.
If my reading of "The Battle in Seattle" is correct, the next 10 years will be a period of intense corporate-bashing. Corporations like Philip Morris Cos. Inc. will be pushed to the wall. Aggressive megacorps like Monsanto that think they can "SLAP suit" -- prevent criticism via libel chill -- the world into their kind of nutritional agenda will suffer. (SLAPP is an acronym for "strategic lawsuit against public participation.") Any upcoming MAI or WTO trade initiatives are doomed. A visceral, Net-centred reaction against corporate bigness is under way.
At stake is the very legitimacy of corporations, their "personhood" under the law. Should corporations have legal rights and freedoms under the Constitution like we human beings do, or should they be subordinate legal entities with no inherent rights?
If enough people pick up on the No Corporate "I" meme and start relating to corporations in new, more rambunctious ways, then our business culture will change in profound ways.
For one, there will be harsh, new corporate criminal liability laws. Corporate lawbreakers will not be allowed to bid for government contracts or hold television licences; they will be barred from lobbying activities and financing political campaigns. Companies that dump toxic wastes, damage watersheds, fix prices, exploit employees or keep vital information secret from customers, will pay huge penalties. Rogue corporations that willfully break the law will have their charters revoked, their assets sold and the money funnelled into superfunds for their victims.
Rules of incorporation will be rewritten so that shareholders assume partial liability for the companies they own. They will continue to reap the rewards when the going is good and share prices are rising, but they will have to share the responsibility when the company they own becomes criminally liable.
This one simple mind shift in the way we think about shareholders will transform stock markets. Fewer shares will be traded. Instead of simply choosing the biggest cash cows, shareholders will carefully investigate the backgrounds of the companies they are about to sink their money into. They'll think twice about buying shares in Philip Morris, R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Co. or Monsanto. Too risky. They'll buy into resource companies with good environmental records. They'll stay away from multinationals that use child workers or break labour laws overseas.
In other words, shareholders will be "grounded" -- forced to take responsibility. Stock markets will cease to be gambling casinos. Our whole business culture will heave.

  

Metameme 2:

  



  

Media carta

  



  

This is the idea that every human being has the "right to communicate" -- to receive and impart information through any media.
In 1995, Adbusters Media Foundation (the non-profit organization that I co-founded) launched a Canadian Charter legal action against the CBC for refusing to sell us airtime for our social marketing TV messages. Those 30-second spots included "Obsession Fetish" (a critique of the fashion industry featuring a bulimic Kate Moss look-alike) and "Buy Nothing Day" (a call for a consumer fast every Nov. 26). The case wound its way through the courts until the Supreme Court of Canada threw it out in 1998. We are now trying to take it to the World Court or the United Nations Commission on Human Rights.
Earlier eras had their critical human-rights battles. In this information age, we need to fight for a new right -- one many of us assumed we already had: the right to communicate. This new measure would, for example, give every citizen the right to walk into their local TV station and buy 30 seconds of airtime for a message he or she believes in.
We must fight for potent, practical mechanisms for citizen access to the airwaves. I like the idea of a "two-minute media revolution" in which the CRTC, when granting broadcast licences, is obliged to give two minutes of every broadcast hour back to the people for citizen use.
Antitrust lawsuits, such as the U.S. Attorney-General's suit against Microsoft, can also be an effective way to break up media megacorporations. If enough fed-up citizens demanded a freer, more diverse cultural environment, the U.S. and Canadian governments could be pressured to go after Time Warner, News Corp. and Disney, and limit the number of TV stations, newspapers and radio stations each is allowed to own.
The ultimate goal of course, is to break the commercial monopoly of TV and create a free marketplace of ideas where competing memes and visions of the future battle it out every night on prime time.
Media Carta is the great human-rights battle of our information age. At stake is our ability to create our own culture from the bottom up, instead of having it spoon-fed to us top down by a corporate-owned and -operated media. Maybe Canadians can be the first nation to enshrine "the right to communicate" as a fundamental human right in our Charter of Rights and Freedoms?

  

Metameme 3:

  



  

The True-Cost Marketplace

  



  

In the global marketplace of the future, the price of every product will tell the ecological truth.
Progressive economists and the activist left continue to push for various "eco" and "carbon" taxes that would punish polluters by hitting them in the wallet. In my opinion, this approach is short-sighted. I say, let's have an across-the-board pricing system that tells the ecological truth and let the marketplace sort things out.
Take the personal car. More than any other product, the automobile is a symbol of the need for a true-cost marketplace, in which the price you pay reflects all the costs of production and operation. That doesn't just mean paying the manufacturing cost plus markup, plus oil, gas and insurance. It means paying for the pollution, for building and maintaining the roads, for the medical costs of accidents and the noise and the esthetic degradation caused by urban sprawl. It means paying for traffic policing and perhaps even for the military protection of oil fields and supply lines.
The true cost of a car must also include the real but hard-to-estimate cost to future generations of dealing with the oil depletion and climate change the car is creating today. If we add up the best available estimates, we'd come to a startling conclusion: The fossil-fuel-based automobile industry is being subsidized by unborn generations to the tune of hundreds of billions of dollars every year.
In the true-cost marketplace of the future, no one will prevent you from driving. You'll simply have to pay the real price of piloting your tonne of metal around, spewing carbon out of the tailpipe every year. Your private automobile will cost you, by some estimates, about $100,000. And a tankful of gas, $250.
Moving over a 15-year period toward true-cost driving would force us to reinvent the way we get around. When the majority of people can no longer afford to drive, enormous public demand for monorails, bullet trains, subways and streetcars would emerge. Automakers would design eco-friendly alternatives: vehicles that recycle their own energy, human-and-fuel powered hybrids, lightweight solar vehicles. Citizens would demand more bike lanes, pedestrian paths and car-free downtowns.
A paradigm shift in urban planning would ensue. About 10 years into the transition period, personal automobiles would become more trouble than they're worth. People would start enjoying their calmer lifestyles and the new psycho-geography of their cities.
In a true-cost marketplace, the price of a pack of cigarettes will include the extra burden it places on our health-care system; the price of an avocado will reflect the real cost of flying it thousands of kilometres to your supermarket; the cost of nuclear energy (if indeed we can afford it) will include the estimated cost (and risk) of storing the radioactive waste in Earth's crust for millions of years.
True-cost is a solution almost all political persuasions can sort of agree with. Conservatives like the idea because it's a logical extension of their free-market philosophy. Progressives like it because it involves a radical restructuring of the status quo. Governments like it because it gives them a vital new function to fulfill: that of calculating the true-costs of products, levying eco-taxes and managing our bio-economic affairs for the long term. And environmentalists like it because it may be the only way to achieve sustainability in our lifetimes.
As the millennium turns, I think we're on the verge of a great global mind shift -- a transformation of human affairs comparable in scale to the Renaissance and the Enlightenment. On the eve of this turn, I propose a toast: Cheers! Prosit ! Kanpai ! Hallelujah! May the best memes win.

  

Kalle Lasn is editor of Adbusters magazine and author of Culture Jam: The Uncooling of America(TM)

Cold Toronto winters to be a memory in 50 years, experts predict Forget the snow shovel and snowmobile, people will be occupied with trying to get enough water for crops and lawns, climate researchers say

Author: Southworth, Natalie

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cold-toronto-winters-be-memory-50-years-experts/docview/384398352/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: An increase in the incidence of severe storms and floods is cause for worry, said Paul Kovacs, executive director of the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction, which is funded largely by the University of Western Ontario. "We believe the people in Toronto are quite vulnerable to extreme weather," he said.

Full text:   Forget the snow shovel and snowmobile, people will be occupied with trying to get enough water for crops and lawns, climate researchers say
Cold Toronto winters to be a memory in 50 years, experts predict
Forget the snow shovel and snowmobile, people will be occupied with trying to get enough water for crops and lawns, climate researchers say
Thursday, December 30, 1999
The Globe and Mail
Toronto -- -*** This series asks what it would take to make Toronto a more liveable city.

  



  

Monday: What if . . . Toronto were a city within a park?

  



  

Tuesday: What if. . . Toronto were a family-friendly city?

  



  

Yesterday: What if . . . Toronto were more accessible to cyclists?

  



  

Today: What if . . . Toronto had the weather of Washington, D.C.?

  



  

Tomorrow: What if . . . Toronto reduced its reliance on cars?

  

-***

  

Light rain will taper off by mid-morning with temperatures reaching 12 to 14 degrees Celsius. Expect mild weather overnight and for much of the next three to four months.
No, it's not a cruel joke, rather an almost certain forecast of Toronto's mid-December climate 50 years from now.
Climate in the greater Toronto area is warming at a rate double the global average, Environment Canada says. In the future, weather in Toronto will be more like the present climate of Washington than like a northern city in the shadow of the snow belt.
"This is where the people are, the foundations of our economy. The GTA will get the greatest impact of climate change from a human perspective," said Quentin Chiotti, who is working on a study of atmospheric change in the Toronto and Niagara region.
The warming trend is expected to alter many features of Toronto's infrastructure, environment and cultural life.
To see a bit of the future, look no further than Washington, which has only one or two minor snowfalls in winter and sees temperatures that reach well into the 30s in summer.
"It's December and I'm still playing tennis on an outdoor court," said Dorn McGrath, a professor of urban and regional planning at George Washington University in Washington.
Torontonians may have to say farewell to most outdoor skating rinks and snow shovels. They also may say hello to longer golf and construction seasons, bigger beaches and lower heating bills. Snowmobiling and other wintertime activities are expected gradually to disappear.
"The wildlife could rest in peace in the wintertime again," joked Henry Hengeveld, an adviser on climate at Environment Canada.
He said that the city will have less snow in winter and more intense thunderstorms, sunshine and downpours in summer. The average temperature will increase by four to five degrees in summer and at least that much in winter. "The snow-removal budget will [need to be] almost nonexistent," Mr. Hengeveld said. But money saved will likely be money spent on intermittent bouts of extreme weather, which are expected to increase.
Heat waves could burden the health-care system. As temperatures soar, air quality worsens, smog increases and the elderly and young are more vulnerable to illness, Mr. Hengeveld said.
Health costs are expected to be offset by a warmer climate. The highest mortality from weather in Canada is because of the cold.
In Washington, severe air-pollution problems have erupted in the past five years. Prof. McGrath said warm temperatures and a high number of motorists are to blame. "People are anguishing sufficiently over this," he said.
In the Toronto area, an even more pressing weather concern is expected to arise. "The key concern for Southern Ontario is the drought issue. There will be a need for water management," Mr. Hengeveld said.
With the higher temperatures, the growing season will be longer, providing the potential for more abundant crops. But irrigating the land regularly won't be easy, he said.
The supply of water is expected to decrease, while demand for water, to generate electricity and to farm, is likely to increase.
Environment Canada predicts levels of lakes, including the Great Lakes, will shrink one metre in the next 50 to 100 years.
Heavily laden boats may have difficulty moving through canals and ports. "Boats would have to carry lighter loads and take more trips, which is expensive," said Linda Mortsch, a researcher at the Faculty of Environmental Studies at the University of Waterloo.
However, warmer winters mean the shipping season could be extended because ice won't cover the Great Lakes or the St. Lawrence Seaway for very long.
Also, as the depth of lakes decreases, the size of beaches increases, said Ms. Mortsch. "You will have to walk farther from your dock to your boat."
The change in water temperature and depth will gradually replace trout and whitefish in local lakes with fish that prefer warm, poor-quality water, such as carp. And as temperatures and rain increase in the GTA, more mosquitoes are expected to move into the region, attracted by damp areas in which they can lay their eggs. Malaria might arise in Southwestern Ontario, Mr. Hengeveld said.
An increase in the incidence of severe storms and floods is cause for worry, said Paul Kovacs, executive director of the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction, which is funded largely by the University of Western Ontario. "We believe the people in Toronto are quite vulnerable to extreme weather," he said.
The first storm that exceeded the cost of $100-million occurred 10 years ago in Calgary. The price of extreme weather has skyrocketed since then. Last year, Montreal's ice storm cost $2.5-billion; of that, $1.4-billion was covered by insurance.
Floods are of particular concern because they are not covered by insurance. To reduce costs from flooding, infrastructure must be adapted to handle larger amounts of rainfall. Sewer pipes could be widened and storm sewers deepened to hold more water, Mr. Kovacs said.

Full text:   824. The theme of the White House millennium activities is "Honor the Past - Imagine the Future."
823. In the town of Woodbury, N.J., a millennium project will link the city to its English roots. In the spring of 2000, a 112- foot replica of a 1725 brig, the Phoenix, will retrace the historic journey of Henry Wood, a Quaker from Bury who is credited with founding Woodbury.
822. Some 40 million pilgrims, visitors and tourists are expected to converge on Rome during the year 2000.
821. Ed Woodyard booked his room for New Year's Eve 1999 back in 1983 while the Marriott Marquis in Times Square was still under construction. Woodyard explained that he wanted to relive a memorable New Year's Eve as a child in Times Square.
820. Remember: Pets, alcoholic beverages and weapons are not permitted in American Red Cross shelters.
819. Public shelters should be your last resort. Try staying at a friend's house or a local hotel.
818. If a disaster catches you without a supply of clean water, you can use the water in your hot-water tank, pipes and ice cube trays. As a last resort, you can use the water in the reservoir tank of your toilet (not the bowl).
817. Two ways to purify water: Boiling and disinfection. Boil water for three to five minutes and let cool before drinking. Or add 16 drops of household bleach per gallon of water. Let stand for 30 minutes before using. Do not use scented bleach, color-safe bleach or bleach with added cleaners.
816. Listen to the radio or get a battery-operated television for the latest information.
815. If the electricity goes off, eat perishable food from the refrigerator first.
816. Then, eat the foods from the freezer. To minimize the number of times you open the freezer door, post a list with the freezer contents. In a well-filled, well-insulated freezer, foods usually will have ice crystals in their centers (meaning the food is safe to eat) for three days.
815. Finally, begin to use non-perishable foods and staples.
814. Conventional wisdom has it that Saturday will be the start of a new millennium, but some argue it won't really start until Jan. 1, 2001. Here's why: The sixth-century monk who concocted the calendar we use today didn't factor in a year "zero". That means we went straight from 1 B.C. to 1 A.D. In order for the current millennium to end at the stroke of midnight Friday, the first century would have had only 99 years.
813. Seminole County has spent $1.3 million during the last three years expanding and upgrading its technology with Y2K-compliant systems.
812. Residents can ask Seminole County officials Y2K-related questions on New Year's Eve by calling 407-665-2650, 407-665-2651 or 407-665-2652.
811. Successful Y2K tests were performed in June on Seminole County traffic signals along U.S. Highway 17-92 from First Street to Button Road, and along Lake Mary Boulevard from Markham Woods Road to Hidden Lake Drive, and at two intersections on County Road 46A and another two on Sanford Avenue.
810. Seminole County has considered itself Y2K-compliant since July.
809. Al Hill will lose his status as Seminole County's Y2K coordinator after New Year's Eve. In 2000, he will return to his full- time job as manager of the county's geographical information system.
808. Three older fax machines in Seminole County will continue to work, but will print a partial date of "00" instead of "2000" on faxes received after midnight Friday.
807. There are 80 terms in a Y2K glossary on the federal government's consumer-issues Web page at www.consumer.gov/y2k/.
806. The phrase "computer bug" originated years ago in the United States when "inexplicable faults" were traced to moths found flying inside faulty computers .
805. Debugging is the process of finding and removing the causes of failures in software and has nothing to do with removing moths from computers.
804. If you want to spend New Year's Eve at your favorite International Drive attraction, you may be out of luck. Several attractions are closing early, so call ahead.
803. European visitors can celebrate the New Year on their own time at Lucky Leprechaun Irish Pub. Lucky Leprechaun is hosting two New Year's celebrations - one at 7 p.m. for European tourists and another at midnight for Americans.
802. Dinner attractions such as Medieval Times and King Henry's Feast are charging some princely sums for their special New Year's Eve packages. King Henry's adult tickets are $90 for its 9 p.m. show compared with a regular admission price of $37.95. Medieval Times tickets are $92.95 for the 9:30 show compared with $39.95 regular admission. Call ahead or you could come up short.
800. Y2K will begin at 6 a.m. our time in New Zealand.
799. George Orwell's book 1984 was published 35 years earlier, in 1949. Stanley Kubrick's movie 2001: A Space Odyssey was released 33 years earlier, in 1968. Prince's album 1999 was released 17 years earlier, in 1982.
798. Jazz great Louis Armstrong, who died in 1971, would have turned 100 in 2000. Same goes for actress Helen Hayes, who died in 1993.
797. Officials with Florida Power Corp. and Florida Power and Light sent a mixed signal when trying to assure the public that their nuclear reactor facilities are Y2K-compliant. They said most of the technology in the reactors is so old that Y2K issues won't be affected.
796. Go see a Magic game and watch the potential NBA Coach of the Year in action.
795. If you're thinking about swinging into the new year at Atlantic Dance Hall on Disney's BoardWalk, forget about it. Its 1940s- style New Year's Eve celebration sold out a few weeks ago.
794. Looking for a cozy first meal of the new millennium? For the best romantic dinner spot in Orlando, Sentinel restaurant critic Scott Joseph recently chose Delfino Riviera at the Portofino Bay Hotel at Universal Studios Escape. Call 407-503-1000 for reservations.
793. Before you go back to work, brush up on your rights on the job. Read Your Workplace Rights (Amacom, $22.95) by Robert Gregory.
792. Review the deposit insurance coverage of your credit union account if you have a lot of money in savings. Check with your credit union or the National Credit Union Administration at 703-518-6330.
791. Consumers can get general information on Y2K by calling the President's Council on Year 2000 Conversion at 1-888-USA-4-Y2K.
790. Web junkies should get in as much surfing as they can this week and plan to take a break on New Year's Eve.
788. Stock up on CDs and a few good books.
787. Consider sending important mail certified or registered, to have a record of when you mailed it.
786. Use sunscreen.
785. Better brush up on that hygiene: When asked what will pose the greatest threat to human health in the next century, environmentalist Eugene Linden, author of The Future in Plain Sight: Nine Clues to the Coming Instability, says it is infectious disease. As he sees it, when the biosphere becomes unbalanced from pollution, climate change and human intervention, new infectious diseases - and virulent strains of older ones thought conquered - will be a major threat.
784. Be sure to protect electronic gizmos. Surges and brownouts that could accompany interruptions in electrical service could damage delicate equipment such as computers.
783. Millennium celebrations in China will center on the capital of Beijing, where a "China Century Altar" will be erected. The altar will be a mix of traditional and modern Chinese culture and art.
782. In addition to a New Year's Eve party in Brazil, the group Viva Rio is promoting a 72-hour ceasefire in the 600 slums of Rio de Janeiro. It is a climax to months of local grassroots action and community policing.
781. After conducting a massive audit, federal regulators say more than 99 percent of the nation's financial institutions, automated- teller-machine vendors and service providers are Y2K compliant.
780. A majority of the nation's chief information officers and other executives plan to have plenty of cash on hand in case financial glitches hit when New Year arrives, according to a recent survey by CIO magazine.
779. Using global communications links, the president's Y2K council plans to be a zone-by-zone clearinghouse for information about what is happening around the world. Banks, utilities, multinational companies and other national governments will be feeding information to the White House Y2K communications center, which will become active Tuesday.
778. The FDIC has set up a consumer hotline (1-800-934-3342) to answer questions about Y2K.
777. The task force also is warning the public about callers offering to move your money into a Y2K-safe account, asking for your account number and other personal information.
776. And watch out for pollsters asking personal questions about how you'll prepare for Y2K. The callers are looking for people hoarding cash - likely targets of a burglary.
775. Throughout the world, 2000 will roll like a wave through the time zones, starting in the far West. If things go wrong with certain systems elsewhere, experts in the United States hope to find out soon enough to fix the same type of systems here or implement backup plans.
774. Officials at Orlando International Airport will monitor major airports in Europe and Asia to see what type of problems occur there. OIA also will pass its information on to airports three hours behind in California.
773. Y2K planners are less concerned about potential personal- computer problems and more concerned about so-called embedded systems - internal computer chips that control equipment such as elevators, factories or building systems. Embedded electronics are more difficult to evaluate and test for Y2K problems.
772. The U.S. Commerce Department estimates that the cost of the United States' battle with Y2K will exceed $100 billion.
771. The American Hospital Association says the nation's hospitals have spent about $8 billion to ensure their computers, medical equipment and electronics will not be affected by Y2K.
770. Releasing his report on Y2K and the economy, Commerce Secretary William M. Daley said he foresees minor problems but no lasting overall economic damage from the millennium bug.
769. The National Institute of Standards in Technology - one of the federal government's lead agencies for helping manufacturers - says less than 30,000 of the estimated 380,000 small U.S. factories have obtained the agency's free Y2K-solution self-help kits.
768. Cheap Trick will "Party through the Decades" at Disney-MGM Studios along with LFO and Big Time Operator on Friday from 9 a.m.-2 a.m.
767. Epcot's New Year's Eve celebration includes entertainment by Cubanismo, the Tapestry of Nations parade and IllumiNations 2000: Reflections of Earth show. The park opens at 9 a.m. Friday and closes at 2 a.m. Saturday.
