765. Universal Studios Escape's "Blast Off 2000" celebration will include parades, pyrotechnics and party zones, as well as the usual attractions. The celebrations are at Universal Studios Florida, Islands of Adventure, CityWalk and the Portofino Bay Hotel. Hours: 8 a.m.-2 a.m.
763. Dress up in black tie for dancing at Church Street Station's Millennium Gala Dinner. A multi-course meal will be served at 7 p.m.
762. Pleasure Island has Hootie and the Blowfish, the B-52s and Styx, a buffet with more than 70 items and a champagne toast at midnight with a special performance of "P.I. 2000." Party from 8 p.m.- 2 a.m.
761. Don't drive drunk. The legal threshhold for intoxication is .08 percent blood alcohol. What does that mean? An average 180-pound man can reach .08 percent after four drinks in an hour, and an average 120-pound woman can get there in about 21/2 drinks in an hour.
760. Millennium marathons, anyone? You'll finds hours upon hours of The Andy Griffith Show on TBS, The Jetsons on the Cartoon Network and The Twilight Zone on Sci-Fi.
759. Want to spend the moment the new year starts with a really big star? Try Elvis Presley in Kissin' Cousins at 11:30 p.m. on Turner Classic Movies. Or Cher, who's profiled by VH1 at 11:30 p.m.
758. Eager to watch a movie as the new year dawns? Starting at midnight: When Harry Met Sally... on Lifetime; Back to the Future on TBS; and Legionnaire on USA.
757. The Arlington Institute, a future-oriented think tank, has been polling 1,500 Americans every two weeks since late October about their beliefs, fears and plans concerning Y2K. To see what the institute found, check its Web site - www.arlingtoninstitute.org/VSM/ VSMindex.htm
756. If you have courthouse business, such as paying child support or a traffic ticket, don't wait until the last minute. The Orange County Courthouse is closed Friday.
755. If you are due in court on Monday, Jan. 3, arrive early. The first day of the new year will be very busy. Expect long lines at the elevators.
754. If you get nabbed for drunken driving and need a lawyer, try the Orange County Bar Association's Lawyer Referral Service at 949- 440-6747.
752. If you're really bored and want something to do, take a virtual tour of the Orange County Courthouse. Log on to the internet at www.ninja9.org and click on virtual tour.
751. There is going to be a crush of revelers at the theme parks on New Year's Eve, making it easy to lose track of your family. Arrange a meeting place before you hit the parks. Pick a place that's highly visible and easy to reach, such as Cinderella Castle at the Magic Kingdom or Seuss Landing's Cat in the Hat at Islands of Adventure.
750. Are you ringing in the New Year at a theme park with small children in tow? Don't worry, you can still ride the thrill rides. By taking turns watching the younger tots, parents can still enjoy all the rides.
749. Disney revamped Epcot to prepare for a 15-month celebration of the millennium, and Epcot's Millennium Village is expected to be packed on New Year's Eve. Get to the park early if you want to get in.
748. You only have a few more days to check out the Osborne Family Spectacle of Lights at Disney-MGM Studios. It ends Sunday.
747. Make sure each child carries identification, parents' names and contact information , in case you become separated at a theme park.
746. If you need a moment out of the fray at Orlando International Airport to reflect on the holidays, relax in the airport chapel. It's located just past the security checkpoint to Gates 1-59.
745. As Father Time ticks into the new millennium, estimates are there is a birth every eight seconds in the United States, a death every 12 seconds and an immigrant entering the country every 39 seconds.
744. Horizon 2000, New Year's Eve in Downtown Orlando, will feature family activities, entertainment, rides, fireworks and a Millennium Clock countdown. It's from 6 p.m.-12:30 a.m. at Lake Eola Park.
741. Even if you decide to go at the last minute, Orlando's celebration should have room for you. All city parking garages will be available, and free buses will shuttle you from the more far- flung ones to the park. One must-have: a blanket. And feel free to bring a picnic basket; while food will be sold in the park, a homemade nosh might be tastier and less of a hassle.
743. A normal year has 53 weeks, with one or two of those weeks starting or finishing in the prior or following year. The year 2000 has 54 weeks. The last time that happened was in 1972.
742. Wondering what astrological sign people born New Year's Day have? It's Capricorn.
740. The Y2K problem exists because many older computers and software programs recognize only the last two digits of the year and could mistakenly interpret "00" as 1900.
739. Remember what your mother said about clean underwear? Do a week's worth of laundry, so if power goes out, you've got plenty of clean clothes to wear.
738. Make extra ice.
737. Learn how to use that weather radio your in-laws gave you for Christmas seven years ago.
736. When it comes to what to drive in the new year, two cars come to mind: The Mazda Millenia and the Buick Century 2000, a special edition of the Buick Century.
734. According to the Adams Wine Handbook, Washington, D.C., was the No.1 consumer of champagne and sparkling wine in the United States for 1997, drinking 0.602 gallons per capita. Illinois was a distant second at 0.376 gallons, and Florida ranked 10th at 0.201.
735. For $12,000 you can book a trip that takes you across the international date line from one millennium into the next, at the rocky, remote island of Little Diomede, surrounded by the icy Bering Strait, between Russia and Alaska.
733. There are two basic methods of making champagne: "Methode champenoise," in which the wine is fermented in the bottle itself to make it bubbly; and the cheaper "charmat" process, in which the bubbles are made in a large tank. Discriminating drinkers choose the first process every time.

Third millennium forecast: unsettled: Strange weather in recent years prompts pre-2000 scientific debate: [Final Edition]

Author: Bueckert, Dennis

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/third-millennium-forecast-unsettled-strange/docview/349225816/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area. Using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to? Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Third+millennium+forecast%3A+unsettled%3A+Strange+weather+in+recent+years+prompts+pre-2000+scientific+debate&amp;author=Bueckert%2C+Dennis&amp;issn=08373434&amp;title=The+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-26&amp;spage=D.11&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Looking Back on a Century
Gordon Toole remembers the day it was so cold that spit froze before it hit the ground.
Toole was one of about 20 men working at a military airport at Snag, Yukon, when the temperature plunged to the deepest low ever recorded in North America.
"You can't breathe through your nostrils in air that temperature, it's almost as though they're blocked or frozen," he says from Snag, about 320 kilometres northwest of Yellowknife. "You have to breath through your mouth but they're shallow breaths.
"You cannot take deep breaths because they just catch in your throat, you're gasping."
The temperature was so low it couldn't be recorded on a mercury thermometer. Even in an alcohol thermometer, the liquid sank below the lowest temperature mark, settling into the bulb at the bottom.
They marked the point with a small file -- a pen wouldn't work -- and sent the thermometer to Toronto. Days later the reading was confirmed: - 62.2C.
The record set at Snag on Feb. 3, 1947, still stands. For Canadians who seemingly never get tired of talking about the weather, it's a classic tale, especially since there were no casualties and no real damage.
Recent years have seen many weather extremes that were not so benign.
Few residents of Eastern Canada have forgotten the ice storm in 1998, when millions shivered in darkened houses or sought refuge in crowded public shelters.
Few residents of Manitoba have forgotten the flood of 1997, when the Red River turned into a vast sea, driving some 28,000 people from their homes and submerging whole towns.
The Saguenay Flood of 1996 caused massive damage, as did the Calgary hail storm of 1997. Unpredictable Prairie weather has helped reduce Canada's breadbasket to a basket case as the year 2000 nears.
The Insurance Bureau of Canada estimates that insurance payments for extreme weather events across the country have been doubling every five to 10 years. Payouts last year reached $1.5 billion.
"The weather in Canada has been frightful," says Paul Kovaks, a spokesman for the bureau.
Munich Re of Munich, Germany, one of the world's biggest reinsurance companies, estimates global losses from weather-related natural disasters have increased to $90 billion annually from $7 billion to $8 billion annually in the 1980s.
Part of the rising toll results from population growth -- there's simply more property to be damaged, and more of it is built in risky areas like flood plains and coastlines.
Paradoxically, modern technology can also increase vulnerability to extreme weather since vital energy and communications systems across large areas are intertwined.
"The disaster is the event plus our vulnerability," says federal climatologist Roger Street. "Higher dependence on networked energy, higher dependence on telecommunications, makes us more vulnerable."
The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area.
Climatologist David Phillips says in his recent book Blame it on the Weather that El Nino has become the scapegoat for everything from whale beachings in China to increased crime in Saskatoon.
But using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to?
Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.
Is El Nino a messenger for something much bigger?
Few credible scientists dispute that the climate is warming: the finding has been confirmed by countless studies. There has been great debate about whether the trend is due to natural variability or human influence. Now that debate also seems to be settled.
Over the past century, the average temperature of earth has risen by close to one degree Celsius. Now the big controversy concerns how fast future warming will occur and how it will affect different regions.
Much of the research is done with computer models simulating the atmosphere -- programs so complex that they push existing hardware to its limits. The models have many warts but they're getting better.
"There's some uncertainty, but I feel much safer, much more confident making a weather forecast for the year 2090 than I do an economic or a demographic or a political kind of forecast," says Phillips of the federal Environment Department's atmospheric environment service.
For example, the models show a balmy future for Toronto: "What Kentucky has now is what in fact Toronto will inherit in terms of its climate probably a hundred years from now.
"We've warmed up Toronto by maybe 0.6 of a degree and now we're talking about warming in the summertime of four to five degrees, five to nine degrees in the winter. There's going to be really a leap forward in terms of the climate."
Canadians may welcome the prospect of milder winters, but what about the prospect of more frequent and severe droughts, floods and storms?
"It is extreme events that will be the biggest impact on Canadians from climate change," says Gordon McBean, assistant deputy to the federal environment minister.
At the 1997 Kyoto climate conference, Canada pledged to reduce its greenhouse emissions by six per cent from 1990 levels by 2008- 2012. But since then, emissions have continued to rise and there is still no plan for reversing the trend.
Cutting emissions would require major changes in the way Canadians use energy, and would have an impact on virtually every industry. Disruptions in the near term might be high.
"The cost of action to reduce the risk is today, and the cost of inaction is in the future and hence there's a tendency for discounting the costs of inaction," says Henry Hengeveld, a senior adviser on climate change in the Environment Department.
The fossil fuel industry and the Alberta government have denounced the Kyoto commitment as unrealistic, and skeptics continue to find platforms for their views.
Hengeveld, for one, is not discouraged. He expects that the weather itself will settle the quarrel.
"If we are right in our science, and I think we are, nature will remind us from time to time. One of the things that got this on the agenda in 1988 was the extremely hot, dry summer we had that year. I think this is going to happen from time to time again."

The Speech from the Throne: [2 Edition]

Author: HARDIE BOYS, Michael

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/speech-throne/docview/315117926/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: My Government is committed to putting New Zealand Superannuation on a sound and fairbasis for the long term. The level of payment in relation to other incomes will berestored to that agreed in the multi- party accord in 1993. There will be discussionson moving to a dedicated tax and fund basis for New Zealand Superannuation. Measuresto improve private savings will also be considered, including reducing compliancecosts and providing an alternative tax regime to encourage superannuation savings. My Government will replace the Employment Contracts Act with fairer and more balancedemployment relations legislation. This legislation will preserve the freedom to choosewhether or not to join a union. It will mandate good faith bargaining. The minimumcode will be reviewed and strengthened. Greater enforcement will be undertaken ofthe provisions of the minimum code to ensure they are not flouted. Discussions willtake place in order to institute a new system of paid parental leave. Getting a good start in life is crucial to later success. My Government will expandearly intervention programmes. The first step will be to fund the Plunket Line serviceon a 24-hour-a-day, seven- days-a-week basis. Increased assistance will be made availableto the early childhood education sector. My Government will require teacher registrationand ongoing professional development in this sector. Eventually, the Government willneed to turn its attention to the question of pay parity for kindergarten teachers.Kindergartens will also be returned to the state sector.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Speech+from+the+Throne&amp;author=HARDIE+BOYS%2C+Michael&amp;issn=0113941X&amp;title=Dominion&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-22&amp;spage=11&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   With the opening of Parliament yesterday, Governor-General Sir Michael Hardie Boysdelivered the speech outlining the Government's programme for the next three years.This is an edited version of that speech.
--------------------
`Getting a good start is crucial. The first step will be to fund the Plunket Lineservice on a 24-hour-a-day, seven-days-a-week basis'.
--------------------
NEW ZEALAND remains over-dependent on the production and exporting of commodities.At the same time, New Zealand's skills production in those areas relevant to thenew knowledge-based industries has been inadequate.
My Government is determined to address these structural failings in order to improvereal incomes and provide the means to restore our social services to being amongthe best in the world.
Overall responsibility for the delivery of a new business development programme willbe with a new organisation, Industry New Zealand. The minister for economic developmentwill be moving quickly to set up Industry New Zealand.
Industry New Zealand will have the role of providing policy advice on strategic economicdevelopment issues as well as programmes in enterprise financing, local economicdevelopment, innovation, workplace productivity, ecological sustainability and procurementpolicy.
My Government will provide new assistance for exporters. New Zealand needs to seegreater reciprocity from some of our trading partners on tariff reductions, and amore considered and balanced approach to tariff reduction will be taken.
My Government is committed to putting New Zealand Superannuation on a sound and fairbasis for the long term. The level of payment in relation to other incomes will berestored to that agreed in the multi- party accord in 1993. There will be discussionson moving to a dedicated tax and fund basis for New Zealand Superannuation. Measuresto improve private savings will also be considered, including reducing compliancecosts and providing an alternative tax regime to encourage superannuation savings.
A broad structural review of the taxation system will be undertaken. The review willtake into account the promotion of sustainable growth and increased equity. An independentreview will also be undertaken of the operation of monetary policy since the passingof the Reserve Bank Act in 1989.
My Government will replace the Employment Contracts Act with fairer and more balancedemployment relations legislation. This legislation will preserve the freedom to choosewhether or not to join a union. It will mandate good faith bargaining. The minimumcode will be reviewed and strengthened. Greater enforcement will be undertaken ofthe provisions of the minimum code to ensure they are not flouted. Discussions willtake place in order to institute a new system of paid parental leave.
Getting a good start in life is crucial to later success. My Government will expandearly intervention programmes. The first step will be to fund the Plunket Line serviceon a 24-hour-a-day, seven- days-a-week basis. Increased assistance will be made availableto the early childhood education sector. My Government will require teacher registrationand ongoing professional development in this sector. Eventually, the Government willneed to turn its attention to the question of pay parity for kindergarten teachers.Kindergartens will also be returned to the state sector.
In the compulsory education sector, bulk funding will end and funding budgeted forit will be redistributed to all schools on a fair basis. Homework centres will betrialled to boost educational achievement. The training and professional developmentof teachers will be improved. Problems with the provision of special education serviceswill be tackled. Incentives will be provided to attract staff to rural schools.
In post-compulsory education a new Apprenticeship Act will be introduced. Amendedindustrial training legislation will be passed to encourage continuous upskillingof the workforce. The present competitive model in tertiary education will be abandoned,and a tertiary education commission will be set up to begin rebuilding a collaborativeand cooperative tertiary sector. Interest will not be charged on student loans forfulltime and other low-income students while they study. It is my Government's intentionto fund tertiary education providers at a level sufficient to enable fees to be reducedover time.
Research funding will be increased. Business investment in research, particularlyby small and medium-sized firms, will be encouraged through the establishment ofa small-business research fund.
The most important need is to boost research in science and technology. A high levelScience and Innovation Advisory Council will be established to report to the primeminister to ensure that this occurs.
My Government will change the taxation regime to ensure that it is more favourablein its treatment of research and development costs. This will be accompanied by areview of the depreciation regime to encourage investment in new plant and equipment.
A royal commission into genetic modification will be established. Until it has reported,a moratorium will be imposed on the commercial planting of genetically modified crops.A simple and comprehensive system of labelling genetically modified food will berequired.
The proposed amendments to the Resource Management Act at present before the Housewill be revisited. Amendments which weaken the act will be dropped. Those that donot will proceed. Issues of cost, delay and unevenness in implementation of the actwill be addressed.
By 2010, all waste management shall be on a full cost recovery basis and all existinglandfills will be upgraded or closed. It is my Government's objective to reduce significantlythe waste stream.
Measures that will be taken to control greenhouse gases will include an inquiry intothe impact of climate change and ozone depletion, increased public education programmes,greater investment in public transport and stronger measures on energy efficiency.
My Government will nurture much stronger music, publishing and film industries throughassistance with financing investment, export development and promotion. Format-specificquotas will be introduced for local content on radio and free-to-air television.The National Library and the National Archives will be strengthened. Copyright protectionwill be enhanced. Parallel importing of CDs, videos, films, books and software willbe prohibited for up to two years after first release.
A New Zealand Music Commission will be established to encourage the development ofthe music industry. New initiatives will provide better support for established artists,community arts, and emerging artists. The aim is to expand job opportunities andwealth creation based on the arts as well as to promote New Zealand's identity. RadioNew Zealand and Television New Zealand will be maintained in public ownership.
My Government will not have a state asset sales programme, and Treasury has beeninstructed to cease all work on options for implementing such a programme.
It will be made clear to the boards of state-owned enterprises and other commercialorganisations that their job is not to prepare assets for sale but to enhance theirvalue and ability to contribute to economic and social growth and development asstate-owned entities.
My Government strongly backs public funding and public ownership of core social servicesand will not proceed with the previous government's proposals to commercialise roading.Legislation will be introduced to abolish the private insurance market for statutoryaccident compensation provision. Legislation will also provide for improvements toaccident compensation, including a greater emphasis on rehabilitation, and the reintroductionof entitlements to lump sum compensation.
In the health system, my Government wants to improve the health of New Zealanders,cut waiting times for elective surgery and put in place a coordinated mental healthstrategy.
Within the next three months, the government will finalise a timetable with the MentalHealth Commission for implementation of improved mental health services. Urgent workwill be undertaken to ensure that there are adequate housing and employment strategiesfor people with mental health disabilities.
My Government will increase funding for elective surgery. It is our aim to establishmaximum waiting times for treatment.
The Health Funding Authority will be abolished. Majority-elected boards will overseethe provision of the public health services. The emphasis will be changed to oneof cooperation, not competition, in the provision of health services. My Governmentwill place special emphasis on the health needs of women, older people, rural dwellersand Maoris.
My Government will restore income-related rents for state housing. Low-income statetenants will pay no more than 25 per cent of their income in rent. Social allocationof state housing based on need will be reintroduced and the state house buildingand acquisition programme will be expanded.
Special Housing Action Zones will be established in areas with particularly serioushousing needs where the land is predominantly in multiple ownership in order to increasethe supply and quality of housing.
My Government wishes to encourage home ownership. It will investigate ways of bridgingthe deposit gap so as to assist modest- income families into their first home. TheLow Deposit Rural Lending Scheme will be further developed. Self- build and sweatequity schemes will be supported.
My Government will urgently review aspects of the benefit system, such as abatementlevels, to ensure there are not disincentives for beneficiaries to re-enter the workforce.
My Government will institute an inquiry into the administration of Work and IncomeNew Zealand. In the meantime, a clear signal has been sent that there has to be anend to the culture of extravagance wherever it has developed in government agencies.
Individual needs for job-seekers will be identified through new programmes whichwill ensure quality case management. It is the intention to ensure that no youngperson leaves school to go on the dole. The work capacity assessment pilot will bedropped. A sickness benefit will be re-established as a non-work tested payment.
Burglary and youth crime will be targeted. My Government will provide legal recognitionfor victims' rights, including the right to be heard and to be informed. Court supportfor victims will be improved.
Early intervention programmes will help children to get off the path to crime. Everyschool will be required to enforce an adequate truancy policy.
Bail provisions will be reformed. Hard-core offenders charged with serious crimeswho have a record of breaching bail will have to show why they should get bail. MyGovernment will promote alternative sentences for offenders based on restorativejustice.
New Zealand's security in the world requires the most effective use of our diplomacyand limited capacity to support international peacemaking and peacekeeping. My Governmentsees New Zealand's role as being primarily to assist the United Nations in the latterendeavours.
A third Anzac frigate will not be purchased. The recent contract to acquire 28 F16swill be reviewed to determine whether it can be cancelled.
My Government is conscious that the ambitious programme it has announced will notbe easy to achieve. It is committed to maintaining tight fiscal discipline and hasbegun with itself.
--------------------

Industries strike deal over climate change levy ENVIRONMENT COMPROMISE AGREED: [London edition]

Author: Brown, Kevin

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/industries-strike-deal-over-climate-change-levy/docview/248803343/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The 10 most energy-intensive industrial sectors yesterday accepted an 80 per cent discount on the government's proposed climate change levy, in return for cuts of 2.5m tonnes of carbon pollution over the next 10years. The deal, struck after months of negotiations with John Prescott's environment department, gives industry a smaller tax break than the 90 per cent or more sought by industry lobby groups. The chancellor also reduced the impact on business by cutting the levy by a third to 0.15p per kWh from 0.21p and exempting renewable energy and some environmentally-friendly combined heat and power systems.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Industries+strike+deal+over+climate+change+levy+ENVIRONMENT+COMPROMISE+AGREED%3A&amp;author=Brown%2C+Kevin&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-22&amp;spage=03&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The 10 most energy-intensive industrial sectors yesterday accepted an 80 per cent discount on the government's proposed climate change levy, in return for cuts of 2.5m tonnes of carbon pollution over the next 10years.
The deal, struck after months of negotiations with John Prescott's environment department, gives industry a smaller tax break than the 90 per cent or more sought by industry lobby groups.
However, it offers significantly greater savings than the 50 per cent discount offered when the tax was proposed last year as a key element of the government's international commitments on pollution reduction.
The chancellor also reduced the impact on business by cutting the levy by a third to 0.15p per kWh from 0.21p and exempting renewable energy and some environmentally-friendly combined heat and power systems.
Michael Meacher, the environment minister, said efficiency targets could be met through pollution trading when a workable scheme had been developed. He said the deal would make an important contribution to the UK's climate change programme.
However, industry and environmentalists said the deal was flawed. "It will not be easy, and substantial investment will be required to enable us to reach our target," said James Daglish, president of the paper federation.
Judith Hackitt, director of business and the environment at the Chemical Industries Association, said the deal would cut the impact on the sector to £10m a year from £75m, but the impact would vary considerably between companies.
Michael Roberts, head of industrial policy at the Confederation of British Industry, said several sectors would remain losers, which would damage their international competitiveness.
"No one is going to say that this is a complete victory, but it is better than it was," he said.
The deal is subject to agreement on technical details in the New Year. The government is also consulting on how to decide which energy efficiency investments should be eligible for capital allowances, and which CHP schemes should be exempt.
Friends of the Earth said the government's decision to negotiate the deal with the trade associations representing industry sectors rather than with individual companies would make it very difficult to police.
"We are concerned that the government may have gone for a soft option purely to get round the short-term problem of a lot of whinging from the industry lobbies," the environmental campaigning group said.
"The sectors are going to have to police this, and the history of voluntary agreements in this country is not encouraging."
The levy, which takes effect from April, will be paid by all businesses. But the biggest impact will be on manufacturers, in part because the £1bn it will raise will finance a cut in employers' national insurance contributions, which will mainly benefit the service sector.
The 10 industry sectors that have reached agreement with the government are: cement, glass, food and drink, non-ferrous metals, aluminium, paper, chemicals, foundries, steel and ceramics. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

City briefing: Discount for heavy industry

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/city-briefing-discount-heavy-industry/docview/245454265/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Britain's 10 biggest energy-using sectors yesterday agreed to cut carbon emissions by 2.5m tonnes a year by 2010 in return for an 80% discount in the climate change levy.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=City+briefing%3A+Discount+for+heavy+industry&amp;author=&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-22&amp;spage=20&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Britain's 10 biggest energy-using sectors yesterday agreed to cut carbon emissions by 2.5m tonnes a year by 2010 in return for an 80% discount in the climate change levy.
The agreement, due to be finalised next year, follows a commitment by the chancellor, Gordon Brown, in his pre-budget statement last month to ease burden of the levy on heavy industry.
The levy, designed to help Britain meet its Kyoto target of reducing greenhouse gases by 20% by 2010, will raise pounds 1bn and be offset by a 0.3% cut in employers' national insurance contributions.
The 10 sectors - cement, food and drink, glass, non-ferrous metals, aluminium, paper, chemicals, foundries, steel and ceramics - account for more than half of energy used by manufacturing. The government hopes the levy will cut emissions by 4m tonnes overall.

____________________________________________________________

Hell and high water The UN marked out the 1990s as a decade for natural disaster reduction. But this year has been one of the worst on record for environmental tragedies. And as the death toll rises after this week's floods in Venezuela, experts are predicting far worse to come. Tim Radford reports

Author: Radford, Tim

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hell-high-water-un-marked-out-1990s-as-decade/docview/245454217/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Here is how to become a disaster statistic. Move to a shanty town on an unstable hillside near a tropical coast. Crowd together as more and more people arrive. Wait for the world to get a little warmer. More evaporation means more rain, which means the slopes will get progressively more waterlogged. One day, the land will turn to mud, and the neighbourhood will begin to go downhill. Literally. `One, your house has been washed away; second, the land that you farmed has disappeared; and third, the mud and the debris have spread across other bits of land you might have been able to farm, so it's now useless.' He remembers some of the bedrock facts of survival after hurricane Mitch hit central America in 1998, dumped metres of water on the steep hillsides, and started landslides in which more than 10,000 perished. `We were talking about three metres of carcasses, debris, sand, just unuseable land. They are having to rebuild from scratch and the next time a disaster hits, they have so much less to work with. That's the real threat.' The catastrophe of Caracas came in the very last days of a decade marked out by the United Nations as an international decade for natural disaster reduction, IDNDR for short. The theory behind the decade was that as people were made aware of hazard, so they would prepare themselves to survive it. For a bitter cocktail of reasons, IDNDR didn't work out like that. During the 90s, the population of the world grew at the rate of 10,000 people an hour: 240,000 potential new victims every day, nearly 90 million a year, almost a billion in the decade. Most of the population growth was in the developing world, where people desperate for work moved to the cities, and then found homes on marginal soils, crowded into substandard buildings. Politically, too, life moved on. The idea that governments have an obligation to consider the welfare of all their citizens has been progressively weakened throughout the decade.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Hell+and+high+water+The+UN+marked+out+the+1990s+as+a+decade+for+natural+disaster+reduction.+But+this+year+has+been+one+of+the+worst+on+record+for+environmental+tragedies.+And+as+the+death+toll+rises+after+this+week%27s+floods+in+Venezuela%2C+experts+are+predicting+far+worse+to+come.+Tim+Radford+reports&amp;author=Radford%2C+Tim&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-22&amp;spage=2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Here is how to become a disaster statistic. Move to a shanty town on an unstable hillside near a tropical coast. Crowd together as more and more people arrive. Wait for the world to get a little warmer. More evaporation means more rain, which means the slopes will get progressively more waterlogged. One day, the land will turn to mud, and the neighbourhood will begin to go downhill. Literally.
If the slope is steep enough, a landslide can accelerate to more than 200 miles an hour. Nothing can withstand it. What roads there may have been are gone. Clinics and health services will have been erased. Rescuers will not be able to get in, survivors will have trouble getting out. There will be no food, no clean water supply. The uncounted dead will remain in the shaking soil beneath your feet. Epidemics of cholera and fever will follow. The survivors will be so weakened as to be most likely counted among the dead the next time.
It has just happened in Venezuela, and it will happen again somewhere else very soon. Peter Walker, director of disaster policy for the international federation of Red Cross and Red Crescent societies in Geneva, has seen it too often.
`One, your house has been washed away; second, the land that you farmed has disappeared; and third, the mud and the debris have spread across other bits of land you might have been able to farm, so it's now useless.' He remembers some of the bedrock facts of survival after hurricane Mitch hit central America in 1998, dumped metres of water on the steep hillsides, and started landslides in which more than 10,000 perished. `We were talking about three metres of carcasses, debris, sand, just unuseable land. They are having to rebuild from scratch and the next time a disaster hits, they have so much less to work with. That's the real threat.'
The catastrophe of Caracas came in the very last days of a decade marked out by the United Nations as an international decade for natural disaster reduction, IDNDR for short. The theory behind the decade was that as people were made aware of hazard, so they would prepare themselves to survive it. For a bitter cocktail of reasons, IDNDR didn't work out like that. During the 90s, the population of the world grew at the rate of 10,000 people an hour: 240,000 potential new victims every day, nearly 90 million a year, almost a billion in the decade. Most of the population growth was in the developing world, where people desperate for work moved to the cities, and then found homes on marginal soils, crowded into substandard buildings. Politically, too, life moved on. The idea that governments have an obligation to consider the welfare of all their citizens has been progressively weakened throughout the decade.
On top of all that, add climate change and the spectre of global warming. Seven of the 10 warmest years have occurred in the 90s. The other three were all in the late 80s. A hotter ocean breeds fiercer cyclones and hurricanes. It surrenders greater quantities of water as evaporation, and more powerful winds dump this water against mountainsides with increasing fury. Last year the warmest on record was the worst year ever for climate-related disasters. According to Red Cross calculations, 57,513 people died and 335 million people lost their homes, or their livelihoods, or their crops, or their livestock, or their children, or their hopes. The economic bill not totted-up household losses, just the damage that could be estimated from satellite imagery was put at more than $50bn.
`Maybe it is the decade of heightening awareness to the fact that natural disasters actually kill far many more people than wars do. The figures for 1998 make appalling reading,'says Walker. This year, it will be worse. `We have appealed for more funding this year than any year in our history. Part of that is Kosovo rebuilding. But in terms of natural disasters, particularly those associated with rainfall and cyclones, it has been one of the worst on record.'
There are floods now in Vietnam and Yemen. There has been flooding in the Congo. People in Vanuatu were swept away by a tsunami last month. A catastrophic cyclone piled mountains of water onto the coasts of Orissa, India, in October. Three hurricanes in succession hit the eastern seaboard of the US. Disaster professionals have totted up more than 75 cases of major natural disaster floods, drought, windstorm, earthquake, avalanche, volcanic eruption and forest fire since the year began. Sometimes these hit the victims of another disaster the year before. Central America, for instance, has had some of the worst floods ever this year, as well as last.
`Everybody asked the question: are these floods as big as Hurricane Mitch?,' says Walker. `Answer: no. OK then, let's move on to the next major story. But the effect at household and village level is far larger than it would have been if you had not had hurricane Mitch. It's a double whammy.'
By the time it ends, 1999 will look like a year for apocalyptic fantasies; a kind of rehearsal for the predicted arrival of four horsemen, swiftly on the heels of the midnight moment of the closing millennium, cantering to bring calamity to a climax. If only. All the betting from the disaster professionals is that things will get worse and go on getting worse. A burgeoning population will go on competing for unevenly shared resources in a world in which sea levels will rise and land available for agriculture will dwindle. At the same time as millions are made miserable by torrential rain and coastal flooding, many arid zones will become drier. This is already evident: the Yellow River, once notorious for flooding the Chinese landscape, failed to reach the sea at all on 226 days in 1997 and a number of nations are already in armed conflict over water.
Around half the world now lives in cities, and more than 500m people live within range of a volcanic eruption. There are more than 500 active volcanoes, and around 50 of those are in eruption each year. An even greater number live at risk, in some degree, from earthquakes. These have taken more than 1.6m lives this century in 1976 one earthquake in China killed 242,000 people and the shaking earth claimed new victims this year in China, Turkey, Mexico and Greece. In each region, survivors will go on paying the price for years and the poorest are hurt the most. A calamitous quake in Kobe, Japan, in 1995, killed 5,466 and made 300,000 homeless in $100bn worth of damage: it was the costliest natural catastrophe the world had ever seen. Many people had no insurance: some of the victims are still in temporary housing five years on, others are still paying off mortgages for homes that suddenly ceased to exist in a few nightmare seconds. The survivors in tents along the Anatolian faultline will be in temporary shelter for years to come, at the mercy of increasingly inhospitable skies.
That is because a warming world means a stormier world, as people in low-lying countries such as Bangladesh discover repeatedly, and as Caracas discovered, cruelly, this week. `There are 40% more intense Atlantic hurricanes now than there were 30 years ago,' says Professor Bill McGuire of the Benfield Greig hazards research centre at University College, London. `That's one effect of warming the sea. You are going to get more windstorms. You are going to get unprecedented amounts of rain in very short times.'
McGuire is a vulcanologist. He has been warning for years that the world hasn't seen the worst nature can do, not by a long way. Forget about Mt St Helens in Washington State, or Pinatubo in the Philippines, he says, the worst eruption in human history was probably Mt Tambora in 1815, in Indonesia. It pumped so much dust into the stratosphere that it effectively cancelled the following summer in Europe and America, triggering famine and in part, at least, a worldwide cholera epidemic.
But geological evidence shows that 73,000 years ago there was a super-eruption that caused the kind of global winter you might expect only after allout thermonuclear war. `It reduced temperatures by maybe 6C in some places and plunged the whole planet into winter for at least a few years. It was devastating, there was no growing season. There are about two of these events every 100,000 years and the last one was 73,000 years ago.'
There are 30,000 earthquakes a year, most either relatively mild, or in uninhabited areas. Disaster experts estimate an average of one major disaster each year. Some of the nations at greatest risk the western United States, Japan, New Zealand have educated populations and carefully monitor building standards. But the lesson is that even prepared countries are not prepared enough, and a big earthquake in a rich nation could still trigger effects that would ripple round the world. Even if a disaster doesn't cost lives, it still costs money. With worldwide economic growth, more is always at stake. The insurance losses last year were more than the total for the entire 60s.
`The big one everyone is waiting for is Tokyo. It will come, and that will result in global economic chaos, and it will kill a hell of a lot of people as well,' says McGuire. `There are still a million wooden buildings in the Tokyo region. The gross domestic product of the area is greater than that of Britain, and if that is wiped out, the Japanese will have to bring all their monetary resources abroad, and that will devastate the global economy. There are lots of things worse than the Venezuela tragedy coming up.'

Strange weather prompts pre-2000 scientific debate: [Final Edition]

Author: Bueckert, Dennis

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/strange-weather-prompts-pre-2000-scientific/docview/275681259/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area. Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995. There are reasons to think that global warming increases both the frequency and intensity of El Nino events, Kevin Trenberth, chief of climate analysis at the National Center for Atmospheric Research at Boulder, Colo., recently told a scientific journal.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Strange+weather+prompts+pre-2000+scientific+debate&amp;author=Bueckert%2C+Dennis&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Record&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-20&amp;spage=A.04&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Note
Gordon Toole remembers the day it was so cold that spit froze before it hit the ground.
Toole was one of about 20 men working at a military airport at Snag, Yukon, when the temperature plunged to the deepest low ever recorded in North America.
"You can't breathe through your nostrils in air that temperature, it's almost as though they're blocked or frozen," he says from Snag, about 320 kilometres northwest of Yellowknife. "You have to breathe through your mouth but they're shallow breaths.
"You cannot take deep breaths because they just catch in your throat, you're gasping."
Toole recalls the crystal clarity of sounds. He could distinctly hear the barking of dogs in a village some five kilometres away. An airplane overhead was deafening.
The temperature was so low it couldn't be recorded on a mercury thermometer. Even in an alcohol thermometer, the liquid sank below the lowest temperature mark, settling into the bulb at the bottom.
They marked the point with a small file -- a pen wouldn't work -- and sent the thermometer to Toronto. Days later the reading was confirmed by experts: -62.2C.
The record set at Snag on Feb. 3, 1947, still stands. For Canadians who seemingly never get tired of talking about the weather, it's a classic tale, especially since there were no casualties and no real damage.
Recent years have seen many weather extremes that were not so benign.
Weather extremes
Few residents of Eastern Canada have forgotten the ice storm in 1998, when millions shivered in darkened houses or sought refuge in crowded public shelters.
Few residents of Manitoba have forgotten the flood of 1997, when the Red River turned into a vast sea, driving some 28,000 people from their homes and submerging whole towns.
The Saguenay Flood of 1996 caused massive damage, as did the Calgary hail storm of 1997. The current drought in the Prairies has helped to reduce Canada's breadbasket to a basket case as the year 2000 nears.
The Insurance Bureau of Canada estimates that insurance payments for extreme weather events across the country have been doubling every five to 10 years. Payouts last year reached $1.5 billion.
"The weather in Canada has been frightful," says Paul Kovaks, a spokesman for the bureau.
Munich Re of Munich, Germany, one of the world's biggest reinsurance companies, estimates global losses from weather-related natural disasters have increased to $90 billion annually from $7 billion to $8 billion annually in the 1980s.
Part of the rising toll results from population growth -- there's simply more property to be damaged, and more of it is built in risky areas like flood plains and coastlines.
Paradoxically, modern technology can also increase vulnerability to extreme weather since vital energy and communications systems across large areas are intertwined.
"The disaster is the event plus our vulnerability," says federal climatologist Roger Street. "Higher dependence on networked energy, higher dependence on telecommunications, makes us more vulnerable."
Climatologists would like to come up with an extreme-weather index so they could measure the apparent rise in instability. One of the problems is they can't agree on how to define an extreme weather event.
The great ice storm, for example, did not involve record-setting precipitation. It resulted from multiple factors, none of which would in itself be ranked as highly unusual; it was the combination that was unusual.
The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area.
El Nino effect
Climatologist David Phillips says in his recent book Blame it on the Weather that El Nino has become the scapegoat for everything from whale beachings in China to increased crime in Saskatoon.
But using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to?
Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.
Is El Nino a messenger for something much bigger?
There are reasons to think that global warming increases both the frequency and intensity of El Nino events, Kevin Trenberth, chief of climate analysis at the National Center for Atmospheric Research at Boulder, Colo., recently told a scientific journal.
"Because El Nino is involved with the movement of heat around, it is conceptually easy to see how increased heating from the buildup of greenhouse gases can interfere."
Some environmentalists are more forceful in their assessment.
"Warming is destabilizing the climate that has supported human civilization for thousands of years," says Europe-based Earthaction.
"The deep oceans are warming, fracturing Antarctic ice shelves and disrupting rainfall patterns. Glaciers are melting. Oceans are rising. Tropical diseases are spreading."
Climate warming
Few credible scientists dispute that the climate is warming: the finding has been confirmed by countless studies. There has been great debate about whether the trend is due to natural variability or human influence. Now that debate also seems to be settled.
"The balance of evidence suggests a discernible human influence on global climate," says the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, drawing on the expertise of hundreds of scientists around the world.
In the words of Environment Minister David Anderson in Parliament this year: "The best and most credible evidence we have before us suggests that climate change due to human activity is a reality."
Global warming is caused by the buildup of greenhouse gases which are transparent to incoming solar rays but opaque to those rays when they bounce back from the surface of Earth at a different wave length.
Over the past century the average temperature of earth has risen by close to one degree Celsius. Now the big controversy concerns how fast future warming will occur and how it will affect different regions.
Much of the research is done with computer models simulating the atmosphere -- programs so complex that they push existing hardware to its limits. The models have many warts but they're getting better.
Canadians may welcome the prospect of milder winters, but what about the prospect of more frequent and severe droughts, floods and storms?
"It is extreme events that will be the biggest impact on Canadians from climate change," says Gordon McBean, assistant deputy to the federal environment minister.
Research suggests the warming is relatively subtle so far: Night- time temperatures are warmer than they used to be while day time maximum temperatures haven't changed much.
Another way of putting it: Canada is not getting hotter, it is getting less cold.
"If you go through the seasons, the winter is actually warming more than the summer is warming," says McBean. "Season by season, we're having bigger warming overall in the wintertime, so that makes you less cold."
The less-welcome news, perhaps, is that rising temperatures mean higher evaporation, which in turn will lead to higher precipitation. The models suggest this precipitation is likely to occur in less frequent but bigger rainfall or snowfall events.
"We have seen enough warming that the alarm bells should at least be sounding," says McBean.
"If there was an asteroid of large size coming toward the Earth and we thought there was a 30 per cent chance of it hitting the Earth, there would be very strong action by governments to try and do something, shoot it down or whatever."
At the 1997 Kyoto climate conference, Canada pledged to reduce its greenhouse emissions by six per cent from 1990 levels by 2008- 2012. But since then, emissions have continued to rise, and there is still no plan for reversing the trend.
Cutting emissions would require major changes in the way Canadians use energy, and would have an impact on virtually every industry. Disruptions in the near term might be high.
"The cost of action to reduce the risk is today, and the cost of inaction is in the future and hence there's a tendency for discounting the costs of inaction," says Henry Hengeveld, a senior adviser on climate change in the Environment Department.
The fossil fuel industry and the Alberta government have denounced the Kyoto commitment as unrealistic, and skeptics continue to find platforms for their views.
Hengeveld, for one, is not discouraged. He expects that the weather itself will settle the quarrel.

Turnaround Decade began with a bang, ended with a whimper: [Final Edition]

Author: Cooperman, Jim

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/turnaround-decade-began-with-bang-ended-whimper/docview/358261812/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: At the beginning of the decade significant progress was made in B.C. on a number of fronts that the environmental community should take pride in: land-use planning began, a sustainability Act was drafted; a Forest Practices Code was legislated; timber supply reviews were initiated; and many new parks were created. On the pollution front, pulp mills began to clean up their act, air quality improved and accumulations of toxic chemicals in wildlife were reduced. Meanwhile, the turnaround continues within the environmental community. Thanks to substantial funding from Canadian and U.S. foundations and from individual donors, hundreds of activists are now working on projects. These activists work hand-in-hand with thousands of volunteers to better the environment and society. One organization alone, the Sierra Legal Defence Fund, has grown from a staff of four to 43 lawyers and support staff, including 10 in its Toronto office. The B.C. environmental community has become a force to reckon with as alliances are made with other sectors and the international marketplace pays increasingly higher attention to our concerns.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Turnaround+Decade+began+with+a+bang%2C+ended+with+a+whimper&amp;author=Cooperman%2C+Jim&amp;issn=1185491X&amp;title=Kamloops+Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-14&amp;spage=A.6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The 1990s began with high optimism and energy to turn around the destructive impacts of pollution and resource extraction. During what the Worldwatch Institute labelled the Turnaround Decade , activists across the world busted their butts to create change.
At the beginning of the decade significant progress was made in B.C. on a number of fronts that the environmental community should take pride in: land-use planning began, a sustainability Act was drafted; a Forest Practices Code was legislated; timber supply reviews were initiated; and many new parks were created. On the pollution front, pulp mills began to clean up their act, air quality improved and accumulations of toxic chemicals in wildlife were reduced.
For more than half of this decade, B.C. shimmered in glory as the environment took prominence in government decision-making.
But with the change in leadership, came a change in direction. Instead of sustainability and ecosystem protection, economic forces redirected decision-making to focus on labour and industrial interests. Mega-projects, government bailouts, cuts to the Environment Ministry budget, jobs and timber, and a business lens to oversee legislation became the order of the day.
Long approved land-use plans remain unimplemented as the government dithers over the wording of higher level objectives. Admittedly, parks continued to be created, but overall, a coat of brown paint quickly covered up the green tint.
Meanwhile, the turnaround continues within the environmental community. Thanks to substantial funding from Canadian and U.S. foundations and from individual donors, hundreds of activists are now working on projects. These activists work hand-in-hand with thousands of volunteers to better the environment and society. One organization alone, the Sierra Legal Defence Fund, has grown from a staff of four to 43 lawyers and support staff, including 10 in its Toronto office. The B.C. environmental community has become a force to reckon with as alliances are made with other sectors and the international marketplace pays increasingly higher attention to our concerns.
But these accomplishments pale beside the global impacts of environmental destruction. The impacts of climate change have moved from theory to reality as insurance claims skyrocket from storms, fires and floods. Media reports of Arctic ice sheets breaking up, tropical forests burning, sea life dying and massive wind storms spread gloom and doom throughout the world. And now, instead of trying to turn things around, we are trying to prevent further globalization from lowering environmental standards and further widening the gap between the rich and the poor.
The breakdown of the WTO talks in Seattle, although seemingly a victory, is but a stopgap measure as powerful transnational corporations continue to profit at the expense of the health of the planet and its people. The irony is that with each new disaster, the GDP goes up instead of down, as more forests are felled and more people are employed to rebuild communities.
If all our hard work failed to turn things around in the last decade of the second millennium, then what should we call the first decade of the new millennium? As the planet continues to heat up and the oceans rise, perhaps the new decade should be called The Wet One. Or, if the alliance of people and groups to fight further globalization continues to build, we could call it the People Power decade. We can only hope that all people of the industrialized countries simplify their lifestyles to lessen over-consumption so as to finally help turn things around.
Credit: Special to The Daily News

FORD'S SENSE COMPANY TAKES LEAD WITH SMART STEPS TO CLEAR AIR

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/fords-sense-company-takes-lead-with-smart-steps/docview/436222900/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The automaker, to its credit, has just quit the Global Climate Coalition, a group of businesses whose stance on the rise in greenhouse gases can be roughly summarized as, "What, me worry?" The GCC is much more fearful of environmental regulators than of global heat-up, and it has spent a lot of money trying to persuade people that global warming is a figment of someone's fevered imagination.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=FORD%27S+SENSE+COMPANY+TAKES+LEAD+WITH+SMART+STEPS+TO+CLEAR+AIR&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Detroit+Free+Press&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-13&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Ford Motor Co. has become the latest in a line of corporate converts on the
subject of global warming. The automaker, to its credit, has just quit the
Global Climate Coalition, a group of businesses whose stance on the rise in
greenhouse gases can be roughly summarized as, "What, me worry?"
The GCC is much more fearful of environmental regulators than of global
heat-up, and it has spent a lot of money trying to persuade people that global
warming is a figment of someone's fevered imagination. It's true the issue is
full of unknowns. There's no doubt about the buildup of greenhouse gases. What
happens as a result of that buildup, though, is not easily predictable.
Still, many businesses have decided to factor the rise in the global
thermometer into their plans. Insurance companies are a good example. When
they started thinking about their liability, if melting polar ice or
mega-hurricanes send the Atlantic Ocean washing over Miami or Manhattan, they
became intensely interested in the consequences of global warming.
The prudent policy is to begin with actions that are beneficial whether or not
the worst fears about climate change come true. It looks as if that's what
Ford is doing. Its low-emission trucks and high-mileage hybrids will emit
fewer greenhouse gases. But if global warming fizzles, the new vehicles will
still provide cleaner air and lower fuel costs.
And Ford will be one jump ahead of the regulators all the way. That doesn't
sound like bunny-hugging foolishness. It sounds like good business and
responsible behavior that more firms should follow.
ILLUSTRATION: Drawing MARGARET SCOTT/Special to the Free Press

Liberal caucus priorities: roads, taxes: Convention in March: [National Edition]

Author: Mike Blanchfield and Joan Bryden

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/liberal-caucus-priorities-roads-taxes-convention/docview/329584772/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: OTTAWA - The Liberal caucus of MPs has voted to give priority to discussions about tax cuts rather than the prime minister's children's agenda. The caucus agreed to 10 resolutions that will be considered by delegates at the Liberal's biennial convention in March. In addition to support for an improved infrastructure program that will encourage greater spending on road improvement schemes, the MPs signalled their intent to push for broad tax cuts for both businesses and individual Canadians. Paul DeVillers, Liberal caucus chair, said he was a "little bit" surprised that a national highway program emerged as the priority for caucus discussions, followed by the call for tax reductions. Although the roads issue has been raised several times in recent years, it has not received the provincial support necessary to get the roads paved.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Liberal+caucus+priorities%3A+roads%2C+taxes%3A+Convention+in+March&amp;author=Mike+Blanchfield+and+Joan+Bryden&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-07&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: See related story "Why polls don't tell the whole truth on tax cuts" on page C7.
OTTAWA - The Liberal caucus of MPs has voted to give priority to discussions about tax cuts rather than the prime minister's children's agenda.
The caucus agreed to 10 resolutions that will be considered by delegates at the Liberal's biennial convention in March. In addition to support for an improved infrastructure program that will encourage greater spending on road improvement schemes, the MPs signalled their intent to push for broad tax cuts for both businesses and individual Canadians.
In an agreed resolution, the caucus notes, "Canadian taxpayers have strongly endorsed the concept of tax reduction" and "the general public understands the need for debt reduction in order to reduce the interest and debt-repayment burden facing future generations of taxpayers."
The resolution says the Liberal caucus strongly endorses "the concept of substantial and sustainable reductions to personal and business tax rates as well as a long-term debt reduction plan with achievable and measurable targets to be established."
The resolution also recognizes the need for the reduction of Canada's $577-billion debt. Recently, business leaders, among them John Roth, chief executive of Nortel Networks Corp., have urged the federal government to offer tax breaks to help emerging high-tech firms attract and keep talented employees.
In one of eight auxiliary resolutions that Liberal MPs also want debated next March is a proposal to raise the annual tax break for small business to $400,000 from $200,000, "thereby fostering job growth, reinvestment and research and development amongst Canada's small business sector."
Paul DeVillers, Liberal caucus chair, said he was a "little bit" surprised that a national highway program emerged as the priority for caucus discussions, followed by the call for tax reductions. Although the roads issue has been raised several times in recent years, it has not received the provincial support necessary to get the roads paved.
Mr. DeVillers said the emphasis on infrastructure funding and tax cuts should not be interpreted as a sign that Liberal MPs do not care about new spending on such items as the children's agenda. Nor, he said, are they straying from Jean Chretien's formula of devoting half of budget surpluses to new social and economic programs and the other half to debt and tax relief.
Mr. DeVillers said most MPs expect the children's agenda -- a $9- billion, five-year set of programs and spending, including increasing the annual child-tax benefit and extending maternity and parental benefits from the current maximum of six months to one full year -- to be the focus of February's budget, a month before the party convention, leaving little need for subsequent discussion.
"I think that's why it's conspicuous by its absence, because it's a given in the next budget," he said.
The leading auxiliary resolution calls for amendments to the Employment Insurance Act that would promote job creation and also make it easier to access EI.
And in an apparent attempt to address two growing financial crises on very different fronts, the caucus has put forth resolutions to aid farmers and the Canadian Armed Forces.
The MPs are urging Agricultural Supply Management Programs be kept in place "to ensure the protection and stability of our most strategic resources [namely agricultural production]." And they are calling for an increase in defence spending to address the greater role Canada plays on the international stage in NATO and United Nations peacekeeping missions.
The caucus also voted in favour of resolutions calling for discussions with the provinces on the implementation of pharmacare, a national strategy for freshwater resources, job creation and climate change.

Roads, tax cuts Grit priorities; Liberal MPs attempt to put a more right-leaning face on policy to be considered at party's convention: [Final Edition]

Author: Mike Blanchfield and Joan Bryden The Ottawa Citizen

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/roads-tax-cuts-grit-priorities-liberal-mps/docview/254331430/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In a move that seems to ignore calls for a children's agenda, Liberal MPs say their No. 2 priority is a resolution that calls for a tax break for "business" as well as individual Canadians. The resolution also recognizes the need for the reduction of Canada's $577-billion debt mountain. DeVillers said the emphasis on tax cuts should not be interpreted as a sign that Liberal MPs don't care about new spending on such items as the government's vaunted children's agenda. Nor, he says, are they straying from Jean Chretien's formula of devoting half of budget surpluses to new social and economic programs and the other half to debt and tax relief. Eight "auxiliary" resolutions were adopted and they will be debated in workshops during the party's convention. The top auxiliary resolution calls for amendments to the Employment Insurance Act that would promote job creation and make access to EI easier.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Roads%2C+tax+cuts+Grit+priorities%3B+Liberal+MPs+attempt+to+put+a+more+right-leaning+face+on+policy+to+be+considered+at+party%27s+convention&amp;author=Mike+Blanchfield+and+Joan+Bryden+The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-07&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Fixing roads and cutting personal and business taxes will be the top priorities of federal Liberal MPs at the party's convention this March.
The Liberal caucus, in a move that appears to try to put a more right-leaning stamp on government policy, voted Nov. 24 on 10 resolutions to be considered by delegates at the Liberal's biennial convention.
No. 1 on the list is a resolution urging the Liberal government to "call upon the Federal, Provincial and Municipal governments to work together toward the creation of another infrastructure program, paying specific attention to a National Highway Component."
But in a move that seems to ignore calls for a children's agenda, Liberal MPs say their No. 2 priority is a resolution that calls for a tax break for "business" as well as individual Canadians. The resolution also recognizes the need for the reduction of Canada's $577-billion debt mountain.
Recently, prominent business leaders, among them John Roth, CEO of Nortel, have urged the federal government to offer tax breaks to help emerging hi-tech firms attract and keep talented employees.
The resolution notes that "Canadian taxpayers have strongly endorsed the concept of tax reduction" and that "the general public understands the need for debt reduction in order to reduce the interest and debt repayment burden facing future generations of taxpayers."
The resolution says the Liberal caucus strongly endorses "the concept of substantial and sustainable reductions to personal and business tax rates as well as a long-term debt reduction plan with achievable and measurable targets to be established."
And one of eight auxiliary resolutions that Liberal MPs also want debated next March is a proposal to raise the annual tax break for small business from $200,000 to $400,000 "thereby fostering job growth, reinvestment and research and development amongst Canada's small business sector."
Liberal caucus chairman Paul De Villers said he was "a little bit" surprised that a national highway program emerged as the top priority for caucus.
Although the issue has been raised several times in recent years, it has not received the provincial support necessary to get the roads paved.
Though the recent throne speech highlighted the Liberals' new children's agenda, it is not the subject of any of the 10 caucus resolutions.
DeVillers said the emphasis on tax cuts should not be interpreted as a sign that Liberal MPs don't care about new spending on such items as the government's vaunted children's agenda. Nor, he says, are they straying from Jean Chretien's formula of devoting half of budget surpluses to new social and economic programs and the other half to debt and tax relief.
DeVillers said most MPs expect the children's agenda to be the focus of next February's budget, a month before the party convention.
Will be debated
Eight "auxiliary" resolutions were adopted and they will be debated in workshops during the party's convention. The top auxiliary resolution calls for amendments to the Employment Insurance Act that would promote job creation and make access to EI easier.
And in an apparent attempt to address two growing financial crises on very different fronts, the caucus has put forth resolutions to aid farmers and the Canadian Forces.
The MPs urge that Agricultural Supply Management Programs be kept in place "to ensure the protection and stability of our most strategic resources (agricultural production)."
And they call for an increase in defence spending to address the greater role Canada plays on the international stage in NATO and on United Nations peacekeeping missions.
The caucus also voted in favour of resolutions calling for discussions with the provinces on the implementation of pharmacare, a national strategy for freshwater resources, job creation and climate change.

Liberal MPs want party to slash taxes: Delegates to spring convention will be asked to endorse `substantial and sustainable' cuts: [Final Edition]

Full text: Fixing roads and cutting personal and business taxes will be the top priorities of Liberal MPs at the party's convention this March.
The Liberal caucus, in a move that appears to try to put a more right-leaning stamp on government policy, voted Nov. 24 on 10 resolutions to be considered by delegates at the Liberals' biennial convention.

But in a move that seems to ignore calls for a children's agenda, Liberal MPs say their No. 2 priority is a resolution that calls for a tax break for "business" as well as individual Canadians.
The resolution also recognizes the need for the reduction of Canada's $577-billion debt mountain.

The MPs' resolution notes that "Canadian taxpayers have strongly endorsed the concept of tax reduction" and that "the general public understands the need for debt reduction in order to reduce the interest and debt repayment burden facing future generations of taxpayers."
The resolution says the Liberal caucus strongly endorses "the concept of substantial and sustainable reductions to personal and business tax rates as well as a long term debt reduction plan with achievable and measurable targets to be established."
And in one of the eight auxiliary resolutions that Liberal MPs also want debated next March is a proposal to raise the annual tax break for small business from $200,000 to $400,000, "thereby fostering job growth, reinvestment and research and development amongst Canada's small business sector."

Nor, he says, are they straying from Jean Chretien's formula of devoting half of budget surpluses to new social and economic programs and the other half to debt and tax relief.
Mr. DeVillers said most MPs expect the children's agenda to be the focus of next February's budget, a month before the party convention.
"I think that's why it's conspicuous by its absence because it's a given in the next budget," he said.
Eight auxiliary resolutions will be debated in workshops during the party's convention. The top auxiliary resolution calls for amendments to the Employment Insurance Act that would promote job creation and also make it easier to access EI.
And in an apparent attempt to address two growing financial crises on very different fronts, the caucus has put forth resolutions to aid farmers and the Canadian Forces.
The MPs urge that Agricultural Supply Management Programs be kept in place "to ensure the protection and stability of our most strategic resources (namely agricultural production)."
And they call for an increase in defence spending to address the greater role Canada plays on the international stage in NATO and on United Nations peacekeeping missions.
The caucus also voted in favour of resolutions calling for discussions with the provinces on the implementation of pharmacare, a national strategy for freshwater resources, job creation and climate change.
