Insurance lessons: [National Edition]

Author: Kovacs, Paul

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurance-lessons/docview/329508113/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: More than four million Canadians were affected directly by the 1996 Saguenay flood, the 1997 Red River flood and last year's ice storm. Last year alone, Canadians spent $3-billion recovering from weather extremes. This suffering could have been reduced if we had invested in projects to make Canadians less vulnerable and more resilient to extreme events.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurance+lessons&amp;author=Kovacs%2C+Paul&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-12-01&amp;spage=C.07&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Re: Insurance Risk Not in the Weather, Nov. 16.
This editorial was written about a very different conference on Building Resilient Communities than the one I attended. Some speakers discussed aspects of climate change during the conference, but mostly we heard from leading scientists and experts about lessons that we should learn from past disasters. The overall message was: We have the knowledge to reduce the human suffering and the financial losses due to extreme events, what we need now is the leadership and drive to act.
More than four million Canadians were affected directly by the 1996 Saguenay flood, the 1997 Red River flood and last year's ice storm. Last year alone, Canadians spent $3-billion recovering from weather extremes. This suffering could have been reduced if we had invested in projects to make Canadians less vulnerable and more resilient to extreme events.
Canadian insurers propose the establishment of a fund to invest in projects to protect Canadians. Small investments today could save taxpayers a great deal of money and personal hardship in the future. For example, $63-million was spent in the 1960s to build a floodway in Winnipeg and, since then, governments have saved several hundreds of millions of dollars in disaster relief payments. We have the knowledge to make our communities more resilient to extreme events. Those who would like to help are invited to contact the Insurance Bureau of Canada.
Paul Kovacs, senior vice-president, policy development, Insurance Bureau of Canada.

Home truths: You're invited to join a house warming party Homeowners can save money - and the planet - by spending a few pounds on their properties.

Author: Langton, Elizabeth

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/home-truths-youre-invited-join-house-warming/docview/245458432/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: This winter, householders will squeeze money through gaps in the floorboards, throw it out of the window and through the roof. They will waste thousands of pounds on sky-high fuel bills through a lack of basic insulation in their homes, say energy campaigners. The arguments for putting more effort into saving energy are hard to ignore. Carbon dioxide emissions could be cut by 11.5% in the UK if everyone introduced energy-efficient measures in the home, says the Energy Saving Trust, an advisory body supported by the government. Not only is the planet suffering but homeowners are shelling out unnecessarily, it says. The average house can save pounds 200 a year through energy-efficient measures that may cost little or nothing to put into place. Last month the government published plans to make home buying and selling in England and Wales faster and easier. Among the proposals are measures to provide homebuyers with detailed information about the quantity of heat, light and other forms of energy used by the property they want to buy as part of a report on its condition.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Home+truths%3A+You%27re+invited+to+join+a+house+warming+party+Homeowners+can+save+money+-+and+the+planet+-+by+spending+a+few+pounds+on+their+properties.&amp;author=Langton%2C+Elizabeth&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-27&amp;spage=22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: This winter, householders will squeeze money through gaps in the floorboards, throw it out of the window and through the roof. They will waste thousands of pounds on sky-high fuel bills through a lack of basic insulation in their homes, say energy campaigners.
Now environmental charities are calling on the government to cut the price of DIY home insulation products to galvanise consumers into action.
The arguments for putting more effort into saving energy are hard to ignore. Carbon dioxide emissions could be cut by 11.5% in the UK if everyone introduced energy-efficient measures in the home, says the Energy Saving Trust, an advisory body supported by the government.
Not only is the planet suffering but homeowners are shelling out unnecessarily, it says. The average house can save pounds 200 a year through energy-efficient measures that may cost little or nothing to put into place. Last month the government published plans to make home buying and selling in England and Wales faster and easier. Among the proposals are measures to provide homebuyers with detailed information about the quantity of heat, light and other forms of energy used by the property they want to buy as part of a report on its condition.
Friends of the Earth has lobbied the government for two years to introduce the measures, which are supported by the Council of Mortgage Lenders. The charity expects properties to be given an energy efficiency rating along with advice on improvements. The extra information is expected to cost no more than pounds 10, says FoE.
The government is committed to tackling climate change and global warming by cutting carbon dioxide emissions by a fifth by the year 2010. Critics say ministers are in danger of falling short of that target unless they do more to encourage householders to play a part.
Environmental campaigners want ministers to end the anomaly between the amount of VAT charged on energy consumption or fuel - 5% - and the amount charged on energy saving materials - 17.5%. The government has made some concessions but these have been confined to a limited reduction in VAT rates on materials purchased under particular government grant schemes, says Martyn Williams, senior parliamentary officer at FoE.
The government says its hands are tied by the EU law that prevents it lowering VAT. Mr Williams has written to chancellor Gordon Brown arguing that this position is no longer sustainable. In September, the French government cut VAT rates on energy-saving materials from 22% to 5.5% and Italy is following suit.
Mr Williams welcomes the inclusion of an energy audit in the homebuyer's survey, but points out that the proposals are unlikely to be implemented for another couple of years. In the meantime, cutting the costs of energy-saving materials would give householders an added incentive to take the matter seriously, he says.
Some lenders are doing their bit to encourage homeowners to lag their boilers, insulate their lofts and fill their cavity walls. Homebuyers who undertake a Homefile survey offered through the Woolwich receive a energy efficiency rating on the property as well as more traditional elements such as a home condition report.
Norwich and Peterborough has gone further in offering financial carrots to encourage green thinking. Last year the society launched a green mortgage, designed to `encourage homebuyers and developers to reduce harmful greenhouse gas emissions". In practice this means measuring a property's energy efficiency using a procedure which rates it between 1 and 100 - the higher the rating, the more efficient the home.
New homes have an average rating of 70 and second-hand properties a dismal 35. The government is asking developers to build homes with a rating of 80 or more.
For those living in older homes or who want to remortgage, the N&P's green mortgage offers a 1.25% dis count off the standard variable rate for two years, with the proviso that they take out the society's buildings and contents insurance during the discounted rate period; a free energy survey and pounds 500 cashback towards home improvements recommended by the energy survey. Those buying new- build homes through the N&P with an energy rating of 80 or more can obtain a 2% discount off the standard variable rate for two years.
Savers with the Ecology Building Society can apply for an Earthsaver Remortgage which promotes energy efficient home improvements. The society's valuation comes with a free energy survey that includes the current energy rating and the maximum score achievable if improvements are made. For the society to lend, the borrower must undertake to achieve 80% of the maximum energy rating score.
Householders not in the market for a new mortgage or survey can have their home surveyed by an energy rating assessor. The Federation of Authorised Energy Rating Organisations can provide the name of local ex perts. Alternatively, the Energy Saving Trust offers a free DIY home energy check via the Energy Efficiency Hotline on 0345-277- 200. After answering some questions, applicants will receive a personalised report on the most cost efficient ways to save energy in their home.
Climate change and global warming are not far-off catastrophes: experts predict their effects will be felt within the next few years. Friends of the Earth says homeowners who make small improvements to their homes now will have a positive impact on the future.
Illustration Caption: article-hmtruths

People: Williams, Martyn

Where $609 Billion in Federal Spending Will Go: [FINAL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/where-609-billion-federal-spending-will-go/docview/408529849/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: After an odyssey that began with President Clinton delivering his fiscal 2000 budget plan to Congress on Feb. 1, Congress finished its work for the year late last week, completing action on a $385 billion spending package that finances seven Cabinet departments and the District of Columbia government (which got $435.8 million). In all, the 13 spending bills covering all defense and domestic spending other than entitlements total $609 billion. This figure, however, doesn't account for the 0.38 percent across-the-board cut agreed to by Congress and Clinton to help offset new spending. A tumultuous year in which American farmers were parched by drought, soaked by floods and buffeted by low commodity prices led to an 11 percent increase in the Department of Agriculture's budget to $68 billion, up from $61 billion for fiscal 1999.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Where+%24609+Billion+in+Federal+Spending+Will+Go&amp;author=&amp;issn=01908286&amp;title=The+Washington+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-26&amp;spage=A.43&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: After an odyssey that began with President Clinton delivering his fiscal 2000 budget plan to Congress on Feb. 1, Congress finished its work for the year late last week, completing action on a $385 billion spending package that finances seven Cabinet departments and the District of Columbia government (which got $435.8 million).
Eight other appropriations bills were passed earlier.
In all, the 13 spending bills covering all defense and domestic spending other than entitlements total $609 billion. This figure, however, doesn't account for the 0.38 percent across-the-board cut agreed to by Congress and Clinton to help offset new spending.
Outlays are the amount of money the government proposes to spend in the fiscal year, which began Oct. 1. The agencies generally discuss their funding in terms of budget authority, the amount of money the law allows the government to commit to spend in either the current or future fiscal years. Highlights of how each agency would be affected:
AGRICULTURE
A tumultuous year in which American farmers were parched by drought, soaked by floods and buffeted by low commodity prices led to an 11 percent increase in the Department of Agriculture's budget to $68 billion, up from $61 billion for fiscal 1999.
The budget includes a farm bailout package worth $8.7 billion, up from the $6 billion emergency package passed last year.
Clinton accepted the $8.7 billion figure in order to get aid to farmers as quickly as possible but criticized it as poorly targeted, with payments possibly going to farmers who had not planted crops.
Clinton and Agriculture Secretary Dan Glickman also complained that the back-to-back bailouts were a direct result of the 1996 Freedom to Farm Act, passed by Republicans intent on phasing out federal subsidies for farmers. Glickman has promised to offer legislation next year to revamp the Freedom to Farm law.
The largest chunk of the agriculture budget, $35 billion, is devoted to food and nutrition programs, including food stamps, school lunches and the Women, Infants and Children program.
The second-biggest piece, $17.6 billion, is intended to aid farmers through operational loans, crop insurance and price supports.
--Ben White
COMMERCE
The Commerce Department came away with $8.7 billion, $3.5 billion more than its fiscal 1999 funding. The reason for the huge jump: the 2000 census. Despite the overall gain, the department's budget is still $370 million below the White House's request.
About $4.48 billion of the department's outlays is emergency funding for the census. Lawmakers gave the White House almost all the funding it wanted for the census, falling just $11 million short.
The National Weather Service got $604 million, a $43 million increase. But the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, which oversees the weather service, took a major hit: It was funded at $2.35 billion, $176 million above the previous year's level but $164 million below Clinton's request.
Also taking a big hit was the National Institute of Standards and Technology's Advanced Technology Program, which has helped companies develop infant technologies since fiscal 1990. It got $142 million, $61 million less than in the previous year, but the program has roughly that amount of carry-over money from past budgets.
--John Burgess
DEFENSE
The defense appropriations bill delivered $267.8 billion in outlays for the Pentagon, $4.5 billion more than Clinton had requested. To meet spending caps, Congress built a number of bookkeeping maneuvers into the legislation, such as counting some military pay costs as emergency spending and stretching out funding on some programs.
The $73.9 billion appropriated for personnel costs includes a 4.8 percent across-the-board pay increase for service members--the biggest raise given to the armed forces in 18 years--plus a number of targeted raises for individuals with selected skills or experience that make them especially valuable or hard to retain.
Perhaps the most controversial procurement item in the current budget is the $1 billion for the Air Force's F-22 Raptor, the newest stealth fighter.
--Roberto Suro
EDUCATION
At the Education Department, which has prospered during the Clinton years, the budget rose to $35.6 billion, up $2.1 billion or 6.3 percent. Most of the increase will be used for the department's traditional mission of helping disadvantaged students.
The largest single increase, $700 million, is devoted to special education services that school districts must provide under federal law to learning-disabled children. An additional $254 million is intended for after-school programs in inner-city and rural areas. An extra $210 million is to be spent on the Title 1 remedial program for disadvantaged students, which remains the agency's biggest discretionary program. A Clinton initiative to hire more teachers to reduce class sizes in the earliest grades received a $100 million funding increase.
The maximum benefit under the Pell Grant program for low-income college students is up $175, to $3,300.
--Kenneth J. Cooper
ENERGY
Not counting the 0.38 percent across-the-board spending cut, the Department of Energy ended up with $17.432 billion in budget authority--up from $17.308 billion the previous year.
The biggest increase was for DOE's cleanup of environmental hot spots such as Hanford, the former nuclear weapons manufacturing site in Washington state. DOE received a $93 million increase for that type of environmental management. Other increases were $46 million for defense programs, such as DOE's atomic weapons stockpile stewardship, and $55 million for work on energy efficiency and renewable energy.
DOE programs on environmental safety and health as well as nonproliferation and national security sustained small cuts.
--Martha M. Hamilton
EPA
The Environmental Protection Agency, a major target of GOP budget- cutting after the Republicans took over Congress in 1995, held its own. The $7.59 billion total for the agency was the same as in the previous year, and funds for environmental programs and management grew by $50 million--less than what Clinton had requested.
Congress generally avoided "riders" restricting EPA's environmental enforcement, although the final budget exempts scrap metal dealers from liability for cleanup costs at "Superfund" toxic- waste sites.
However, Congress cut the president's request for the Climate Change Technology Initiative by more than $111 million, bringing its 2000 funding to $115 million, the same as in 1999. State-level revolving funds for safe drinking water amounted to $820 million, $20 million above the president's request, while the revolving funds for clean water were boosted $500 million above the request, to $1.35 billion.
--Dan Morgan
HHS
Congress provided $4.65 billion for the Health and Human Services Department this year, up from $4.3 billion last year.
As part of an ongoing effort to double biomedical research over the next five years, the bill boosts the National Institutes of Health's budget by 15 percent, to $17.9 billion, $2 billion above the president's request. However, the legislation delays $3 billion of that spending to avoid dipping into the Social Security surplus. NIH does not expect the delay to affect research activities, unlike the original plan to postpone $7.5 billion in spending.
The measure includes $1.6 billion for Ryan White AIDS programs-- $184 million more than last year's level--and $3 billion for the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, a $264 million boost.
--Juliet Eilperin
HUD
Republicans had threatened severe cuts in the Department of Housing and Urban Development budget but, in the end, they agreed to modest increases in most major HUD programs. All told, the department got $26.3 billion, up $2.2 billion from the year before--but $1.7 billion below what Clinton had requested.
The most striking compromise brought 60,000 new rental vouchers for low-income families, after the Clinton administration had requested 100,000 and Republicans had countered with zero.
The budget also includes more money for public housing renovation projects, homelessness initiatives and anti-discrimination programs. And it will fund a bipartisan plan to help protect hundreds of thousands of subsidized-housing residents from the conversion of their subsidized apartments to market-rate housing.
--Michael Grunwald
INTERIOR
Interior Department officials say they are pleased with their fiscal 2000 appropriation of about $8.35 billion, a 5.5 percent increase over fiscal 1999. "We've always been one of the best-loved departments, but we haven't always been one of the best funded," said John M. Berry, assistant secretary for policy, management and budget. "We're glad to see Congress start to turn that around a little bit."
Interior got almost $330 million in budget authority for the administration's "Land Legacy" program for land conservation and acquisition, a little more than half the administration's original request. The Bureau of Indian Affairs received a 7.2 percent increase in funding, including $95 million to improve the agency's management of tribal members' trust assets.
Interior also got $2.86 billion for the land management operations of the National Park Service, Bureau of Land Management and U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service--just $25 million shy of the administration's original request.
--Tom Kenworthy
JUSTICE
The Justice Department's budget for fiscal 2000 is $21.2 billion, slightly more than the $20.8 billion in fiscal 1999 and reflecting a leveling off after years of growth, officials said.
While there was a modest increase to account for inflation and the federal pay raise, there was a significant decrease of about $850 million in funding for the Community Oriented Policing Services program (COPS), which is aimed at putting more police officers on the streets. The Bureau of Prisons got the biggest chunk of the budget, with $3.1 billion for salary and expenses and $557 million for new prison construction.
While the Justice Department did not receive the $20 million it wanted for a massive lawsuit against the tobacco industry, the agency is permitted to reprogram funds within its budget to carry the lawsuit forward. Justice did get $10 million for a new "community prosecution" program led by Deputy Attorney General Eric H. Holder Jr.
And the budget includes money for the Immigration and Naturalization Service to hire 1,000 border patrol officers.
The department's civil rights division, which handles hate crimes, got a funding boost of more than $10 million, to $82.2 million.
--David A. Vise
LABOR
In the end, the budget for fiscal 2000 turned out to be a pleasant surprise for the Labor Department. After months of seeing the House trim one program after another, budget negotiators restored almost all of the cuts during the final days of negotiations.
The department ended up with about $11.22 billion in budget authority--a 3 percent increase over the fiscal 1999 level, or half of the increase the White House had requested. The final amount was 11 percent higher than the spending authority approved by the House.
In the final budget negotiations, spending authority was restored for a wide variety of departmental programs, from worker protection and employment training to occupational safety and health, although almost all of the funding was below the president's original budget request. The final spending bill restored $143 million that had been cut in the bill vetoed on Oct. 27 by Clinton.
--Frank Swoboda
STATE
Congress appropriated $22.3 billion for international affairs, $200 million more than the amount the president had requested but less than the $23.4 billion spending level reached during fiscal 1999.
The fiscal 1999 amount had swelled from an initial appropriation of $19.7 billion after supplemental bills were passed for humanitarian aid for Kosovo, anti-narcotics operations and heightened embassy security after the bombings in Tanzania and Kenya. The fiscal 2000 budget continues to stress embassy security, with $314 million for building new and secure embassies and $254 million for increased operational security spending, such as hiring more guards and diplomatic security agents. It also doubles support for international peacekeeping operations, to $500 million.
In the final international affairs appropriations, Congress put in $1.8 billion to fund the Wye accords--which provide additional aid to Israel, Jordan and the Palestinian Authority. An extra $25 million was inserted for Egypt. Separately, Congress finally authorized $926 million--appropriated over three years--to cover U.S. arrears owed to the United Nations. The administration, however, got only $123 million of the $370 million it sought to ease the burden borne by poor, heavily indebted developing countries. The Peace Corps failed to get the money needed to expand, but got enough to maintain its current level of volunteers.
--Steven Mufson
TRANSPORTATION
Congress approved a total of $50.17 billion in mandatory and discretionary spending for transportation in fiscal 2000, a $2.9 billion increase over the previous year.
The bill includes $27.7 billion for highway spending, a $2.2 billion increase, and $5.79 billion for mass transit, a $407 million increase. The measure also provides $1.95 billion for airport improvements, a $350 million increase, and full funding of $571 million for Amtrak, the financially troubled railroad.
--Eric Pianin
TREASURY
The Treasury Department spending bill generally kept its programs at about the same funding levels as the previous year. The bill met Clinton's request for the Internal Revenue Service, providing $8.2 billion, which will allow the agency to continue its modernization and reorganization initiatives.
The Customs Service received $1.94 billion, including $4 million to target international child pornography trafficking and child exploitation via the Internet.
Overall, the bill contains $12.3 billion for Treasury, up from $12 billion in fiscal 1999.
The bill also doubles the presidential salary to $400,000, effective in January 2001, and allows a pay raise for members of Congress, who will receive a $4,600 cost-of-living increase in January 2000, increasing their salaries to $141,300.
--Stephen Barr
VETERANS AFFAIRS
The Department of Veterans Affairs received about $19 billion for veterans' medical care in fiscal 2000, a $1.7 billion increase and far more than Congress has ever provided for VA health care in a single year. The VA said the extra money will help improve the quality of its medical care and cut down on waiting times for health services.
There is also more than $23 billion for entitlement programs. To expedite claims processing, Congress provided an extra $51 million over last year's level, which the VA said would allow the department to move 440 additional employees into claims work.
--Stephen Barr

Subject: Government agencies; Federal budget; Government spending

Business given climate change warning: [London edition]

Author: Bilefsky, Dan

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/business-given-climate-change-warning/docview/248790473/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Businesses in Britain's wealthiest regions fail to recognise that climate change could destroy their livelihoods, environmental experts have warned. In a report examining the effect of climate change on Britain over the next 50 years, specialists argue that global warming could bring storm surges and rising sea levels to the south-east. It says the service sector, which employs 1.5m people in the region, could be hard hit as coastal flooding and dry, hot summers transform transport, health and work.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Business+given+climate+change+warning&amp;author=Bilefsky%2C+Dan&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-25&amp;spage=05&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Businesses in Britain's wealthiest regions fail to recognise that climate change could destroy their livelihoods, environmental experts have warned.
In a report examining the effect of climate change on Britain over the next 50 years, specialists argue that global warming could bring storm surges and rising sea levels to the south-east.
It says the service sector, which employs 1.5m people in the region, could be hard hit as coastal flooding and dry, hot summers transform transport, health and work.
Experts warn that water-intensive users such as paper, pharmaceutical and chemical manufacturers could become uncompetitive if above-average tempera-tures dried up the water supply and the quality of groundwater was damaged.
The insurance sector may face spiralling claims for coastal flooding, subsidence and wind, and millions of pounds would have to be spent on flood defences.
Meanwhile, droughts could reduce rivers to shallow streams and an increase in pests and livestock disease would affect already hard- pressed farmers.
Merylyn McKenzie-Hedger, programme co-ordinator of the UK Climate Impacts Programme, which commissioned the study, said: "The south-east is under pressure from many quarters and climate change is going to make things worse. A new planning agenda is required - doing nothing is not an option."
However, the report, produced by consultants W S Atkins, the Meteorological Office and the Agricultural Development and Advisory Service, suggests that hot dry summers could provide a fillip to the tourist industry as visitor numbers grow.
It is being launched at the Royal Geographical Society in London today. Rising to the Challenge Impacts of Climate Change in the South East in the 21st Century. Contact: Sue Postle-Hammond. 01344 856130. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Brown warned on new climate tax: [FIRST Edition]

Author: Morley, Chris

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/brown-warned-on-new-climate-tax/docview/323249890/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: GORDON Brown's planned pounds 1 billion tax on big energy users is "seriously flawed" and will hit West Midlands firms with the most part-time workers, top business advisers claim. The Chancellor gave ground by moderating plans for the so-called climate change levy in his pre-Budget speech earlier this month, but did not silence his critics who claim it will particularly hit industry in the region.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Brown+warned+on+new+climate+tax&amp;author=Morley%2C+Chris&amp;issn=&amp;title=Evening+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-23&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: GORDON Brown's planned pounds 1 billion tax on big energy users is "seriously flawed" and will hit West Midlands firms with the most part-time workers, top business advisers claim.
The Chancellor gave ground by moderating plans for the so-called climate change levy in his pre-Budget speech earlier this month, but did not silence his critics who claim it will particularly hit industry in the region.
Carole Rankin, Birmingham-based partner with Deloitte & Touche, said: "The levy will significantly increase the energy bills of many companies thereby potentially putting many of those companies out of business or driving them overseas.
"It will also involve considerable extra compliance costs for energy providers."
Her comments were backed by economic adviser Prof Roger Bootle who said that the proposed offsetting of the energy tax through a national insurance rebate was badly flawed.
He said: "It cannot yet be known whether the amount to be raised from the levy and the amount to be handed back will be equal."
He pointed out that the redistribution of cash would not only depend on how much energy companies used but also the structure of their wage bill.
With an pounds 83 limit below which no national insurance is paid, those firms with a host of part-time workers earning less than the threshold would get less back.
"Employers of part-time workers, or those businesses which offer employment commanding relatively low wages - jobs which the Government is supposedly trying to encourage - will enjoy the lowest rebates and, even if they are only average users of energy,may be hit most severely," said Prof Bootle.

People: Bootle, Roger

Climate change levy set to cost industry dear: [CITY Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-levy-set-cost-industry-dear/docview/326602060/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The climate change levy will add greatly to the costs of many industries, accountants Deloitte & Touche have warned. Although, in theory at least, it will be offset by a reduction in employers' National Insurance contributions, the levy is estimated to raise approximately pounds 1 billion a year. Ms Carole Rankin, a partner with Deloitte & Touche in Birmingham, said: "CCl will significantly increase the energy bills of many companies, thereby potentially putting many of them out of business or driving them overseas.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+change+levy+set+to+cost+industry+dear&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Birmingham+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-19&amp;spage=22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The climate change levy will add greatly to the costs of many industries, accountants Deloitte & Touche have warned.
Although, in theory at least, it will be offset by a reduction in employers' National Insurance contributions, the levy is estimated to raise approximately pounds 1 billion a year.
Ms Carole Rankin, a partner with Deloitte & Touche in Birmingham, said: "CCl will significantly increase the energy bills of many companies, thereby potentially putting many of them out of business or driving them overseas.
"It will also involve considerable extra compliance costs for energy providers."
A CCl discount of up to 80 per cent will be applicable to limited categories of energy-intensive business but only if certain energy measures are introduced.
Prof Roger Bootle, economic adviser at Deloitte & Touche, said: "Given that the aim of the tax is to discourage the use of energy, it is to be expected that high energy users would be badly hit. But the design of the tax and the supposedly offsettingreduction in national insurance is seriously flawed.
"It cannot yet be known whether the amount to be raised from CCL and the amount to be handed back will be equal."
But, says the firm, even if the rates for CCl and rebates are accurately set so as to make them exactly offsetting, there will be a redistribution between firms that is largely arbitrary, depending, not only upon the amount and type of their energyconsumption, but also upon the structure of their wage bill.
The rebate comes in proportion to their NI bill, yet their NI bill is not in proportion to their wage bill because there is an pounds 83 threshold below which no National Insurance is paid.

People: Rankin, Carole

Energy Services order boost: [FIRST Edition]

Author: CHRIS MORLEY Industrial Correspondent

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/energy-services-order-boost/docview/323272403/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: A WEST Midlands energy management company has powered to more than pounds 10 million of potential orders in two days - helped by Gordon Brown. Halesowen-based Energy Services found its order books swelling this week as it exhibited at the NEMEX '99 show at the National Exhibition Centre. The independent company, which has the backing of Midlands Electricty, helps businesses manage their energy and environmental needs with an eye on government legislation.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Energy+Services+order+boost&amp;author=CHRIS+MORLEY+Industrial+Correspondent&amp;issn=&amp;title=Evening+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-19&amp;spage=37&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A WEST Midlands energy management company has powered to more than pounds 10 million of potential orders in two days - helped by Gordon Brown.
Halesowen-based Energy Services found its order books swelling this week as it exhibited at the NEMEX '99 show at the National Exhibition Centre.
The independent company, which has the backing of Midlands Electricty, helps businesses manage their energy and environmental needs with an eye on government legislation.
Its dramatic orders flow followed last week's confirmation by the Chancellor that he was to press on with the proposed energy tax which will hit heavy industrial consumers of power the hardest.
Although he modified the planned climate change levy, it was not enough to reassure many sectors of industry.
It has caused a storm with West Midlands business leaders, many of whom believe the rebate through the national insurance roll would benefit only labour-intensive service industries which were relatively light on energy consumption.
Under the tax, which will come into force in April 2001, rebates for intensive users will be boosted to up to 80 per cent and allow exemptions for combined heat and power systems.
But Mr Brown ruled out taking steps to weight the tax against carbon use, leaving a large number of companies and organisations excluded from any potential rebates.
Energy Services spokesman Stuart Jackson said: "With the millennium bug preparations largely out of the way, businesses are waking up to the implications of the climate change levy and the challenge to remain competitive by making energy efficiencies andimproving business performance.
"The Chancellor's softening of the levy in last week's pre-Budget report, while welcome does not go far enough and businesses realise that there will be a high price to pay if they fail to make preparations."
Mr Jackson added: "Industries should not delay in reviewing their energy management strategies now.
"They may well find that hidden energy savings could bring significant benefits in the future."

People: Jackson, Stuart

Insurance risk not in the weather: [National Edition]

Author: Corcoran, Terence

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurance-risk-not-weather/docview/329629013/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Daily Show, a satirical news report broadcast by the Comedy Channel in the United States, recently did a bit on how television news tends to treat every storm and hurricane movement as a looming and unprecedented mega-disaster. They called the phenomenon "The Storm of the Century of the Week." The timing was perfect. The bit coincided with the arrival of another hurricane off the U.S. coast. Before it had even touched land, the big U.S. networks were plotting a trail of destruction; Canada's networks were bringing in experts to compare it to Hurricane Hazel of 1954. In the end, nothing happened. The storm of the century of the week mostly sailed out to sea, more or less ending another routine hurricane season -- but not without again leaving the continent in a state of high anxiety over the weather.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurance+risk+not+in+the+weather&amp;author=Corcoran%2C+Terence&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-16&amp;spage=C.7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Daily Show, a satirical news report broadcast by the Comedy Channel in the United States, recently did a bit on how television news tends to treat every storm and hurricane movement as a looming and unprecedented mega-disaster. They called the phenomenon "The Storm of the Century of the Week." The timing was perfect. The bit coincided with the arrival of another hurricane off the U.S. coast. Before it had even touched land, the big U.S. networks were plotting a trail of destruction; Canada's networks were bringing in experts to compare it to Hurricane Hazel of 1954. In the end, nothing happened. The storm of the century of the week mostly sailed out to sea, more or less ending another routine hurricane season -- but not without again leaving the continent in a state of high anxiety over the weather.
At least the Comedy Channel's writers have the wit to spot climatological nonsense. The same cannot be said for Canada's insurance industry, which yesterday jumped aboard the wild weather chuckwagon as part of its campaign to get governments to spend more money on risk-reducing infrastructure.
At a Toronto conference, the Insurance Bureau of Canada, through a subsidiary called the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction, claimed global warming was playing havoc with our weather and causing huge insurance losses. "The increase in extreme weather claims is part of a global trend in which climate change has played a significant role," said G. Terry Squire, chief executive of the Co- operators Group and chair of the loss reduction institute.
To back the claim on global warming, the insurance firms called in Gordon McBean, assistant deputy minister at Environment Canada. Mr. McBean is a well-known expert in the business of turning unproved speculation into words that make it sound like climate change is underway and is responsible for increasing bouts of bad weather. Even though there's no scientific evidence, Mr. McBean manages to get the message out anyway that greenhouse gas emissions are the cause of the Saguenay and Red River floods, the ice storm and other expensive insurance risks. Coming next are falling water levels in the Great Lakes, more storms, drought and other forms of climate chaos.
Despite the Insurance Bureau's claims about global warming, the world view on the subject is different. Swiss Reinsurance has been compiling stats on natural disasters since 1970. "Scientifically speaking, there is as yet no indication of a significant long-term change in the frequency of events which can be ascribed to climatic change," said the company in its latest report. "Science is concentrating increasingly on longer-range probability forecasts for natural events, with natural climatic variability being a central consideration." What that means is that whatever bad-weather events we have had in recent years, they are nothing unusual.
Canada's insurance industry may indeed be facing increasing claims and therefore losses, but they have nothing to do with climate change. The chart below shows that the increase in natural catasptrophes follows closely the increase in the much higher incidence of man-made disasters. This general increase in turn can be attributed to a number of different factors. The main factor is a sharp increase in reporting. A hurricane that landed on unoccupied land 30 years ago is today a reported event with claims and casualties. Natural disasters also include events that are unrelated to weather, such as earthquakes. Earthquakes, however, have not yet been linked to greenhouse gas emissions.
There's nothing wrong with trying to cut your losses. Indeed, insurers may have a genuine problem. Governments are not spending enough on the infrastructure needed to avoid costly insurance claims. Take flood control. If the sewage and water runoff trunk lines have not been maintained or have not kept up with population growth and water use habits, a storm could force back-ups into basements and cause other damage. Insurers would rather not pay the rising claims.
Risk avoidance is one thing, but jumping into bed with the global warming industry, including Environment Canada, is an unseemly display of opportunism. The price of misleading and distorting can sometimes be higher than the reward.
Illustration Chart/Graph: Swiss Reinsurance / DISASTERS: Man-made versus natural: (See print copy for complete chart/graph.) ;

Subject: Insurance industry; Global warming; Earthquakes; Reinsurance; Greenhouse gases; Emissions; Greenhouse effect; Climate change; Storms

Business indexing term: Subject: Insurance industry Reinsurance; Industry: 52413 :‎ Reinsurance Carriers

Insurance risk not in the weather: [National Edition]

Author: Corcoran, Terence

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurance-risk-not-weather/docview/329613058/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Daily Show, a satirical news report broadcast by the Comedy Channel in the United States, recently did a bit on how television news tends to treat every storm and hurricane movement as a looming and unprecedented mega-disaster. They called the phenomenon "The Storm of the Century of the Week." The timing was perfect. The bit coincided with the arrival of another hurricane off the U.S. coast. Before it had even touched land, the big U.S. networks were plotting a trail of destruction; Canada's networks were bringing in experts to compare it to Hurricane Hazel of 1954. In the end, nothing happened. The storm of the century of the week mostly sailed out to sea, more or less ending another routine hurricane season -- but not without again leaving the continent in a state of high anxiety over the weather.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurance+risk+not+in+the+weather&amp;author=Corcoran%2C+Terence&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-16&amp;spage=C.07&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Daily Show, a satirical news report broadcast by the Comedy Channel in the United States, recently did a bit on how television news tends to treat every storm and hurricane movement as a looming and unprecedented mega-disaster. They called the phenomenon "The Storm of the Century of the Week." The timing was perfect. The bit coincided with the arrival of another hurricane off the U.S. coast. Before it had even touched land, the big U.S. networks were plotting a trail of destruction; Canada's networks were bringing in experts to compare it to Hurricane Hazel of 1954. In the end, nothing happened. The storm of the century of the week mostly sailed out to sea, more or less ending another routine hurricane season -- but not without again leaving the continent in a state of high anxiety over the weather.
At least the Comedy Channel's writers have the wit to spot climatological nonsense. The same cannot be said for Canada's insurance industry, which yesterday jumped aboard the wild weather chuckwagon as part of its campaign to get governments to spend more money on risk-reducing infrastructure.
At a Toronto conference, the Insurance Bureau of Canada, through a subsidiary called the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction, claimed global warming was playing havoc with our weather and causing huge insurance losses. "The increase in extreme weather claims is part of a global trend in which climate change has played a significant role," said G. Terry Squire, chief executive of the Co- operators Group and chair of the loss reduction institute.
To back the claim on global warming, the insurance firms called in Gordon McBean, assistant deputy minister at Environment Canada. Mr. McBean is a well-known expert in the business of turning unproved speculation into words that make it sound like climate change is underway and is responsible for increasing bouts of bad weather. Even though there's no scientific evidence, Mr. McBean manages to get the message out anyway that greenhouse gas emissions are the cause of the Saguenay and Red River floods, the ice storm and other expensive insurance risks. Coming next are falling water levels in the Great Lakes, more storms, drought and other forms of climate chaos.
Despite the Insurance Bureau's claims about global warming, the world view on the subject is different. Swiss Reinsurance has been compiling stats on natural disasters since 1970. "Scientifically speaking, there is as yet no indication of a significant long-term change in the frequency of events which can be ascribed to climatic change," said the company in its latest report. "Science is concentrating increasingly on longer-range probability forecasts for natural events, with natural climatic variability being a central consideration." What that means is that whatever bad-weather events we have had in recent years, they are nothing unusual.
Canada's insurance industry may indeed be facing increasing claims and therefore losses, but they have nothing to do with climate change. The chart below shows that the increase in natural catasptrophes follows closely the increase in the much higher incidence of man-made disasters. This general increase in turn can be attributed to a number of different factors. The main factor is a sharp increase in reporting. A hurricane that landed on unoccupied land 30 years ago is today a reported event with claims and casualties. Natural disasters also include events that are unrelated to weather, such as earthquakes. Earthquakes, however, have not yet been linked to greenhouse gas emissions.
There's nothing wrong with trying to cut your losses. Indeed, insurers may have a genuine problem. Governments are not spending enough on the infrastructure needed to avoid costly insurance claims. Take flood control. If the sewage and water runoff trunk lines have not been maintained or have not kept up with population growth and water use habits, a storm could force back-ups into basements and cause other damage. Insurers would rather not pay the rising claims.
Risk avoidance is one thing, but jumping into bed with the global warming industry, including Environment Canada, is an unseemly display of opportunism. The price of misleading and distorting can sometimes be higher than the reward.
Illustration Chart; Caption: Chart/Graph: DISASTERS: Man-made versus natural: (See print copy for complete chart/graph.)
Credit: National Post

Week in Review A look back at the top stories from the last week: [SPOKANE Edition]

Author: Compiled by news editor Kevin Graman from staff and wire reports.

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/week-review-look-back-at-top-stories-last/docview/394875210/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: UnitedHealthcare, one of the nation's largest managed-care companies, said Monday that it will stop overruling doctors' decisions about what care patients should receive. Sponsors of the "patients bill of rights" in Congress said United's decision proves their point. But Republican leaders said it confirms their view that market forces are working to correct any abuses by the managed-care insurance industry. That's the latest from the landmark Nurses' Health Study, conducted at the Harvard School of Public Health. Presented Monday at an American Heart Association meeting in Atlanta, the findings highlight the paradox that relatively few people in this supposedly health-crazed land are indeed doing everything they can to avert the disease that is most likely to kill them.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Week+in+Review+A+look+back+at+the+top+stories+from+the+last+week&amp;author=Compiled+by+news+editor+Kevin+Graman+from+staff+and+wire+reports.&amp;issn=10647317&amp;title=Spokesman+Review&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-14&amp;spage=A.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: NATION
Doctor-managed care
UnitedHealthcare, one of the nation's largest managed-care companies, said Monday that it will stop overruling doctors' decisions about what care patients should receive.
The company, which covers 14.5 million people nationwide, is abandoning a cornerstone of the managed-care industry's cost- containment strategy and one of the features most responsible for the outpouring of public ill will toward HMOs.
Sponsors of the "patients bill of rights" in Congress said United's decision proves their point. But Republican leaders said it confirms their view that market forces are working to correct any abuses by the managed-care insurance industry.
Taking it to heart
The good news was women who actually eat sensibly, don't smoke, get some exercise, keep the weight down and have an occasional drink can reduce their chance of heart disease an astonishing 82 percent.
The bad news was only 1 percent actually follow all the rules.
That's the latest from the landmark Nurses' Health Study, conducted at the Harvard School of Public Health. Presented Monday at an American Heart Association meeting in Atlanta, the findings highlight the paradox that relatively few people in this supposedly health-crazed land are indeed doing everything they can to avert the disease that is most likely to kill them.
POLITICS
McCain rallies
Suddenly, the front-runner is on the run.
An American Research Group poll showed Arizona Sen. John McCain in a statistical tie with George W. Bush in New Hampshire.
It is only one poll in only one state - the Texas governor holds an overwhelming lead over all rivals in national surveys - but McCain's surge threatens to reduce the value of one of Bush's greatest assets: the perception that his nomination is inevitable, and he is the best bet to reclaim the White House from Democrats.
Noblesse oblige to a fault
Donald Trump, political neophyte and presidential wannabe, has come up with a simple way to save Social Security, erase the national debt, and slash taxes for working Americans:
Gouge the rich.
Trump, toying with a bid for the Reform Party nomination, on Tuesday unveiled the highly unorthodox plan, which would slap fat cats such as The Donald himself with a one-time 14.25 percent tax on their entire net worth. Trump's personal contribution: $725 million.
The admittedly sketchy proposal, of Trump's own design, was immediately attacked by some financial experts, who worried such a drastic hit would set off a mad exodus of capital from the United States.
REGION
School shooter gets life
Kip Kinkel, the Oregon 17-year-old who killed his parents before going on a high school shooting rampage in Springfield, was sentenced Wednesday to serve nearly 112 years in prison, effectively the rest of his life.
He had admitted to killing his parents, Bill and Faith Kinkel, and two students - Mikael Nickolauson and Ben Walker - at Thurston High during two days in May 1998. Kinkel also admitted to trying to kill 25 students at Thurston and a police detective following his arrest.
Though he stood in court Wednesday and read a statement apologizing for his crimes, Kinkel seemed confused himself.
"I absolutely loved my parents and had no reason to kill them," he said. "I had no reason to dislike or kill or try to kill anyone at Thurston. I am truly sorry for all of this."
Arid Northwest
Five degrees may not sound like much, but by 2050 they'll make a world of difference to people living in Washington, Oregon and Idaho.
A University of Washington study of climate change says we can expect wetter winters, more flooding, fewer salmon, water shortages and drought-damaged forests.
"The consensus of climate scientists is that a warmer world in the next century is more than just a reasonable possibility ...," said Philip Mote, lead author of the new report.
Illustration Caption: Republican presidential hopeful Sen. John McCain of Arizona poses with 5-month-old Jack Parker-Hanlon in Weare, N.H., on Friday. A poll has McCain in a statistical deadheat with Texas Gov. George Bush in New Hampshire. File/Associated Press

People: Kinkel, Kip

Prudence imperfect: VIEWPOINT CHRIS HUMPHRIES: The chancellor made some welcome changes, but on some of the key issues for small businesses he failed to deliver: [London edition]

Author: Humphries, Chris

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/prudence-imperfect-viewpoint-chris-humphries/docview/248903400/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: All pre-Budget reports traditionally have catchy titles designed to set the tone for what lies within. While she only appeared twice in Gordon Brown's speech on Tuesday, it seemed his soulmate 'Prudence' may have written the decidedly non-sexy sound-bite for this year's statement - "Stability and steady growth for Britain". However, stability and steady growth, and the end of boom-and-bust cycles, are exactly what businesses have been calling on successive governments to deliver for many decades. The chancellor may have inherited a sound economic base from his Tory predecessors, but he has succeeded in securing a level of stability that consistently earns bouquets from businesses for his record on economic management. Although the pound remains too high, providing an additional challenge that our beleaguered exporters could well do without, Mr Brown's statement contained the key elements businesses need for continued economic stability - no big give-aways, and continued tight controls on public spending.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Prudence+imperfect%3A+VIEWPOINT+CHRIS+HUMPHRIES%3A+The+chancellor+made+some+welcome+changes%2C+but+on+some+of+the+key+issues+for+small+businesses+he+failed+to+deliver&amp;author=Humphries%2C+Chris&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-11&amp;spage=17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: All pre-Budget reports traditionally have catchy titles designed to set the tone for what lies within. While she only appeared twice in Gordon Brown's speech on Tuesday, it seemed his soulmate 'Prudence' may have written the decidedly non-sexy sound-bite for this year's statement - "Stability and steady growth for Britain".
However, stability and steady growth, and the end of boom-and-bust cycles, are exactly what businesses have been calling on successive governments to deliver for many decades. The chancellor may have inherited a sound economic base from his Tory predecessors, but he has succeeded in securing a level of stability that consistently earns bouquets from businesses for his record on economic management.
Although the pound remains too high, providing an additional challenge that our beleaguered exporters could well do without, Mr Brown's statement contained the key elements businesses need for continued economic stability - no big give-aways, and continued tight controls on public spending.
'Enterprise' was far more popular than 'Prudence' on Tuesday, including the chancellor's welcome endorsement of the National Enterprise Campaign. But what else do enterprises need, and what did they get?
Growth businesses also need a tax regime that encourages longer-term investment and maximises global competitiveness.
The significant changes to the capital gains tax regime, including a cut in the rate and a reduction in the period for tapering relief to five years, were a very positive response to calls from small business organisations, including the British Chambers of Commerce, over the past five years.
It should have been a welcome addition to a UK business tax regime that had once been the envy of much of Europe, except that the envy is rapidly disappearing.
The BCC has calculated that the increases in business taxes from this government will amount to £30bn over the life of this parliament.
This calculation was confirmed last week when the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development announced That Britain has the fastest growing tax burden of any country in Europe, with the UK paying more tax than Germany for the first time in a generation. Treasury attempts to challenge these claims by semantic arguments over definitions cut no ice with businesses.
The pre-Budget package also contained other changes that businesses have asked for - the review of planning law; the new Investment Incentive and the already announced Regional Investment Funds to help close the equity investment gap for small businesses; the end to the fuel duty escalator; and changes to the climate change levy. The last two, however, appear to contain loopholes through which the government could easily drive a fleet of French fuel tankers.
Sadly, Mr Brown said almost nothing about the most significant barrier to small business competitiveness - the strangulating impact of new employment legislation and other regulatory burdens. A new minister to co-ordinate efforts to cut red-tape provides little comfort.
Despite all its rhetoric, this government, like all its recent predecessors, has sharply increased the regulatory burden on businesses. The UK has created more than 5,000 new regulations in the life of every recent parliament, with more than 2,700 new regulations before the half-way mark of this parliament.
The unpaid tax collector role for pay-as-you-earn and national insurance plus the new obligations to pay working family tax credit and collect student loans; the rules for the working time directive admitted by ministers to be excessive; the parental leave directive where the government refused to introduce a derogation for small businesses despite explicit provision for this in the directive; the minimum wage, accepted by the business community at the level set, but then surrounded by excessive record keeping and controls; value added tax regulations; the complex phasing rules of capital gains tax itself - the list goes on.
The real problem for enterprise and small businesses with this pre-Budget package is that, on the key issues of tax and regulation, it just tinkers round the edges. The Better Regulation Task Force and the Small Business Service must get real teeth on regulation; the chancellor should establish a Tax Simplification Task Force to do the same job for business taxation. Until then, the response from small businesses can only be 'good - but not nearly good enough'.
Chris Humphries is director-general of the British Chambers of Commerce Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Elderly top Brown's TV ratings: [2 Edition]

Author: Brogan, Benedict; Political Editor

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/elderly-top-browns-tv-ratings/docview/332549151/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: With revised forecasts predicting robust economic growth, Mr Gordon Brown delighted Labour back benchers by scrapping the fuel- duty escalator, announcing free television licences for over-75s, and slashing capital gains tax for long-term investments in high-risk ventures to as low as 10%. But there was no disguising the possibilities opening up before him as he prepares to take Labour into the next General Election campaign, probably in spring 2001. The new spending round due to be announced next summer will give Mr Brown a chance to deploy his so- called war chest in the interests of securing a second term for Labour. Under changes announced yesterday, the fuel duty escalator which imposes real-terms increases of 6% each year in the price of petrol and diesel, will be abandoned. There had been claims it was crippling economic development in rural areas like the Highlands, which received a second boost when the Chancellor announced plans to scrap the existing air passenger duty for flights from airports in the Highlands and Islands.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Elderly+top+Brown%27s+TV+ratings&amp;author=Brogan%2C+Benedict%3BPolitical+Editor&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-10&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE Chancellor yesterday gave Britain a pre-election preview of what he could do with the Treasury's ballooning cash surplus when he unveiled a package of attention-grabbing measures to help pensioners, the poor, and entrepreneurs.
With revised forecasts predicting robust economic growth, Mr Gordon Brown delighted Labour back benchers by scrapping the fuel- duty escalator, announcing free television licences for over-75s, and slashing capital gains tax for long-term investments in high-risk ventures to as low as 10%.
Faced with demands from trade unions and activists for increased spending, Mr Brown warned he would not risk "the future of our economy" by launching into a debate over how best to divide the "national wealth" produced by strict expenditure controls and better- than-expected economic growth. Experts suggest the Government may have as much as #20bn spare to spend.
But there was no disguising the possibilities opening up before him as he prepares to take Labour into the next General Election campaign, probably in spring 2001. The new spending round due to be announced next summer will give Mr Brown a chance to deploy his so- called war chest in the interests of securing a second term for Labour.
Under changes announced yesterday, the fuel duty escalator which imposes real-terms increases of 6% each year in the price of petrol and diesel, will be abandoned. There had been claims it was crippling economic development in rural areas like the Highlands, which received a second boost when the Chancellor announced plans to scrap the existing air passenger duty for flights from airports in the Highlands and Islands.
Although Mr Brown said increases may still be introduced on a Budget-by-Budget basis as part of the Government's commitment to the environment, any cash raised will go to a ring-fenced fund for road and public transport improvements.
This so-called hypothecation - assigning taxes to a particular purpose - will also be introduced next year for the annual 5% real- term increase in tobacco duty. Mr Brown said the #300m generated by Britain's cigarette habit would go to additional spending on the health service.
But there were doubts, voiced by the Opposition, that the carefully-targeted news in the Pre-Budget Report disguised creeping tax increases that are hitting entrepreneurs and damaging the economy's long-term prospects.
Figures published yesterday showed that while Britain avoided a recession last year, the Treasury has had to revise downwards its forecast growth in 2001 as the Bank of England's successive interest rate increases begin to bite.
The Treasury predicted rising Budget surpluses and uprated forecasts for economic growth: now estimated at 1.75% in the current year rising to between 2.5% and 3% next year, but dropping back to between 2.25% and 2.5% in 2001.
While Mr Brown hailed expected cash surpluses rising from #9.5bn this year to #13bn in 2001, adjusted figures suggest the surplus will in fact be #3.5bn this year, falling to #3bn in 2001.
There were also complaints that the Government had caved in to big business by diluting the impact of its proposed climate change levy. The changes to this levy work out as a 90% discount on the original proposals in return for agreed industry action to cut emissions of harmful "greenhouse" gases, a move condemned by environmentalists.
Mr Brown said he was laying the ground for a "decade of reform" where "enterprise and fairness can go hand in hand". He said his ambition for the next decade was to reduce child poverty by half in a decade, and to send half of all school leavers on to degrees. He told MPs it was time to move on from the "sterile century-long conflict between enterprise and fairness" which saw the left focus on a good society while the right promoted a good economy.
He announced that investors willing to put their money into new, riskier ventures will see capital gains tax drop dramatically, from 40% down to 22% for a three-year investment, and to just 10% for five years. It stunned the Tories.
Mr Brown also challenged companies to offer shares to their employees and as an inducement said the first #3000 of shares would be free of income tax and employees could buy another #1500 from their pre-tax salary, as part of his aim to open share ownership to all.
To encourage business in de-prived communities, there are initiatives designed to help entre- preneurs willing to take risks in inner cities with small grants of up to #4000 and help with computer and management training.
In another move, the Office of Fair Trading is to be given beefed- up "trust busting" powers, including the ability to impose fines of up to 30% of a firm's turnover, to tackle business cartels and anti- competitive behaviour. In addition Lord Grabiner QC has been appointed to head a task force to investigate ways of cracking down on the #50bn "black economy", with the prospect of imposing a tough new daily signing-on requirement for benefit claimants suspected of cheating.
For pensioners, the surprise announcement that households with an over-75 will from next autumn no longer have to pay for their #101 TV licence was coupled with a pledge to make the #100 winter fuel allowance a permanent pre-Christmas fixture.
As part of his drive to bring more people into work, Mr Brown announced a substantial extension of the New Deal which will be rolled out to take in all unemployed by offering them either a place on a training course or a job. A new Children's Fund will be developed to champion ways of integrating help for children.
The Tories dismissed the promise of free TV licences as a "gimmick" and accused the Chancellor of professing to be pro- business when, in reality he was pursuing anti-business policies.
Shadow Chancellor Francis Maude said angrily: "There is a new corruption investing British politics today: the corruption of the smug few who are trying to make mugs of the many."
At a glance guide - Free television licence for everyone aged 75 and over.
- Pensioners' #100 winter allowance to be paid every year.
- Automatic fuel tax escalator scrapped.
- Future petrol tax rises to be decided "Budget by Budget".
- Any rises going to a ring-fenced fund to modernise public transport.
- New Deal for unemployed extended to include over 25s.
- #300m proceeds from 5% tobacco tax escalator to go straight to the NHS.
- New Integrated Child Credit to be paid to mothers.
- Climate change levy almost halved and proceeds returned to business by a 0.3% cut in employers' National Insurance contributions.
- Crackdown on black economy estimated by Treasury at #50bn.
- Tougher moves against tobacco smuggling.
- Capital gains tax cuts to stimulate investment in growth companies.
- Tax breaks to encourage wider employee share-ownership.
- 10,000 more schools to be modernised through windfall levy.
- Tougher competition rules and utility regulation.
q 10,000 more college-based childcare places for lone parents.
q New higher growth forecast for the next two years.
- 10p starting rate of tax extended to unearned investment income.
- Measures worth #30m to boost enterprise in disadvantaged areas.
- National jobs telephone line.
- Air passenger duty scrapped in Highlands and Islands.

People: Brown, Gordon

Anger and joy as fuel escalator is scrapped: [2 Edition]

Author: Watson, Craig

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/anger-joy-as-fuel-escalator-is-scrapped/docview/332547892/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Greens accuse the Chancellor of bowing to the business lobby and making a decision that will have no effect on traffic growth THE Chancellor yesterday infuriated environmentalists and delighted road users by announcing that the controversial mechanism for increasing fuel duty above inflation would be scrapped. It had been widely tipped that the "fuel duty escalator" would be reviewed, though Mr Gordon Brown decided to go a step further. Other measures detailed by Mr Brown included a pledge to increase cuts in carbon pollution from 1.5m tonnes a year to 2m tonnes by 2010, and a decrease in the proposed climate change levy on polluters from #1750m to #1000m, with a 0.3% cut in employers' National Insurance contributions ensuring businesses would not lose out.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Anger+and+joy+as+fuel+escalator+is+scrapped&amp;author=Watson%2C+Craig&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-10&amp;spage=6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Greens accuse the Chancellor of bowing to the business lobby and making a decision that will have no effect on traffic growth THE Chancellor yesterday infuriated environmentalists and delighted road users by announcing that the controversial mechanism for increasing fuel duty above inflation would be scrapped.
It had been widely tipped that the "fuel duty escalator" would be reviewed, though Mr Gordon Brown decided to go a step further.
However, he did say fuel duty increases would still be considered on a "Budget by Budget" basis, with cash from real term increases ring-fenced for spending on public transport and modernising the road network.
Other measures detailed by Mr Brown included a pledge to increase cuts in carbon pollution from 1.5m tonnes a year to 2m tonnes by 2010, and a decrease in the proposed climate change levy on polluters from #1750m to #1000m, with a 0.3% cut in employers' National Insurance contributions ensuring businesses would not lose out.
Renewable energy and combined heat and power are to be exempt, while the rate of the levy per kilowatt hour was cut from 0.21 pence to 0.15 pence for businesses complying with the scheme. Support for energy efficiency measures under the levy will be trebled to #150m.
Mr Brown hinted that an aggregates tax may still be introduced in the future, if the quarrying industry did not voluntarily improve its environmental record.
Further talks will take place on the use of pesticides before any tax on their use is imposed.
The Chancellor said: "The Government wants to ensure that economic growth takes place in a way which ensures the effective protection of the environment and the prudent use of natural resources."
Mr Brown said it was possible to scrap the fuel escalator, which was introduced in 1993 to increase fuel duty by 6% above inflation every year, because deficit cuts had been achieved and environmental policies introduced. He promised that any future real term rises in road fuel duties would be lower than in the past.
Mr Phil Flanders, regional director of the Road Haulage Association for Scotland, said the move was good news, but did not solve the problems faced by the haulage industry.
He said: "We are still paying twice as much for fuel as on the continent. Scottish hauliers are still suffering because we are uncompetitive with continental hauliers."
The RAC welcomed the "radical shift" in policy. A spokesman said: "We are glad to see the Chancellor has taken his foot off the fuel escalator.
"This is a step in the right direction, but it remains to be seen how much fuel duty will be increased by in the next Budget."
The AA also welcomed the commitment to scrap the escalator, but warned the pledge to invest cash raised from above-inflation fuel tax rises on roads and public transport must involve new money and not prove to be a "conjuring trick".
Business representatives gave a cautious welcome to the Chancellor's statement.
The Scottish Council Development and Industry said the abolition of the escalator was a "victory for common sense" and would improve competitiveness, but other groups questioned the cost of the climate change levy, particularly for the manufacturing sector.
Scotland's First Minister Donald Dewar said he "particularly welcomed the decision to step off the fuel duty price escalator".
He added that the decision to consult on an exemption from air passenger duty for flights from airports in the Scottish Highlands and Islands was an important recognition of the area's transport needs.
Political opponents gave a mixed reaction to the fuel duty announcement, most stressing that Mr Brown had failed to promise a freeze or reduction in real term increases.
SNP deputy leader John Swinney said: "Public pressure has forced Gordon Brown to slow his attacks on the motorist - but they are not stopping, and next year we will see further increases in prices at the pumps, which are already the highest in Europe."
Lothians Green MSP Robin Harper said the Chancellor had weakened under pressure from big business, particularly over the escalator.
He added: "This is just about the worst decision the Chancellor could have made. It was the one element of the tax system that might have had an effect on road traffic growth.
"The reality is that the Government has given up when the fuel duty escalator was beginning to bite."
Friends of the Earth Scotland said the Government had gone soft over its attitude to tackling transport emissions.
Head of research Dr Richard Dixon said: "Government cannot have a credible programme on climate change if they abandon the fuel duty escalator."
Sustainable transport group TRANSform Scotland said the Government had given in to the business lobby over the escalator.
Manager Colin Howden welcomed the climate change levy but questioned whether it would be as effective as the fuel tax at cutting pollution.

People: Brown, Gordon
