Heat and power plants should be exempt from climate change levy: [London edition]

Author: Wertheim, J D

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/heat-power-plants-should-be-exempt-climate-change/docview/248788824/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Heat+and+power+plants+should+be+exempt+from+climate+change+levy&amp;author=Wertheim%2C+J+D&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-08&amp;spage=20&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: From Mr J.D. Wertheim.
Sir, As the UK's sole producer of soda ash, Brunner Mond very much welcomes the glimmer of hope on the climate change levy.
We strongly believe that, given the excellence of its environmental benefits and the energy efficiency of its operation, combined heat and power plants should be exempt from the levy. Our new CHP plant currently under construction will achieve thermal efficiency levels of 87 per cent, eliminate all SO{2} and particulate emissions from our old power plants, cut nitrous oxides emissions by two-thirds, and reduce our CO{2} emissions by 16.5 per cent while increasing electricity generation sixfold.
The investment in our CHP plant is £150m. The government's current proposals offer no credit for having already made such investments or for achieving such a high level of environmental performance. Our liability for the levy, at the full rate, would be £4m a year, and we would receive an offsetting reduction in employers' National Insurance contributions of £50,000 a year. This is not revenue neutral.
The government says that environmental taxes are to "reward the goods and punish the bads". This levy does exactly the opposite.
J.D. Wertheim, Chairman and Chief Executive, Brunner Mond Group, PO Box 4, Mond House, Northwich, Cheshire CW8 4DT Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Full text: Leading car manufacturers are threatening to stop buying British components unless government plans for a pounds 1.75bn energy tax are radically redesigned.
Rover, Ford and Vauxhall say the tax will add to their costs, forcing them to buy components overseas, a move that would threaten thousands of British jobs.
Representatives from the three car manufacturers expressed their opposition to the proposed tax on carbon emissions at a meeting with the treasury minister Stephen Timms last week.
They said a proposed cut in national insurance bills, intended to neutralise the tax, would not help them as they were not labour intensive. `He [Mr Timms] was keen to hear their concerns,' a treasury spokeswoman said.
The manufacturers are the latest industrial lobby to call for a rethink on the carbon tax, which was announced in the budget last March. Mr Brown is expected to modify his proposals in Tuesday's pre- budget report.
In doing so, however, the chancellor will have to be careful not to alienate the green lobby, which is urging him to stand firm against industry opposition to the tax.
In a paper published today, the environmental group WWF says: `We will be looking closely at the chancellor's statement on Tuesday to make sure he remains committed to key elements of the levy and does not cave in to industry scare-mongering.'
WWF says the climate change levy will benefit the environment and business by improving the economy's long-term competitiveness.
`Failure to make appropriate changes in the short run will raise the long-term economic and environmental costs.'

Challenging That Cheeseburger

Author: Akst, Daniel

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/challenging-that-cheeseburger/docview/431295538/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: IMAGINE that you're a trial lawyer. You've noticed that Americans are getting fatter, and that the government blames obesity and inactivity for more than 300,000 premature deaths every year. You've also noticed that scientists believe fatty foods kill people, and that fast-food outlets are selling cheeseburgers with both hands. You pilot your late-model Mercedes into the drive-through lane, risking grease stains on the leather upholstery. You make a purchase and discover that fast-food packaging is mute as to a more important risk. Nowhere does it say that eating a bucket of hot wings could result in serious injury or death. So you sue, hoping to win millions on behalf of heart patients -- as well as potential heart patients, who claim compensation for the worry that all those cheeseburgers may someday give them heart disease. Better yet, perhaps the states, unwilling to regulate cheeseburgers, will retain you to sue on behalf of the taxpayers, who have had to foot the public health bill for all the cheeseburgers they can't stop eating.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Challenging+That+Cheeseburger&amp;author=Akst%2C+Daniel&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-07&amp;spage=3.4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: AuthorAffiliation Daniel Akst is a novelist and financial journalist. His column tilting at conventional business wisdom appears the first Sunday of each month. E-mail: culmoney@nytimes.com.
IMAGINE that you're a trial lawyer. You've noticed that Americans are getting fatter, and that the government blames obesity and inactivity for more than 300,000 premature deaths every year. You've also noticed that scientists believe fatty foods kill people, and that fast-food outlets are selling cheeseburgers with both hands.
You pilot your late-model Mercedes into the drive-through lane, risking grease stains on the leather upholstery. You make a purchase and discover that fast-food packaging is mute as to a more important risk. Nowhere does it say that eating a bucket of hot wings could result in serious injury or death.
So you sue, hoping to win millions on behalf of heart patients -- as well as potential heart patients, who claim compensation for the worry that all those cheeseburgers may someday give them heart disease. Better yet, perhaps the states, unwilling to regulate cheeseburgers, will retain you to sue on behalf of the taxpayers, who have had to foot the public health bill for all the cheeseburgers they can't stop eating.
Far-fetched? Why? The same line of reasoning underlies the litigation that has succeeded so spectacularly against the tobacco companies; similar suits are pending against manufacturers of guns.
''The connection between cigarettes and guns,'' says Richard Daynard, a law professor at Northeastern University who founded the Tobacco Products Liability Project to spur lawsuits against the industry, ''is that both used political influence to escape safety regulation, and eventually people got tired of that and turned to the courts.''
He is right, and at first it is easy to applaud the plaintiffs' lawyers who brought the suits. But are guns and cigarettes really the same? For better or worse, Americans have the right to bear arms, the government already regulates guns, and it's at least debatable whether guns, unlike cigarettes, really do more harm than good. Besides, our experiences with outright prohibition of dangerous products like alcohol and drugs have been terrible.
The point is not the merits of gun control (which I advocate) or smoking (which I abhor). The point is, who decides? Despite the role of money in politics, government can regulate harmful products whenever there is the political will to do so. So it is puzzling to find governments as plaintiffs in product liability lawsuits. ''It's bizarre to go to court and ask for relief from the fact that you have the wrong laws,'' said Daniel Polsby, a law professor at George Mason University.
Consider global warming. When coastal cities find themselves building dikes to keep the waves at bay, why not sue Exxon and General Motors over their role in changing the world's climate? And why wait until the waters start rising? The science on global warming is already substantial, but having good science on your side is no prerequisite for suing, as the makers of silicone breast implants (and their unfortunate shareholders) well know.
By the lights of modern jurisprudence, it would make perfect sense for ''victims'' of fast food and climate change to sue, and the sooner the better: they might even succeed at deterring substantial harm. One claim against gun makers is that they failed to take the steps they should have to make sure that weapons aren't used improperly. But last year, 15,935 Americans died in alcohol-related car accidents, and the auto industry for years has known how to install devices that could make it more difficult for someone impaired by alcohol to start a car. Why isn't the plaintiff's bar assaulting Detroit on that point?
Perhaps it is just that they haven't gotten to it yet. At the moment, another target is in their sights: H.M.O.'s. The lawyers see themselves as protectors of innocent patients against profit-hungry corporations bent on rationing care. If the lawyers succeed (their first case is against Aetna), H.M.O.'s may indeed change the way they do business. But premiums will inevitably rise, forcing more Americans to join the 44 million who already lack health insurance.
THAT'S the trouble with letting lawyers and juries tackle social problems that elected officials can't seem to solve: when the buck is passed to the courts, the outcome may never suit anyone but the lawyers. Consider the Supreme Court's record. It upheld slavery until the Civil War made its decisions moot. It tried to paper over racial segregation with the ''separate but equal'' fiction, and when that failed, ordered public schools integrated, yet de facto school segregation persists. Roe v. Wade has hardly laid the abortion debate to rest.
It's hard to believe that lawyers, judges and juries can do any better with guns or global warming (or gluttony). But if the political system remains paralyzed by money and cynicism, it shouldn't be surprising if the courts try.
Illustration Drawing (Toni Zules)

Subject: Products liability; Litigation; Public good

Economic SPADS could put Blair on the wrong track: [3 Edition]

Author: Green, Alf

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/economic-spads-could-put-blair-on-wrong-track/docview/331195884/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It comes at a deeply inconvenient time for politicians. Here in the UK, the Blair government is through its mid-point and looking increasingly towards its prospects of re-election. On Tuesday, Gordon Brown will deliver his latest pre-Budget report to MPs. No doubt the Iron Chancellor, awash with #12 billion of accumulated reserves and basking in the reflected glory of a growth performance this year which wrong-footed the entire City, looks like exceeding Treasury forecasts and has helped persuade the MPC that interest rates need to rise, will play the restrained miracle worker. BROWN will rise in the Commons with Tony Blair's confirmation of his Chancellor's profoundly ferrous qualities still ringing in his ears. There will be no irresponsible pre-election spending spree, the PM promised the CBI. We can count on Brown's resolute determination to stick to the existing spending targets of the Comprehensive Spending Review for that. Then there are the peripheral parts of Britain, like Labour- voting Scotland, where the economic cycle is most out of kilter with London and dearer money is the last thing we need. In the imprecise art of interest rate management, the MPC may think it is simply following the government's stated remit and choking off future inflationary pressures.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Economic+SPADS+could+put+Blair+on+the+wrong+track&amp;author=Green%2C+Alf&amp;issn=&amp;title=Sunday+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-07&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   balancingact
SINCE the fatal Paddington crash we have all become familiar with the rail industry acronym SPAD. Too many SPADs - signals passed at danger - compromise rail safety and can, as we've seen, kill innocent people.
Politicians and central bankers all agree there is no room for SPADs when it comes to managing an economy either. This isn't necessarily a matter of life and death. But to avoid the very real material ravages of boom and bust on people's lives, preventative action must be taken long before the economic signals start flashing red.
Preventing economic SPADs is, in principle, a much tougher proposition than making trains obey track signals. The brakes are not so demonstrably linked to the wheels. The warning signals can be much more obscure. And the time delays between driver reaction and any consequences being felt can be considerable.
In pre-millennial Britain, those now driving the macro-economy, the members of the Bank of England's monetary policy committee, have only one brake lever, interest rates, with which to pre-empt danger signals - principally inflationary pressures - which may only rear their ugly heads some 18 months to two years further down the tracks.
It can take that long for the impact of dearer or cheaper money today to feed through to company order books, personal consumption patterns and all the other variables that add up to whether an economy is performing in a sustainable or an unsustainable way.
And that impact can vary considerably, depending on which carriage of the UK economic train you happen to be occupying. Not so long ago the Bank of England governor Eddie George got into hot water for appearing to suggest the loss of northern manufacturing jobs might be a price worth paying to keep UK inflation under control.
Just last month, Greenwich NatWest produced some fresh analysis concluding that Northern Ireland, Scotland and the north east of England are indeed the three northern, peripheral parts of these islands least well correlated with the prevailing UK economic cycle and most likely to suffer damage as rates rise. Tentative support for all those tabloid headlines suggesting we Scots take the pain when the powers-that-be try to choke off yet another London property boom.
Putting the task like that, you begin to wonder why anyone would want to be a member of the MPC which last week pushed up UK interest rates by a quarter point for the second time in three months. The job looks like mission impossible. But mission impossible or not, the trend in global interest rates is now decisively upwards.
The MPC decision came as no real surprise. But the bigger half- point hike in euro-zone rates announced the same day by the European Central Bank was a lot less predictable and, potentially, much more damaging to already fragile confidence levels across the continent. With America's Federal Reserve poised to join the trend, possibly later this month, a monetary squeeze is clearly under way on both sides of the Atlantic.
It comes at a deeply inconvenient time for politicians. Here in the UK, the Blair government is through its mid-point and looking increasingly towards its prospects of re-election. On Tuesday, Gordon Brown will deliver his latest pre-Budget report to MPs. No doubt the Iron Chancellor, awash with #12 billion of accumulated reserves and basking in the reflected glory of a growth performance this year which wrong-footed the entire City, looks like exceeding Treasury forecasts and has helped persuade the MPC that interest rates need to rise, will play the restrained miracle worker.
BROWN will rise in the Commons with Tony Blair's confirmation of his Chancellor's profoundly ferrous qualities still ringing in his ears. There will be no irresponsible pre-election spending spree, the PM promised the CBI. We can count on Brown's resolute determination to stick to the existing spending targets of the Comprehensive Spending Review for that.
But the Chancellor has already set in train other fiscal changes whose full impact has yet to be felt in the critical period running up to the next election. If interest rates, including the cost of home loans, are now set on an inexorable upward path with as many as three or four more quarter point hikes next year being predicted by City economists, the overall impact on voter opinion as the next election looms could become extremely damaging to Labour's prospects.
Basic rate income tax is already due to be cut by 1p from next April. But at the same time, the married couples allowance and residual mortgage interest relief are being axed. The upper earnings limit on national insurance contributions will start to rise and the new children's tax credit will be "tapered away" from families paying higher rate tax. On top of that, there's the fuel duty escalator, due for another steep hike in April unless the government does a U-turn.
Many of Labour's new-found supporters in middle Britain may find themselves increasingly out of pocket as our redistributionist Chancellor moves resources towards poorer families, particularly poorer families with children.
And if, thanks to the MPC's efforts to quench inflationary pressures two years down the road, middle Britain has to pay more for its mortgages, its overdrafts and its plastic, the honeymoon with Blair, the longest-lasting honeymoon in post-war British politics, may finally be on the wane.
There's a battle looming too with British industry which has been conned into believing lower rates of corporation tax mean companies are actually handing less over to the Exchequer. Apart from higher borrowing costs and that same fuel price escalator, industry is bracing itself for a fight over the climate change levy, due to take #1750 million out of company profits from the year after next.
Then there are the peripheral parts of Britain, like Labour- voting Scotland, where the economic cycle is most out of kilter with London and dearer money is the last thing we need. In the imprecise art of interest rate management, the MPC may think it is simply following the government's stated remit and choking off future inflationary pressures.
But if opinion polls flash red next year, that's one SPAD that will surely force Gordon Brown and colleagues to rethink their entire strategy.

People: Brown, Gordon; Blair, Tony

____________________________________________________________

Brown feels energy tax heat: [3 Edition]

Author: Wilson, Steve; steve.wilson@sundayherald.com

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/brown-feels-energy-tax-heat/docview/331187201/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: [Gordon Brown] meets Nasdaq chairman Frank Zarb at Wednesday's launch of the market's pan-European share index. The Chancellor was under pressure last week as it was claimed that his proposed energy tax would increase fuel bills and threaten jobs Photograph: Peter Jordan/Reuters In his pre-budget statement, Gordon Brown will also flesh out proposals for tax credits on research and development conducted by small and medium-sized firms. Despite criticisms that they will do little to increase overall share ownership, he will also address the issue of all-employee share schemes, having earlier pledged to double the number of UK firms offering such schemes. Companies will be permitted to offer employees #3000-worth of "free" shares per annum, while for "10 key workers" in growing businesses the figure will be raised to #100,000.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Brown+feels+energy+tax+heat&amp;author=Wilson%2C+Steve%3Bsteve.wilson%40sundayherald.com&amp;issn=&amp;title=Sunday+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-07&amp;spage=24&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   THE CHANCELLOR Gordon Brown is expected to fudge the amount of money that the government anticipates raising from its controversial energy tax in his pre-budget statement on Tuesday.
Strident protests from industry have hampered civil servants' attempts to calculate how much the proposed energy tax might raise, especially since the criteria for exemptions have yet to be fixed.
By taxing carbon fuel, the government hopes it will encourage business to become more energy efficient. It maintains that the tax is necessary to ensure Britain meets international pledges to reduce CO2 emissions.
But companies that use large amounts of energy, notably in chemicals and heavy industries, have lobbied hard for a watering down of the proposals. They warn that the tax will mean massive increases in fuel bills, threatening both investment and jobs. Martin Gale, managing director of Irvine-based Caledonian Paper, warns that his company would suffer a #2 million hit if the climate change levy went ahead, which could lead to his factory's closure.
However, such claims have been derided as "scaremongering" by environmental lobby groups, which welcome Brown's proposed tax.
Earlier this year, Brown said that the so-called climate change levy would raise #1.75 billion, which he claimed would be redistributed to companies in the form of a 0.5% reduction in employers' national insurance contributions.
Companies are also hoping that Brown will on Tuesday announce the scrapping of the deeply unpopular "fuel price escalator". Originally introduced by the Conservatives, the measure means that tax on petrol and diesel rises by inflation plus 6% each year.
Industry is less confident that there will be a U-turn on the energy tax. Modest concessions have already been made by the Treasury, for example to exempt the chemical industry's more energy- hungry processes, but business fears that the government will plough on regardless of their criticisms.
Donald Adamson, tax partner at KPMG in Edinburgh, anticipates that the levy will be introduced from April 2001, with the rates applying to different types of fuel to be set in the forthcoming budget.
In his pre-budget statement, Gordon Brown will also flesh out proposals for tax credits on research and development conducted by small and medium-sized firms. Despite criticisms that they will do little to increase overall share ownership, he will also address the issue of all-employee share schemes, having earlier pledged to double the number of UK firms offering such schemes. Companies will be permitted to offer employees #3000-worth of "free" shares per annum, while for "10 key workers" in growing businesses the figure will be raised to #100,000.
But KPMG's Adamson warned that Brown might also instigate a "crackdown" on share schemes. There's a risk he will make it more difficult for companies to enjoy corporate tax deductions on gains made by employees that participate in qualifying share ownership trusts.

You've noticed that Americans are getting fatter, and that the government blames obesity and inactivity for more than 300,000 premature deaths every year. You've also noticed that scientists believe fatty foods kill people, and that fast-food outlets are selling cheeseburgers with both hands.

Petrol revenues `must be spent on public transport': [FOREIGN Edition]

Author: Harrabin, Roger

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/petrol-revenues-must-be-spent-on-public-transport/docview/311555106/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: THE GOVERNMENT'S "green" tax plans were criticised by its own experts as unfair and illogical yesterday. Worse, they do not do enough to protect the environment, the three members of the Academic Panel on Environmental Taxation said. If the Chancellor wants to continue with the 6 per cent escalating tax on petrol, he must spend the proceeds on public transport, the panel told Radio 4's Today programme, adding that plans for a climate- change levy will do disproportionate harm to small industries.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Petrol+revenues+%60must+be+spent+on+public+transport%27&amp;author=Harrabin%2C+Roger&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-06&amp;spage=13&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE GOVERNMENT'S "green" tax plans were criticised by its own experts as unfair and illogical yesterday. Worse, they do not do enough to protect the environment, the three members of the Academic Panel on Environmental Taxation said.
The panel's comments will be discomforting for Gordon Brown as he polishes the pre-Budget statement he will make next week.
If the Chancellor wants to continue with the 6 per cent escalating tax on petrol, he must spend the proceeds on public transport, the panel told Radio 4's Today programme, adding that plans for a climate- change levy will do disproportionate harm to small industries.
"The Government is right to see environmental taxation as a good idea," said the panel's chairman, Dr Dieter Helm, an Oxford economist. "But it also sees environmental taxes as a way of raising money and the two motives get muddled up."
The panel is scathing about the proposed climate-change levy, a tax on electricity which will be used to reduce businesses' national insurance contributions. But the tax will raise the same amount from heavily polluting coal as from clean gas. Even wind and solar power will be taxed at the same rate.
Dr Helm said the levy was "extremely badly designed". "If the purpose of the levy is to do something about carbon emissions, you should tax carbon."
The Government should plough the proceeds of the tax into improving energy efficiency in business, not cutting national insurance, said his colleague, Professor David Pearce, of London University. "Most heavily polluting industries are gaining exemptions from the levy anyway - the only people who'll pay the tax will be little industries."
The third panel member, Professor David Newbery of Cambridge, condemned the fuel-price escalator. Motorists are already paying for more than the damage they cause the environment, he said: "On purely environmental terms, the escalator should have stopped last year."
The Chancellor could make a case for the escalator by promising to spend the proceeds on public transport, Professor Pearce said.
The Government should also raise the tax on energy used in people's homes, a Conservative policy that Labour promised to scrap if elected. "What they should have done was to continue with the tax to increase the price of fuel, then use it to insulate the homes of the poor," said Professor Newbery.

People: Newbery, David

Ethical investment: Funds face shaming Friends of the Earth is threatening to expose the big unethical investors, reports Tony Levene

Author: Levene, Tony

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ethical-investment-funds-face-shaming-friends/docview/245470097/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mainsteam insurance and pension funds are in for a roasting in the new year. Green pressure group Friends of the Earth intends to name and shame big financial groups which invest in companies that harm the environment. FoE policy director Tony Juniper says the first move will be the publication of a report in early January which will state whether big groups such as Legal & General, Norwich Union and the Prudential are green or red. He says: `We're going to expose the insurance sector and show up those companies which derive dividends from companies causing gross environmental damage. We'll name both the funds and their investments.'

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Ethical+investment%3A+Funds+face+shaming+Friends+of+the+Earth+is+threatening+to+expose+the+big+unethical+investors%2C+reports+Tony+Levene&amp;author=Levene%2C+Tony&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-06&amp;spage=14&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Mainsteam insurance and pension funds are in for a roasting in the new year. Green pressure group Friends of the Earth intends to name and shame big financial groups which invest in companies that harm the environment.
FoE policy director Tony Juniper says the first move will be the publication of a report in early January which will state whether big groups such as Legal & General, Norwich Union and the Prudential are green or red.
He says: `We're going to expose the insurance sector and show up those companies which derive dividends from companies causing gross environmental damage. We'll name both the funds and their investments.'
Juniper believes that almost anyone with any money has some invested through pension or insurance policies with mainstream investment companies.
He adds: `Nearly all investors in their plans are concerned about green issues and support moves towards environmental best practice. We aim to mobilise them in a way that major fund management companies will not be at all comfortable with.'
But it won't all be the stick. FoE also intends proffering the carrot of self interest. It will tell financial companies that unless they use their shareholder voting power for green issues, they will suffer as much as any individual from climate change and environmental disaster.
Global warming will hit insurance company profits, for instance, because they will have to pay out more often for floods and other disasters.
But Juniper and FoE do not stop their probing at mainstream funds. In common with a growing number in the ethical and green investment market, they are questioning the role of specialist green and ethical funds and their future direction.
Ethical and environmental investing has come a long way since Friends Provident launched the first Stewardship fund a decade and a half ago. There are now some 30 funds with total assets approaching pounds 1.7bn - or around pounds 30 for every UK citizen. And that's before counting privately managed ethical funds for small pension plans and wealthy individuals.
But unlike most other investment areas, savers have to make two decisions before deciding to entrust their cash to a unit or investment trust. Besides working out whether a fund has competent managers - or whether a child with a pin could do better -they must also resolve just how deep their green streak runs or how ethical they want the fund to be. For there are huge variations between the ethics of the funds.
John Fleetwood of specialist IFA Ethical Investment Services, which is based in Preston, says that while many fund managers claim to adopt positive criteria to balance or outweigh negative factors such as animal test ing, pornography, military equipment production, tobacco and environmental issues, `in many instances, the evidence is far from compelling. It is relatively easy for a large company to implement a good equal opportunities policy and to donate a numerically large amount to charity. But it could remain to all intents and purposes, a very standard company'.
Sometimes, funds both condemn a company with one hand, and buy its shares with the other. The Murray Johnstone Ethical World Fund, a pounds 6m trust launched in May, has an `alert' on IBM because of military contracts, on Microsoft due to its far east operations and banks in general for indiscriminate lending.
But among the fund's top 10 holdings are Microsoft, IBM and NatWest.
This can lead to investor confusion, but it also allows the fund's managers to put pressure on company managers, rather than adopting the more usual stance of selling out and having no say through investor pressure.
Later this month, Jupiter is launching an Environmental Opportunities Fund. Instead of rejecting `sin' companies outright, it will adopt a `best of sector' approach which invests in any company that has a record of being the best for green and socially responsible performance in its class.
The fund could end up in investments which would be shunned by others. But fans of this approach argue that backing the best even in a less than savoury sector sends a message to the others - and gives green investment a toehold in the boardroom.

Brown's grey areas on tax: [1 Edition]

Author: Young, Alf

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/browns-grey-areas-on-tax/docview/332733674/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: But who's right? William Hague told the CBI this week that the big conflicts of the next century will be economic, not territorial, and that the victors' main weapons will be low tax rates, not guns and bombs. Is the present Chancellor an instinctive tax-cutter? Is Britain, under New Labour, well on its way to becoming one of those low-tax combatants in the economic wars of the 21st century? As the OECD row broke, a Treasury spokesman told the Press Association: "The tax burden is not rising. The tax burden is falling. The Budget Red Book shows the tax burden as 37.2% in 1998/99 and 36.6% in the coming year." There is one factual mistake and a barrow-load of spin in that answer. Turn to page 159 of the current Red Book, where the tax take is charted as a percentage of gross domestic product (GDP), and you will find that figure of 37.2% is the forecast for this year (1999/00) and not the outturn in 1998/99. There is, indeed, a fall to 36.6% predicted for next year (2000/01) but the commentary suggests it will be due to the phasing out of the windfall tax on the utilities and lower corporation tax receipts as a result of economic slowdown rather than any proactive tax-cutting mission by the Chancellor.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Brown%27s+grey+areas+on+tax&amp;author=Young%2C+Alf&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-05&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: 'TOTALLY untrue," was the Chancellor's curt response yesterday to a new report suggesting Britain's tax burden rose faster than other European countries' in year one of New Labour.
The survey, by the respected Paris-based Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, was seized on gleefully by those sections of the London press desperately seeking reasons to back William Hague. It was seized on even more gleefully by Mr Hague's party, hungry for fresh indictments on its "taxation by stealth" charge sheet against the government.
But who's right? William Hague told the CBI this week that the big conflicts of the next century will be economic, not territorial, and that the victors' main weapons will be low tax rates, not guns and bombs. Is the present Chancellor an instinctive tax-cutter? Is Britain, under New Labour, well on its way to becoming one of those low-tax combatants in the economic wars of the 21st century?
If Gordon Brown really is accusing the OECD of lying and the Opposition, in turn, is accusing the Chancellor of being economical with the actualite about his fiscal record, those of us who prefer facts and evidence to political bluster and spin need some robust answers to questions like these.
As the OECD row broke, a Treasury spokesman told the Press Association: "The tax burden is not rising. The tax burden is falling. The Budget Red Book shows the tax burden as 37.2% in 1998/99 and 36.6% in the coming year." There is one factual mistake and a barrow-load of spin in that answer.
Turn to page 159 of the current Red Book, where the tax take is charted as a percentage of gross domestic product (GDP), and you will find that figure of 37.2% is the forecast for this year (1999/00) and not the outturn in 1998/99. There is, indeed, a fall to 36.6% predicted for next year (2000/01) but the commentary suggests it will be due to the phasing out of the windfall tax on the utilities and lower corporation tax receipts as a result of economic slowdown rather than any proactive tax-cutting mission by the Chancellor.
And the projected fall is for one year only. Overall the trend is up. The picture of Britain and tax over the past 20 years is one of the tax take rising sharply as a percentage of GDP - remember Geoffrey Howe doubled VAT in the first Thatcher Budget - to a peak of 39% in 1982. From 1984 onwards, the total tax take fell steadily, bottoming out in 1994 at 33%. Apart from next year's projected fall, it has been rising ever since.
We should not be surprised. In his first three Budgets Gordon Brown may have cut headline rates of income and corporation tax. But the present Chancellor has proved himself very adept at clawing a lot more back by shrinking allowances and taking his secateurs to those parts of the fiscal undergrowth that few people, outside Whitehall and the accountancy profession, ever penetrate.
In his first Budget, in July 1997, Gordon Brown added around #6000m a year to total taxes. In his second Budget, in March 1998, he added a further #1210m by year three. Only last March, in his third Budget, did the Chancellor end his speech taking less in tax from the nation than before he stood up. And in that case the bulk of the largesse, #3555m of tax cuts, doesn't come into effect until the tax year starting in April 2001.
In the current financial year (1999/00) the overall effect of those three Brown Budgets is to push the tax take up by #5030m. So the comparative OECD survey comes as no surprise. The only real surprise is that the Chancellor, despite the clear evidence of his own Treasury's Red Books, wants to maintain the fiction that he is, first and foremost, a tax cutter.
Perhaps that's because he dare not give public credence to the other thing he is quite clearly about. The dreaded R word. Redistribution.
If you want to explore Gordon Brown's overall tax strategy to date, free of spin and counter-spin, there are tables in each of the first three Brown Red Books which cost each and every budget measure he's announced so far in terms of "Exchequer yield", a euphemism for tax take. These six pages of A4 will tell you more about this chancellor's fiscal strategy than acres of claim and counter-claim.
For industry, the story is one of carrot and stick. Apart from the windfall tax on the privatised utilities which has been used to try to get the young unemployed back into work, the headline-grabbing cuts in corporation tax (down 3p in the pound since Labour came to power) have largely been paid for by abolishing advance corporation tax and making companies pay their tax bills quarterly. Reducing employers' national insurance contributions from 2001 (cost #1700m) will be paid for by the proposed climate change levy (raising #1750m). And then there's the annual fuel price escalator.
For a chancellor who likes to be seen as pro-enterprise - he was detailing more tax breaks for small company entrepreneurs only this week - the overall picture is remarkably neutral. The big surprise is that so many businesses have swallowed the spin and moan only about red tape, not the tax burden.
It's only when you get down to taxes on individuals that the full redistributionist force of the three Brown budgets becomes clear. There are plenty of hits on property-owning, car-driving middle Britain, from hikes on stamp duty and that same fuel price escalator to the abolition of both the married couple's allowance and mortgage interest relief, and increases in the upper earnings limit on national insurance from next April.
There are carrots here, too - improved child benefit, the new 10p starting rate of income tax, and the cut in the basic rate to 22p next April. But most of the proceeds of the tax grab on individuals has been redirected at helping poorer families, particularly poorer families with children. The working families tax credit and the abolition of the entry rate for National Insurance contributions will cost a combined #2700m next year.
But, for Gordon Brown and this Labour Government, redistributionist thinking seems to go off the rails when it comes to the elderly. I'm not just thinking of the pathetic 73p a week rise planned, thanks to current low inflation levels, for the state pension. Or the even more miserable 25p rise that currently goes to the over-eighties.
I'm thinking back to Gordon Brown's biggest single budget raid of all on the nation's company pension schemes. According to his first Red Book, the chancellor is raising a staggering #5400m this year from his 1997 decision to abolish tax credits paid to pension schemes and UK companies.
Combined with the stricter funding requirements introduced by the 1995 Pensions Act, that one decision has put a lot of final salary pension schemes in some difficulty. Combined with tougher accounting rules for companies with pension schemes currently being floated by the Accounting Standards Board, these changes could push more companies into reconsidering whether they want to be in the business of providing pensions at all.
It may have seemed a great wheeze at the time to Brown's youthful team of advisers. But if you add in the impact of the current endowment mortgage scandal, which may leave hundreds of thousands of ageing British citizens with outstanding bills for their homes when their mortgage payments stop, the redistributionist and not-so-tax- cutting Mr Brown may find his Government has a grey Britain crisis on its hands before too long.

People: Brown, Gordon

Heavy Weather in Bonn

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/heavy-weather-bonn/docview/308417861/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Some prominent Europeans have complained to us recently about what they see as the "isolationist" attitudes of the Republicans who control the U.S. Senate. We don't buy that characterization for a minute. Major U.S. free trade initiatives of the last six years, for example, have had more backing from Republicans in the Congress than from Democrats and would not have been approved otherwise. The U.S. Senate has no official veto, of course, over what other countries want to do. But everybody knows major international initiatives like the Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty, the treaty establishing a permanent international criminal tribunal, and the Kyoto Protocol on global warming, are pretty much dead letters without American participation.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Heavy+Weather+in+Bonn&amp;author=&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-05&amp;spage=10&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Some prominent Europeans have complained to us recently about what they see as the "isolationist" attitudes of the Republicans who control the U.S. Senate. We don't buy that characterization for a minute. Major U.S. free trade initiatives of the last six years, for example, have had more backing from Republicans in the Congress than from Democrats and would not have been approved otherwise.
That doesn't address objections that the Republican Senate has been hostile to a recent string of proposed United Nations treaties. But even there, we venture an alternative hypothesis -- that the checks and balances enshrined in the U.S. Constitution are currently saving the rest of the world from its own worst impulses.
The U.S. Senate has no official veto, of course, over what other countries want to do. But everybody knows major international initiatives like the Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty, the treaty establishing a permanent international criminal tribunal, and the Kyoto Protocol on global warming, are pretty much dead letters without American participation.
These thoughts are spurred by the seeming irrelevance of the U.N. meeting on climate change currently taking place in Bonn. In terms of the number of countries and delegates participating, and in terms of the scope of the conference's ambition -- drastic reductions in global energy use -- it's just as big an event as the Rio and Kyoto conferences that preceded it. But it hasn't received even a tenth of the media attention. And we suspect that has something to do with the fact that its recommendations have barely a snowball's chance in a greenhouse hell of ever being implemented.
Not so long ago, of course, global warming was the crise celebre. Everybody who knew anything knew that drastic action was needed to curb climate change. U.S. Vice President Al Gore even once wrote grandly when he was a senator, "We must make the rescue of the environment the central organizing principle for civilization." Wow!
But then serious scientists swung into action and began to examine some of the U.N. claims. The most sensitive measuring systems couldn't detect any warming of the earth's atmosphere in the second half of this century. The computer models used to predict future warming were ridiculously simplistic. And the costs of stabilizing atmospheric carbon dioxide levels, if it could be done at all, were, if you'll forgive us, stratospheric.
Sensible Americans started to realize that the best insurance policy is keeping the world rich enough in economic terms to adapt to any changes they might encounter in the future. Even if Mr. Gore manages to be elected president next year, and the Democrats capture the Senate, America is not likely to support a global economic suicide pact.
There are some who claim carbon dioxide targets can be painlessly achieved by using energy more "efficiently." But that scenario rests on the dubious premise that greedy capitalists who will do everything else to make a buck are carelessly wasting money on energy use.
So here's our prediction for the future of the Kyoto debate. The global climate change industry -- activists, ministers and bureaucrats -- will go on as usual, jetting around the world to conferences lamenting the fact that politicians are too short-sighted to do anything "for the children." But even European governments, for whom "austerity" means redistributing only 50% of the national wealth, will realize that the costs of "doing something" are prohibitive. Naturally, of course, they'll publicly blame the U.S. for their inaction. But privately, we suspect, there will be a few sighs of relief in European capitals. Saved again from another dumb treaty.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Global Warming Is Definitely Real" -- WSJE Dec. 14, 1999)

Subject: Global warming; Treaties; Climate change

Title: Heavy Weather in Bonn

column: REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)

Source type: Newspaper

Climate hots up for government: ENVIRONMENT VIEWPOINT PAUL EKINS: How committed is the UK to countering global warming? Ministers must soon make up their minds how they're going to achieve their targets: [London edition]

Author: Ekins, Paul

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-hots-up-government-environment-viewpoint/docview/248793123/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Its consultation paper on the subject a year ago accepted climate change as "one of the greatest environmental threats facing the world today". Since then, a shower of further consultation papers has followed: on the proposed climate change levy, announced at the time of the last Budget in March, on renewable energy, in the same month, and on the new home energy efficiency scheme, in May. The consultation periods are drawing to a close. The government must soon make up its mind. The first indication of its thinking will be revealed next Tuesday when it responds, in its pre-Budget report, to feedback on the proposed levy. Business has waged an unremitting campaign against the measure over the past six months. On the face of it this is curious, for the government is proposing to return to business, through cuts in employers' National Insurance contributions or incentives for improved energy efficiency, all the revenues raised from the levy. "Significant losers" - the sectors whose net cost is more than 0.1 per cent of turnover - employ only 7 per cent of the workforce and generate 10 per cent of the UK's value-added.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+hots+up+for+government%3A+ENVIRONMENT+VIEWPOINT+PAUL+EKINS%3A+How+committed+is+the+UK+to+countering+global+warming%3F+Ministers+must+soon+make+up+their+minds+how+they%27re+going+to+achieve+their+targets&amp;author=Ekins%2C+Paul&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-04&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A moment of truth is approaching for the government as it draws together the threads of its strategy for addressing climate change.
Its consultation paper on the subject a year ago accepted climate change as "one of the greatest environmental threats facing the world today". Since then, a shower of further consultation papers has followed: on the proposed climate change levy, announced at the time of the last Budget in March, on renewable energy, in the same month, and on the new home energy efficiency scheme, in May. The consultation periods are drawing to a close. The government must soon make up its mind.
The first indication of its thinking will be revealed next Tuesday when it responds, in its pre-Budget report, to feedback on the proposed levy. Business has waged an unremitting campaign against the measure over the past six months. On the face of it this is curious, for the government is proposing to return to business, through cuts in employers' National Insurance contributions or incentives for improved energy efficiency, all the revenues raised from the levy.
The laws of mathematics dictate that winnings from the levy must exactly balance the losses. However, a detailed sectoral study by Ecotec*, the Birmingham-based environment consultancy, using data from the Office for National Statistics, shows some sectors would be winners and others losers.
In total, sectors employing 67 per cent of the UK workforce, and generating 68 per cent of the economy's value-added, would show a net benefit from the levy.
"Significant losers" - the sectors whose net cost is more than 0.1 per cent of turnover - employ only 7 per cent of the workforce and generate 10 per cent of the UK's value-added.
These figures suggest the shift of taxation that the levy would bring about could substantially benefit the economy as a whole.
The levy will perform an important function in signalling to business that, whatever the vagaries of power at the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries, energy prices are set on an upward trend, which should encourage companies to look more seriously for cost-effective energy-efficiency measures than many do at present. By itself, however, it will fall far short of reducing carbon emissions by the required amount to meet the government's target of a 20 per cent cut from 1990's level by 2010.
A study by Cambridge Econometrics and Forum for the Future**, published today, explores where such carbon savings might come from, and the economic implications of achieving them.
The first conclusion is that the government will not come near its target without substantial new policy measures directed towards all carbon-emitting sectors, namely industry, transport and households. The lead times before such measures become effective mean that they need to be implemented soon if they are to deliver their possible carbon savings by 2010. Government action must closely follow the end of consultation if the target is not to slide out of reach.
The second conclusion from the study is that, given the requisite new policies, the 20 per cent target is eminently attainable. The "20 per cent reduction" policy package in the study includes the generation of 15 per cent of the UK's electricity needs from renewables, and the increase of combined heat and power capacity to 10GWe (gigawatts equivalent) by 2010; a government investment of £3.5bn in domestic energy efficiency over the next 10 years; and taxes on fossil-fuel use by business and in transport.
Carbon reduction is not the only benefit of this policy package. Sulphur emissions are reduced by 12.5 per cent, ensuring that the UK's sulphur commitments for 2010 are satisfactorily met.
The investment in renewable energy should ensure that the UK acquires a vigorous industrial capability in what is widely forecast to be among the fastest growing sectors in the next century. And the improvement in domestic energy efficiency substantially reduces fuel poverty, and would save many of the 30,000 lives that are lost each year because poor people cannot afford to keep warm.
It would be surprising if these benefits came completely free, and they do not. Economic growth would slow from an average 2.56 per cent per year to 2.54 per cent per year between 2000 and 2010. Gross domestic product in 2010 would be 0.28 per cent below what it would have been without the carbon-reduction measures. Put another way, with these policies the economy reaches in mid-February 2011 the same size which, without them, it would have attained at the end of 2010.
Is a six-week delay in economic gratification of this kind justified by the UK's achieving global leadership in addressing the world's worst environmental threat, reducing cross-border acid rain, stopping thousands of people dying of hypothermia and kick-starting a new industry with global potential?
If the UK government thinks not, then its rhetoric of support for sustainable development, published in the sustainable development strategy in May this year, will be revealed as a complete sham. The vigour with which it pursues its target for a 20 per cent reduction in carbon emissions will act as a litmus test of its sustainable development commitment overall. The results of the test will soon be available. * Who Gains from the Climate Change Levy?, Ecotec, Birmingham ** The 20 per cent Solution: Meeting the Government's Targets on Climate Change, Forum for the Future, London
The writer is programme director, Forum for the Future and reader in environmental policy, Keele University Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Take the fiscal neutral route to energy efficiency: [London edition]

Author: Temple, Martin

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/take-fiscal-neutral-route-energy-efficiency/docview/248762711/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Sir, There is a glaring omission in your article "Energy levy plans fuel big consumers' anger" (October 28). The climate change levy, as proposed by the government, will impact on all manufacturing and engineering companies irrespective of size.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Take+the+fiscal+neutral+route+to+energy+efficiency&amp;author=Temple%2C+Martin&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-03&amp;spage=24&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: From Mr Martin Temple.
Sir, There is a glaring omission in your article "Energy levy plans fuel big consumers' anger" (October 28). The climate change levy, as proposed by the government, will impact on all manufacturing and engineering companies irrespective of size.
Groups representing energy-intensive users have put forward strong arguments since their companies will attract the largest levy. Small and medium-sized companies and those using less energy which are outside the Integrated Pollution Prevention Control regime and not subject to negotiated agreements, will also be heavily penalised.
This is best illustrated if you look at a typical example of an engineering company employing about 300 people with an annual energy bill of £750,000 ($1.24m). Under the government's proposals, that company would incur a levy in the region of £110,000, offset by only £19,000 in National Insurance contribution rebate. Affected by up to £90,000, it will be less able to invest in energy-efficient technologies.
The EEF fully endorses the government's commitment to meet its Kyoto targets. There are, however, alternative ways of devising a levy. Extending the opportunity for negotiated agreements will allow more companies to invest in energy efficiency improvements. Capital allowances or levy credits will direct the levy back to those companies which embark on relevant capital expenditure. By achieving near fiscal neutrality, they would be better able to maintain their international competitiveness.
Martin Temple, Director-General, Engineering Employers' Federation, Broadway House, Tothill Street, London SW1H 9NQ, UK Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Risk from having no 'green' nous COMPETITIVENESS ON WEDNESDAY: [London edition]

Author: Brown, Kevin; Wighton, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/risk-having-no-green-nous-competitiveness-on/docview/248761821/se-2?accountid=10226

Full text: Risk from having no 'green' nous
John Gummer, the former Conservative cabinet minister, also popped up at the CBI conference to warn that there was another side to the debate over the proposed climate change levy. Businesses are up in arms about the levy, especially heavy consumers of electricity or gas. But Gummer, at the heart of government for years, says many companies face real problems because they lack environmental awareness. Financial services companies, in particular, are taking big risks by not giving higher priority to the environmental policies of clients and investments, says Gummer. "There are several firms I wouldn't invest in because I know there are some very nasty things around the corner," he says. "One of the important things to change these attitudes is for the insurance, banking and financial institutions to realise it's they that will suffer and . . . it's their customers and stakeholders that will be hit if they don't take on things as big as climate change."
* Today will see the government under renewed pressure in the Commons over its plans to crack down on alleged tax avoidance by consultants, especially in computing. The Treasury has amended the so-called IR35 rules designed to prevent employees avoiding National Insurance by setting themselves up as companies. But Vincent Cable, the Liberal Democrat's industry spokesman, says the change will only lift the threat to "fat cats" such as partners in big City firms. "Computer contractors will still be clobbered very hard and it threatens to have a serious impact on the IT sector." Mr Cable points to the "jarring contrast" with Gordon Brown's announcement this week of tax breaks on options for entrepreneurs. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Comment & Analysis: Analysis:The big clean-up Today the CBI will discuss the introduction of the energy levy on businesses. The horse-trading on discounts will soon follow

Author: Cowe, Roger

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/comment-analysis-big-clean-up-today-cbi-will/docview/245454784/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: British business is in a tizz about the tax on its use of energy which Gordon Brown plans to introduce in the next budget. Delegates at the annual conference of the Confederation of British Industry, gathering in Birmingham this morning, are worried as usual about interest rates and the pound. But nothing is exercising them more or has challenged the government's business-friendly credentials as much as the climate change levy. This new tax will not swell the government's coffers nor will it go far to meeting the UK's target for reducing the carbon dioxide emissions believed to cause global warming. From industry's point of view, most companies will be winners, because the tax will be offset by lower national insurance charges. For most who lose, the pain will be minimal, especially if it stimulates them to save energy. The likely starting tax rate of 0.6p per kilowatt hour is less than a 10th of what energy costs, and for most companies they form only a small part of their total bills. It was originally announced in this year's budget, to be introduced in 2001. The near pounds 2bn to be raised is to be `recycled' in the form of lower national insurance charges for all employers. Business opposition was also supposed to be defused by the way the treasury was following a report by Lord Marshall - the British Airways chairman and past president of the CBI. Also, the government's advisory committee on business and the environment (ACBE), packed with executives from Shell, BP, Amoco and BT, had said in March last year that tax could have a role to play in reducing energy use.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Comment+%26+Analysis%3A+Analysis%3AThe+big+clean-up+Today+the+CBI+will+discuss+the+introduction+of+the+energy+levy+on+businesses.+The+horse-trading+on+discounts+will+soon+follow&amp;author=Cowe%2C+Roger&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-11-01&amp;spage=15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: British business is in a tizz about the tax on its use of energy which Gordon Brown plans to introduce in the next budget. Delegates at the annual conference of the Confederation of British Industry, gathering in Birmingham this morning, are worried as usual about interest rates and the pound. But nothing is exercising them more or has challenged the government's business-friendly credentials as much as the climate change levy.
This new tax will not swell the government's coffers nor will it go far to meeting the UK's target for reducing the carbon dioxide emissions believed to cause global warming. From industry's point of view, most companies will be winners, because the tax will be offset by lower national insurance charges. For most who lose, the pain will be minimal, especially if it stimulates them to save energy. The likely starting tax rate of 0.6p per kilowatt hour is less than a 10th of what energy costs, and for most companies they form only a small part of their total bills.
But businesses hate taxes, no matter how small, partly because they fear the rate of tax may be increased, once the principle has been established. They also point out that the carbon dioxide savings from the levy cannot be guaranteed. Their preferred alternative is legally binding targets on energy use and emissions, backed up by a system to trade permits so that the reductions are achieved at lowest cost.
For the government, the levy is a first move towards taxing `bads', such as pollution, instead of employment. It is a clear statement that all businesses must strive to save energy and so reduce the country's carbon output.
It was originally announced in this year's budget, to be introduced in 2001. The near pounds 2bn to be raised is to be `recycled' in the form of lower national insurance charges for all employers. Business opposition was also supposed to be defused by the way the treasury was following a report by Lord Marshall - the British Airways chairman and past president of the CBI. Also, the government's advisory committee on business and the environment (ACBE), packed with executives from Shell, BP, Amoco and BT, had said in March last year that tax could have a role to play in reducing energy use.
Industry was not placated, however. The recycling promise caused trouble because it has been widely misinterpreted to mean that no individual company, or no industry sector, would be worse off. This idea was always a nonsense. Mr Brown flagged his new measure as a way of shifting taxation from employment to resource use. The inevitable consequence was that industries such as steel, paper and chemical manufacture would lose out, while the service sector would benefit.
Industry has scored some points, however. Most significant has been the accusation that this really is not an environmental measure at all, just the treasury up to its old tricks of meddling in the economy.
To start with, the scale of carbon dioxide savings is trivial, certainly compared with what industry, especially chemicals, has already achieved by way of energy savings. Political considerations have played their part. No discrimination is being made between power generated from nuclear energy or coal, despite their different impacts on climate change. Renewable power from the wind and sun are subject to the levy as well, even though these are the very energy sources which industry should be encouraged to take up.
Industry has also complained, not unreasonably, that it is the sole target of the tax, despite the huge potential for saving in domestic energy consumption. But the government has been determined to avoid its predecessor's fate when Kenneth Clarke tried to push up VAT on domestic energy.
Industry also points out that if the government really wants companies to save energy it should use much more than the planned pounds 50m-a-year to support energy- saving investments, instead of using most of the pounds 1.75bn to cut national insurance. Will it even boost jobs? The chemicals industry, for example, claims more than 5,000 jobs are at risk and one economic analysis suggests employment throughout the economy will fall over a 10-year period because of the levy. (On the other side, envi ronmentalists have produced equally meticulous analyses showing there will be a significant overall boost to jobs.)
Different assumptions produce different results but it is clear that the majority of businesses will gain and most of those which will lose will suffer a pretty insignificant extra cost even if they do not manage to reduce their energy consumption. Even the chemical industry claims that its 170 companies, with sales of more than pounds 30bn, will have to pay only an extra pounds 50m in total - less than 0.2% of sales.
Ecotec, for the Worldwide Fund for Nature, concluded that the tax will be neutral or positive for companies employing more than 90% of the country's workforce. And it suggested that the sectors which will suffer will incur very limited extra costs - food manufacturing was identified as one of the biggest losers but the cost would be only 0.15% of the sector's sales.
Lord Marshall had suggested that heavy users of energy be given a discount on the levy. A figure of 50% was suggested, but the CBI has argued that in these special circumstance the discount should be as much as 95%. Such a huge discount, effectively exempting them from the tax, seems out of the question. But Mr Brown is likely to make some concessions, possibly increasing the discount to 75% and widening the range of companies which can claim it.
He will also probably bow to environmental pressure to exempt renewable energy sources, and give government support to the trading of emissions permits, the tool favoured by big business for meeting the climate change target. The levy will stay, however, to the chagrin of the CBI.
Roger Cowe writes on business and the environment
Behind the levy
Under the Kyoto agreement of 1997, the UK's emissions have to be cut to 189m tonnes by 2010, a reduction of 12.5%
Labour's 1997 manifesto upped this promise to 20%
Changes in energy use look likely to have cut emissions to the Kyoto level by 2000 but are forecast to rise to about 194m tonnes of carbon by 2010
The levy should reduce emissions by 1.5m tonnes by 2010

Whiners get help -- but not taxpayers: [National Edition]

Author: Terence Corcoran Financial Post Editor

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/whiners-get-help-not-taxpayers/docview/329534847/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Trick or treat! Sorry, no treat -- just tricks, about $47- billion worth. Remember the Speech from the Throne, with Adrienne Clarkson dressed up as Snow White promising to keep us all wrapped up and warm through the next session of Parliament? Well, that wasn't Snow White. That was the wicked Chretien cabinet, and that speech was a coded blueprint for a $32-billion spending extravaganza. Even the most optimistic forecasters who have looked at the Liberals' 50-50 plan say the tax-cut potential is hopelessly inadequate. Royal Bank economist John McCallum, in a recent study, said that Liberal formula would at best produce a "trivial" reduction in personal income taxes and minor fiddles with employment- insurance premiums and business taxes. At most, Canadians might see only a 5% cut in their income taxes by 2004. Black & White Photo: Colin Price, the Province / John McCallum, Royal Bank economist, said the Liberals' 50-50 plan would at best produce a "trivial" reduction in personal income taxes. ;

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Whiners+get+help+--+but+not+taxpayers&amp;author=Terence+Corcoran+Financial+Post+Editor&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-30&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Trick or treat! Sorry, no treat -- just tricks, about $47- billion worth. Remember the Speech from the Throne, with Adrienne Clarkson dressed up as Snow White promising to keep us all wrapped up and warm through the next session of Parliament? Well, that wasn't Snow White. That was the wicked Chretien cabinet, and that speech was a coded blueprint for a $32-billion spending extravaganza.
And remember all the talk recently about a fat fiscal dividend coming out of the big surpluses Ottawa was building? Well, don't waste time figuring out how Ottawa could spend the money. It's going, going, gone. According to the numbers in the Finance Department's Halloween bag of "spending pressures" seeping out of cabinet, the government is already on the brink of allocating most of the spending portion of the surplus for the next five years.
Forget, too, about significant income tax cuts. Under these unplugged programs, federal spending stays high for the next five years and opportunities for tax reduction are sharply reduced. If the economy gets into any kind of trouble, tax cut options could dry up altogether.
In addition to the $32-billion packed into the Throne Speech, there's another $15-billion in fresh money set to dribble away into dozens of classic Liberal sops to the nation's whiners.
They include $250-million for feature film support, $250-million for sound recordings, $750-million for housing and more than $1- billion for another experimental employment-insurance drain.
Most of the spending binge is unnecessary. You have to really work as a government to concoct these plans to spend money. We don't need $1.5-billion to develop a climate change strategy, $500- million for weather services, $1-billion for "lifelong learning" initiatives, or millions more for such economic losers as Atomic Energy of Canada.
None of these numbers appear to be nailed down, but they have a pretty solid feel to them. And the $47-billion total, give or take a couple of billion, makes it clear that the Chretien government is going to stick to its famous plan: spend first, cut taxes if we have to later if there's any money left. At this rate, there might not be.
Over the next five years, according to most forecasts, Ottawa's surpluses could add up to about $90-billion, assuming the economy grows at a steady 4.5% clip and nothing happens to drive interest rates crazy or revenues suddenly down. The Chretien government's mantra has been that it will spend 50% of the projected surpluses and use the other 50% to either cut taxes or pay down some debt.
Even the most optimistic forecasters who have looked at the Liberals' 50-50 plan say the tax-cut potential is hopelessly inadequate. Royal Bank economist John McCallum, in a recent study, said that Liberal formula would at best produce a "trivial" reduction in personal income taxes and minor fiddles with employment- insurance premiums and business taxes. At most, Canadians might see only a 5% cut in their income taxes by 2004.
If the leaked ministerial priorities emerge as the Liberal plan, the chances of even that small cut in taxes are sharply reduced. With half the surplus spent from the start, there's no real cushion to make sure that tax cuts can be delivered. Any surprises -- interest rate shocks, slowdowns, revenue disruptions -- would eat into the tax cut or debt-reduction option.
When Paul Martin delivers his fiscal update next Tuesday, we won't see any of these long-term spending priorities. Even his budgets stick to the short term, keeping us all in the dark lest we actually figure out what's going on. Behind the scenes, however, it's clear that new spending is at the top of the government's agenda. How many cabinet ministers have submitted their tax- reduction priorities? Maybe that list will be in the next leak.
John McCallum, Royal Bank economist, said the Liberals' 50-50 plan would at best produce a "trivial" reduction in personal income taxes.
Illustration Black & White Photo: Colin Price, the Province / John McCallum, Royal Bank economist, said the Liberals' 50-50 plan would at best produce a "trivial" reduction in personal income taxes. ;

TRIBUNAL WEIGHING GENOCIDE CHARGES AGAINST SERB LEADER: [FINAL EDITION]

Author: Compiled by Dave Hirschman from these news services: The Associated Press, Los Angeles Times-Washington Post, The New York Times and Scripps Howard.

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/tribunal-weighing-genocide-charges-against-serb/docview/393853465/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: PRISTINA, Yugoslavia - President Slobodan Milosevic may face more criminal charges, including genocide, in connection with the bloody crackdown on ethnic Albanians in Kosovo, the chief prosecutor of the international war crimes tribunal said Thursday. Carla del Ponte told reporters that the tribunal, based in The Hague, Netherlands was considering additional charges that included "offenses committed in Croatia and Bosnia-Herzegovina, as well as offenses committed in Kosovo during 1998, including the charge of genocide."
