Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=TRIBUNAL+WEIGHING+GENOCIDE+CHARGES+AGAINST+SERB+LEADER&amp;author=Compiled+by+Dave+Hirschman+from+these+news+services%3A+The+Associated+Press%2C+Los+Angeles+Times-Washington+Post%2C+The+New+York+Times+and+Scripps+Howard.&amp;issn=07454856&amp;title=The+Commercial+Appeal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-29&amp;spage=A.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: NEWS IN BRIEF
PRISTINA, Yugoslavia - President Slobodan Milosevic may face more criminal charges, including genocide, in connection with the bloody crackdown on ethnic Albanians in Kosovo, the chief prosecutor of the international war crimes tribunal said Thursday.
Carla del Ponte told reporters that the tribunal, based in The Hague, Netherlands was considering additional charges that included "offenses committed in Croatia and Bosnia-Herzegovina, as well as offenses committed in Kosovo during 1998, including the charge of genocide."
Tribunal investigators have been in Kosovo gathering evidence for the cases since June 12, when NATO sent in peacekeepers after a 78- day bombing campaign against Yugoslavia.
House panel backs cancer treatment
WASHINGTON - Women now eligible for free mammograms and Pap smears also could get help paying for cancer treatment under legislation approved by a House committee Thursday.
"Early detection is wonderful, but it has to be accompanied by medical treatment," said Rep. Karen McCarthy (D-Mo.), a supporter of the bill.
Nearly 800,000 women nationwide have received free mammograms to screen for breast cancer and free Pap smears to screen for cervical cancer under a program created by Congress in 1990 to help those who lack private health insurance.
But thousands who have discovered that they have cancer lack insurance to help pay for treatment. The cancer treatment would cost the federal government $315 million over five years.
Third Arab nation forms ties to Israel
WASHINGTON - Over Arab and Palestinian objections, Mauritania made history Thursday by establishing full diplomatic relations with Israel in a show of support for peacemaking in the Middle East.
"It is a sovereign decision, taken by us as a sovereign state," Mauritania's foreign minister, Ahmed Ould sid Ahmed, said after exchanging documents with David Levy, the Israeli foreign minister, at a State Department ceremony. "We hope that this decision will foster efforts to arrive at a solution."
The northwest African nation is only the third Arab country to have full relations with Israel. The two others are Egypt and Jordan.
Witness confesses, halts execution
COLUMBIA, S.C. - The execution of a convicted cop killer was blocked Thursday by the South Carolina Supreme Court, a week after a woman recanted her trial testimony and said she was the one who pulled the trigger.
Richard Charles Johnson, 36, was scheduled to be executed by injection Friday for the 1985 shooting death of a state trooper on Interstate 95.
The court's five justices stayed the execution, saying they needed more time to examine material submitted both by Johnson's lawyers and prosecutors. The justices promised to rule by Nov. 10.
Late last week Connie Hess, who was with Johnson that day and whose testimony was used against him at two trials, said it was she and not Johnson who killed the trooper.
Israel deports suspected zealots
JERUSALEM - In its third operation against Christian doomsday groups in a year, Israel on Thursday began deporting 20 foreigners suspected of planning violent acts during the millennium year.
With Christian pilgrims heading to Israel for the celebration of the birth of Jesus, Israeli police have been on alert for religious fanatics who believe they can facilitate the second coming of Christ through violent acts or mass suicide. A special police unit has been established to deal with the threat.
On Monday, Israel arrested 17 Americans, two Britons and two Australians, all members of the House of Prayer and Solomon's Temple groups, on the Mount of Olives.
Climate changes rapidly, study says
WASHINGTON - An ice age that gripped the Earth for thousands of years ended abruptly 15,000 years ago when temperatures soared, according to new findings that suggest the world's climate can change in a few decades.
The study prompts fresh concerns that climate change from global warming could happen suddenly, experts say.
"There was a 16-degree abrupt warming at the end of the last ice age," said Jeffrey Severinghaus, lead author of a study to be published today in the journal Science. "It happened within just a couple of decades. The old idea was that the temperature would change over a thousand years. But we found it was much faster."
BRIEFLY
-- The Justice Department filed suit Thursday to strip a retired Pennsylvania mechanic of his citizenship on grounds he served as a Nazi SS guard at three concentration camps during World War II. The department's Nazi-hunting Office of Special Investigations alleged that Theodor Szehinskyj, born in Poland in 1924, was a guard at Gross- Rosen Concentration Camp, Warsaw Concentration Camp and Sachsenshausen Concentration Camp.
-- President Clinton conceded Thursday that his impeachment scandal may hurt Vice President Gore's election hopes, acknowledging that "a lot of people who may not like me may hold it against him."
-- A man convicted of raping and killing a 94-year-old woman was executed by injection Thursday in Texas. Domingo Cantu, 31, was the 28th inmate executed in the state this year and the 82nd nationwide, the most since 83 were put to death in 1952.

Energy levy plans fuel big consumers' anger: [London edition]

Author: Houlder, Vanessa

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/energy-levy-plans-fuel-big-consumers-anger/docview/248867361/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Critics argue that the levy, which ministers proposed in March, could damage the manufacturing industry, cost thousands of jobs and fail to help the environment. But the government believes that the tax will bring clear environmental benefits. It would, it claims, help the country meet its short-term goals on stabilising greenhouse gas emissions and put it in a stronger position to meet the increasingly stringent targets needed to delay catastrophic climate change. Moreover, ministers stress that the proposal is revenue-neutral. Illustrative figures suggest that it would raise £1.75bn in its first full year from April 2001, which would be fully offset by cuts in employers' national insurance contributions from 12.2 per cent to 11.7.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Energy+levy+plans+fuel+big+consumers%27+anger&amp;author=Houlder%2C+Vanessa&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-28&amp;spage=05&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Plans to tax the energy used by businesses has unleashed a barrage of criticism against the government.
Critics argue that the levy, which ministers proposed in March, could damage the manufacturing industry, cost thousands of jobs and fail to help the environment.
But the government believes that the tax will bring clear environmental benefits. It would, it claims, help the country meet its short-term goals on stabilising greenhouse gas emissions and put it in a stronger position to meet the increasingly stringent targets needed to delay catastrophic climate change.
Moreover, ministers stress that the proposal is revenue-neutral. Illustrative figures suggest that it would raise £1.75bn in its first full year from April 2001, which would be fully offset by cuts in employers' national insurance contributions from 12.2 per cent to 11.7.
The Treasury has indicated that this would imply a levy of 0.21p per kWh for coal and gas and 0.6p per kWh for electricity.
In March, it suggested that energy intensive industries that negotiated efficiency improvements with the government would be offered a 50 per cent reduction in the rate of the levy.
Big industrial energy users such as steel and chemicals companies have been leading the protests against the levy, arguing for discounts of up to 95 per cent. They have high energy costs, and argue that they are doing all they can to improve efficiency.
The levy, they claim, would merely make them uncompetitive against their overseas rivals and, according to research by Business Strategies, would cost 156,000 jobs over the next 10 years.
These high consumers of energy are infuriated by the government's insistance that the levy is revenue neutral, and point out that cuts in national insurance contributions benefit large employers, notably in the public sector, at the expense of manufacturers that tend to be less labour intensive.
However, Michael Meacher, environment minister, says experience has shown that key industrial sectors can typically save 15-20 per cent of their energy costs if they focus on the issue. The tax would change the perception that energy prices were going to go on falling, which has reduced the incentive to introduce energy efficiency measures.
While acknowledging that some companies will lose under the levy, Mr Meacher says that some important industrial sectors are net gainers including telecommunications, pharmaceuticals and construction.
He cites research by Cambridge Econometrics, an economic research group, that suggested a net job gain of 14,000 jobs from the levy.
While the government has found few supporters for the tax in industry, environmentalists have rallied round.
In a joint letter to the chancellor this summer, the directors of the UK's main environmental organisations argued that "the levy sends a clear signal to business, and to wider society, that we are serious about tackling climate change.
"We would urge you to stand firm on your proposals," they said. "Future generations will thank you." Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Call for energy tax concession NEWS DIGEST: [London edition]

Author: O'Connor, Gillian

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/call-energy-tax-concession-news-digest/docview/248847286/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Call+for+energy+tax+concession+NEWS+DIGEST%3A&amp;author=O%27Connor%2C+Gillian&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-28&amp;spage=42&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: ALUMINIUM
Call for energy tax concession
Paul Rata of British Alcan and President of the Aluminium Federation made a plea for further UK energy tax concessions at the federation's annual dinner last night. "We are pleased that energy used in electrolysis for primary aluminium production is now exempt from the levy, but this does not go far enough, leaving the balance of our industry critically exposed. It would logically follow therefore that if the government wishes to protect primary production and also wishes to encourage recycling, then energy used in recycling should also be exempt. We also believe that energy consumption required for environmental protection measures should be excluded from the levy."
When first announced by the UK chancellor in March the Climate Change Levy was presented as neutral to business as a whole: it would cost £1.7bn, but would be balanced by a reduction in National Insurance Contributions (Nics). However, it looks likely to cost the UK aluminium industry £40m a year, with only £2m coming back through reductions in national insurance contributions. Most of the burden would have fallen on the primary smelters, which would have faced "certain closure", said Mr Rata. That threat has now been removed, but the industry is hoping for additional exemptions in the chancellor's pre-Budget report early next month. Gillian O'Connor, Mining Correspondent Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Rebates expected to ease L1.75bn tax blow CLIMATE CHANGE LEVY: [London edition]

Author: Houlder, Vanessa; Wighton, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/rebates-expected-ease-l1-75bn-tax-blow-climate/docview/248814772/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Gordon Brown, the chancellor, is expected to soften the blow of the £1.75bn climate change levy by offering larger rebates for energy intensive industries when he announces the details next month. Although industry leaders have been concerned by the Treasury's attitude during negotiations over the levy, government insiders say the Treasury has taken on board warnings about the impact of the proposals on competitiveness. The government had suggested that discounts of up to 50 per cent could be available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Rebates+expected+to+ease+L1.75bn+tax+blow+CLIMATE+CHANGE+LEVY%3A&amp;author=Houlder%2C+Vanessa%3BWighton%2C+David&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-28&amp;spage=05&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Gordon Brown, the chancellor, is expected to soften the blow of the £1.75bn climate change levy by offering larger rebates for energy intensive industries when he announces the details next month.
Although industry leaders have been concerned by the Treasury's attitude during negotiations over the levy, government insiders say the Treasury has taken on board warnings about the impact of the proposals on competitiveness.
"It has been a genuine consultation and the Treasury recognises the need for flexibility in efforts to meet our environmental targets," said one government member.
The Confederation of British Industry and individual sector lobby groups have been pressing for bigger discounts for energy intensive industries that negotiate energy efficiency improvements. The government had suggested that discounts of up to 50 per cent could be available.
But the CBI has argued that in return for discounts of up to 95 per cent, industries could offer demanding energy efficiency programmes that would allow the government to meet its environmental targets.
It also urged the government to cut the size of the levy so that, when overset by proposed cuts in national insurance contributions, the impact on individual sectors would be revenue neutral.
Stephen Byers, trade and industry secretary, told industry leaders he would argue their case in government. Environment ministers also offered a sympathetic hearing. But industry lobby groups have complained about what appears to be intransigence from the Treasury. There has also been frustration that the Treasury has declined to be involved in negotiations between individual industry groups and the environment department.
"There has been a distinct lack of 'joined-up government' on this," said one industrialist.
The government has been surprised by the strength of opposition to the tax. "Ministers thought they would be able to get away with it with relatively little problem like the windfall tax on the utilities," said one Labour MP.
"But industry felt they were unfairly being singled out given that big polluters like cars and domestic fuel were excluded for political reasons."
Meanwhile the CBI yesterday called on the government to offer additional tax incentives to encourage the development of a broadly based trading scheme in greenhouse gases.
It outlined a vision of an ambitious scheme that would provide a cost-effective way to cut greenhouse gas emissions. The scheme could eventually embrace a large swathe of British industry and could potentially encourage businesses to invest in environmental projects in the domestic and transport sectors.
A small-scale version of the scheme, backed by 25 large companies, could begin running from April 2001, the CBI said. Companies would volunteer for binding emissions limits and then trade together to ensure limits were met in the most cost-effective way. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Weather costs Vt. dearly, study says

Author: Sutkoski, Matt

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/weather-costs-vt-dearly-study-says/docview/873451044/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The burning of fossil fuels adds carbon dioxide to the atmosphere, and that gas traps heat, warming the earth, scientists said.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Weather+costs+Vt.+dearly%2C+study+says&amp;author=Sutkoski%2C+Matt&amp;issn=08948844&amp;title=The+Burlington+Free+Press&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-27&amp;spage=B.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   By Matt Sutkoski
Vermont was among the states most negatively affected by weather disasters in 1998, a study by a national advocacy group indicates. The report is providing fodder for an upcoming conference on the economic effects of climate change in the state.
The study, due for release today, shows that Vermont had the sixth greatest financial losses per capita due to natural disasters, according to the U.S. Public Interest Research Group Education Fund. The rank was based on federal disaster aid figures and insurance losses. The group said global warming is creating a rising number of weather extremes and disasters throughout the United States.
A January ice storm and a series of summertime flash floods made 1998 a particularly bad year in Vermont. The state received $$@$!14.5 million in federal disaster relief, said Joan Lindsay of the Vermont Emergency Management Agency. The USPIRG report showed Vermont sustained $$@$!78,355,625 in damage covered by insurance and federal money.
However, weather experts said individual bouts of severe weather, like the ice storm and floods, cannot be blamed solely on global warming.
"There are other factors involved and one is climatic variability. Different events in our atmosphere
tend to go in cycles,'' State Climatologist Lesley-Ann Dupigny-Giroux said. She noted that Vermont could be in a cycle that tends to create particularly severe storms.
Almost all climatologists agree that the earth has warmed by at least one degree this century. The burning of fossil fuels adds carbon dioxide to the atmosphere, and that gas traps heat, warming the earth, scientists said. The warming might contribute to more severe heat waves and droughts, and could create increasingly powerful storms.
A Nov. 12 conference is scheduled at the University of Vermont to examine how rising global temperatures might affect Vermont's economy. Warmer temperatures would impact farmers, the ski industry and tourism, said George Tyler, an organizer of the conference. If disasters become more common, the state will end up spending more money on road, property and utility repairs, he said.
"The report presents a pretty powerful argument that the scientific community isn't kidding when they say that climate change may be the most important issue we face in the next century,'' Tyler said.
So far this year, the state's weather has not been as severe as in 1998. Still, state and federal authorities are mulling whether to declare parts of Vermont a disaster area because of damage to utilities and roads caused by Tropical Storm Floyd in September, Lindsay said. The state also suffered damage from a summer drought and heat waves, along with severe July thunderstorms.

DTI backs emissions trading CLIMATE CHANGE LEVY GOVERNMENT SAYS IMPACT WILL BE OFFSET BY OTHER MEASURES: [London edition]

Author: Brown, Kevin

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/dti-backs-emissions-trading-climate-change-levy/docview/248879651/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The government sent a clear signal yesterday that it will allow emissions trading to offset the impact of the climate change levy on companies proposed by Gordon Brown, the chancellor. The Treasury and John Prescott's Department of the Environment, Transport and the Regions have refused to say whether they will accept an emissions trading scheme being drawn up by industrialists. However, the trade and industry department makes clear in its response to a critical report on the proposed levy by the Commons trade and industry committee that emissions trading is part of government policy on pollution control.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=DTI+backs+emissions+trading+CLIMATE+CHANGE+LEVY+GOVERNMENT+SAYS+IMPACT+WILL+BE+OFFSET+BY+OTHER+MEASURES%3A&amp;author=Brown%2C+Kevin&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-27&amp;spage=04&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The government sent a clear signal yesterday that it will allow emissions trading to offset the impact of the climate change levy on companies proposed by Gordon Brown, the chancellor.
The Treasury and John Prescott's Department of the Environment, Transport and the Regions have refused to say whether they will accept an emissions trading scheme being drawn up by industrialists.
However, the trade and industry department makes clear in its response to a critical report on the proposed levy by the Commons trade and industry committee that emissions trading is part of government policy on pollution control.
The response does not say how such a scheme might work. But the Confederation of British Industry and the Advisory Council on Business and the Environment are expected to give details today of proposals put to ministers.
The DTI response says the climate levy, which is intended to help the UK meet pollution control obligations set at Kyoto, will "sit alongside other approaches, including emissions trading, regulation, voluntary action and negotiated agreements".
The DTI says it welcomes the CBI/ACBE initiative, and shares its aim of getting a scheme into operation "well in advance" of 2008, when pollution targets must start being met.
The report also signals that energy efficient combined heat and power plants may be exempted, and that it may back down on its refusal to allow tax incentives for investments to improve energy efficiency.
The report says the government thinks "CHP generation can make a valuable contribution to achieving emissions reduction, and is concerned that the final arrangements for the levy do not discourage the development of CHP."
It is considering tax incentives "very carefully" in the light of claims that efficient new generating technology might be otherwise hampered.
The response hints that ministers will find more than the £50m promised for investment in energy efficiency and support for renewable energy.
Business groups were unimpressed. Martin Temple, director-general of the Engineering Employers Federation, said he was "extremely disappointed" that the government's thinking had failed to move on.
Business has argued for up to 95 per cent discounts on the levy for a wide range of companies; more investment in energy saving and renewables; and abandonment of Mr Brown's insistence on raising £1.75bn for recycling through lower national insurance contributions.
AstraZeneca, British Sugar and PowerGen were among companies that lobbied parliament yesterday, claiming that a failure to exempt CHP from the levy could undermine a key source of competition in the power generation market. Competitiveness, Page 6 Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Watch out for the Koreans COMPETITIVENESS ON WEDNESDAY: [London edition]

Author: Brown, Kevin; Wighton, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/watch-out-koreans-competitiveness-on-wednesday/docview/248869423/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Watch+out+for+the+Koreans+COMPETITIVENESS+ON+WEDNESDAY&amp;author=Brown%2C+Kevin%3BWighton%2C+David&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-27&amp;spage=06&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Watch out for the Koreans
The disclosure that Cammell Laird, the ship repair group, is seeking DTI permission to restart shipbuilding at its Birkenhead yard reflects a big increase in competitiveness caused by changes in subsidy rules. Martin Stopford, managing director of Clarkson Research, the shipping consultancy, says Cammell is not alone. There has been talk at other yards of trying to break into the market for sophisticated ships in which east Asian yards do not compete. One promising area is thought to be fast ferries, such as the Australian-built ships operating on the Channel. Watch out, though, warns Mr Stopford. There are whispers that Korea's highly efficient yards may be thinking of having a go at the same market.
* What is Gordon Brown's proposed climate change levy on businesses intended to achieve? Treasury officials recoil in horror at suggestions it is a tax-raising measure designed to allow the chancellor to cut employers' National Insurance Contributions. But the levy will make businesses focus on energy use only if they know how much they are paying. The Treasury is still thinking about whether the tax should be identified separately on fuel bills. But Andrew Warren, director of the Association for the Conservation of Energy, says he has already been told by the environment department this is not likely. Splitting out the levy seems easy enough, says Mr Warren. If the government really wants to change behaviour, that is. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1999. All Rights Reserved.

Climate change levy under attack: [FOREIGN Edition]

Full text: Climate change levy under attack
THE GOVERNMENT faces a two-pronged challenge to its planned climate change levy this week. The British Chambers of Commerce will use its pre-Budget submission to attack the uneven impact that the levy will have on different sectors of the UK economy. The levy is designed to help Britain meet its target of cutting greenhouse gas emissions by 20 per cent by 2010. The British Chambers of Commerce said the plan to recycle the proceeds back to industry by cutting National Insurance payments would penalise heavy industry at the expensive of the services sector.
Meanwhile, a committee of MPs today publishes a report into the levy. In July the Trade and Industry committee said the levy "could prove a blunt instrument which does considerable damage to sectors of the economy already struggling to maintain profitability".

Budget 2000 is Brown's big chance for tax reform: [FOREIGN Edition]

Author: Hogg, Sarah

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/budget-2000-is-browns-big-chance-tax-reform/docview/311563435/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: ALTHOUGH A little more acknowledgement of his inheritance would be seemly - this Chancellor was born with the silver spoon of low inflation in his mouth - Gordon Brown has some cause for his complacency in the state of monetary policy. Now he must turn to the Treasury's day job: budget policy. Gordon Brown's looming surplus, or "war chest", puts him under pressure from an overstretched health service, and for real extra policemen rather than the Home Secretary's statistical artifacts. But he also needs to think about tax. This year, with the exception of national insurance reform, he showed a distressing tendency to tinker. By 2001, he will be in pre-election mode. By 2002, if he is still Chancellor, he may have no breath left for anything but the euro. Budget 2000, on which work now begins, is his big chance for tax reform. First, to recognise weaknesses in existing tax plans. The chart, adapted from the Budget Red Book, shows how the Chancellor has been leaning heavily on certain taxes. But he cannot go on up the excise "escalator" for ever. Higher fuel duties are causing real political trouble, particularly in the countryside.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Budget+2000+is+Brown%27s+big+chance+for+tax+reform&amp;author=Hogg%2C+Sarah&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-25&amp;spage=17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: ALTHOUGH A little more acknowledgement of his inheritance would be seemly - this Chancellor was born with the silver spoon of low inflation in his mouth - Gordon Brown has some cause for his complacency in the state of monetary policy. Now he must turn to the Treasury's day job: budget policy.
Gordon Brown's looming surplus, or "war chest", puts him under pressure from an overstretched health service, and for real extra policemen rather than the Home Secretary's statistical artifacts. But he also needs to think about tax. This year, with the exception of national insurance reform, he showed a distressing tendency to tinker. By 2001, he will be in pre-election mode. By 2002, if he is still Chancellor, he may have no breath left for anything but the euro. Budget 2000, on which work now begins, is his big chance for tax reform.
His must-do list should have at least four items on it.
First, to recognise weaknesses in existing tax plans. The chart, adapted from the Budget Red Book, shows how the Chancellor has been leaning heavily on certain taxes. But he cannot go on up the excise "escalator" for ever. Higher fuel duties are causing real political trouble, particularly in the countryside.
Smuggling has reached such a pitch that it is estimated some 75 per cent of hand-rolled tobacco escapes duty. And built into Mr Brown's numbers are an extra 5 per cent a year on tobacco and 6 per cent a year on fuel, over and above inflation, in each of the next two years. Ouch.
Secondly, to get a grip on eco-taxes. These are God's gift to a Chancellor, providing a moral underpinning to mere money-raising. But a badly designed policy will backfire. Those paying huge petrol duties do notice that the Chancellor wantonly abandoned the (modest) taxation of domestic fuel. The climate change levy is pencilled in to raise a whopping pounds 1.75bn in its first year (2001-02), but its impact is still unclear.
The landfill tax has been successfully launched, but as the rate of tax goes up, more trouble will have to be taken to prevent fly- tipping and use the proceeds to encourage the use of renewable, biodegradable and reusable materials. The traditional mistake is for the Treasury is to concentrate on the numbers while leaving design to other departments. That was how we got the poll tax. Again, ouch.
Thirdly, and for Gordon Brown perhaps most importantly, to decide whether to build a full-blown tax-credit system. The bricks he has put in place for a "bridge to work" have helped some low income families, but have led them into a half-way house whose lay-out is immensely difficult to understand. And the Government has so far hung back from full integration of national insurance for fear of putting the headline rate of income tax up. If it is going to be courageous, Budget 2000 is the time.
Fourthly, to think again about savings taxes. Government policy now hangs on successfully weaning as many people as possible off welfare dependency. An excellent new report from the Institute for Fiscal Studies shows how far from that goal we are.
The new evidence is not so much in the headline findings - that wealth has become more unequally distributed in the past 20 years, and 10 per cent of the population have no assets at all - since anyone who supposed we could get rid of all social security was kidding himself. Where there is real food for thought is in the pattern of assets held by the majority of the population between the extremes of rich and poor.
Most household wealth (about pounds 2.6bn net in the mid-1990s) is in tax-favoured illiquid assets, such as houses. Home ownership among the young peaked in the late 1980s, when tax relief first began to be withdrawn. In short, tax incentives do work. But only if they are well-understood.
At all levels of income, this report shows, more-educated people tend to have more savings. This could be because, following the "life- cycle" model used to forecast savings, highly educated people have more confidence in their future earning power. Or it could be that they are better informed about the inadequacy of social security. Or it could simply be that you need a PhD to understand ISAs.
This report provides further evidence that the complexity of financial products and their tax wrappers is a serious barrier to saving. As chairman of an investment trust, I winced at the report's analysis of the dominance of individual privatisation shares in middle-income portfolios.
I am sure that follows less from a deliberate decision to take risk than from the trusts' failure to communicate ways of holding equities in a form that spreads risk. But there is a lesson for government too: if you want to persuade people to save, you have to make it simple to do so, and you have to go on giving the same message for a long time.
Privatisation shareholding followed a wave of single-minded advertising campaigns. Home ownership took two generations to establish. A nation of savers will be even harder to create, unless both financial services and government cure themselves of their appetite for complexity.
Unfortunately, both have an interest in being able to say that their product is different and better. To be fair to the Treasury, it has - led by the Office of Fair Trading - tried to simplify ISAs by introducing "CAT" standards. But the basic design was over- complicated, and, though improved, still is. The wider problem is that - as the table makes clear - the savings terrain is criss- crossed with different tax rules, fine in theory, daunting in practice.
The deeply rooted instinct of the Treasury is to simplify by levelling taxes up. That would be a mistake, in both policy and politics. The middle classes spent the past two years breathing sighs of relief, but electors are not long grateful for small mercies. Now they are beginning to notice that capital gains tax is biting, despite the changes, and that familiar income tax reliefs are finally disappearing. The last vestige of mortgage interest relief goes next April, as does the marriage allowance, with a delay before the promised child tax credit is introduced.
Meanwhile, the middle classes are being asked to pay higher national insurance, despite being told that it is not going to provide them with anything much in future. They are nervous about pensions relief.
Moreover, since financial activity is even more mobile than tobacco, capital taxes are an area where governments have to be particularly sensitive to international competition. In the Anglo- Saxon world, Australia is now following the United States in bringing down the rate of capital gains tax.
If he embarks on a wide tax-reforming Budget, the Chancellor could take the opportunity to upstage his opponents by abolishing stamp duty and cutting CGT. Now that would cause them to mutter: "ouch".

People: Brown, Gordon

Industry launches plan to stall climate change levy: [FOREIGN Edition]

Author: Michael Harrison Business Editor

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/industry-launches-plan-stall-climate-change-levy/docview/311561055/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: BRITAIN'S BIG energy users are accelerating plans to launch an emissions trading system in an effort to stall the introduction of the Government's climate change levy. The Confederation of British Industry will announce this week that it intends to have a full-blown trading system in operation by next April - a year ahead of the levy being introduced. Originally, the CBI had planned to launch a trial system, enabling a few firms to start trading emissions permits among themselves by the end of this year. The full system would then have been launched towards the end of next year in advance of the introduction of the climate change levy in April 2001.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Industry+launches+plan+to+stall+climate+change+levy&amp;author=Michael+Harrison+Business+Editor&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-25&amp;spage=17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: BRITAIN'S BIG energy users are accelerating plans to launch an emissions trading system in an effort to stall the introduction of the Government's climate change levy.
The Confederation of British Industry will announce this week that it intends to have a full-blown trading system in operation by next April - a year ahead of the levy being introduced.
Originally, the CBI had planned to launch a trial system, enabling a few firms to start trading emissions permits among themselves by the end of this year. The full system would then have been launched towards the end of next year in advance of the introduction of the climate change levy in April 2001.
Now, however, the CBI believes it can have the system up and running much earlier. Successful operation of a permit trading scheme will be vital if industry is to persuade the Government that such a system will be just as effective as an energy tax in ensuring that Britain meets the emission reduction targets it signed up to at the Kyoto summit.
At a meeting between government ministers and energy intensive users last week, it was suggested that both Stephen Byers, the Secretary of State for Trade and Industry, and Michael Meacher, the Environment minister, were becoming more sympathetic to industry's case.
However, the Treasury minister, Stephen Timms, was said to have refused to budge from the announcement set out by the Chancellor in the last Budget. The climate change levy will cost industry pounds 1.2bn, with steelmakers alone facing a bill of pounds 238m.
The intention is to recycle the proceeds from the tax by cutting employers' National Insurance contributions. However, industry calculates that this will only reduce its bill by pounds 140m a year.
The levy is designed to help Britain meet its target of cutting greenhouse gas emissions by 20 per cent of their 1990 levels by 2010.
However, industry argues that such a tax will only have the effect of rendering British business uncompetitive and forcing investment to go abroad. It also argues that fiscal penalties are not the effective way of cutting energy use and thereby reducing emissions.
The CBI is expected to propose a series of options for operating an emissions- trading system, including auctioning of the permits.
The Government has offered some industries, including steel, chemicals and pharmaceuticals, a 50 per cent rebate on the climate levy. But they argue that this is inadequate.

NAC Orchestra makes best of musical cliche: [Final Edition]

Author: Lloyd Dykk, Sun Music Critic

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/nac-orchestra-makes-best-musical-cliche/docview/242761345/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The musicians at least seemed to believe in the music. [Pinchas Zukerman] took the customary dual role of solo violinist and conductor, conducting with his bow, and quite loosely, often strangely --at times his style seemed more like choreography than conducting. Hardly anybody in the string orchestra looked at him for a cue, and why should they? By now they can play this program in their sleep. But they didn't play sleepily, at least after the Spring concerto, which felt automatic. On his 1670 Guarnerius violin Zukerman played with virtuosity, fire, lots of ornament, a sensitive line and sometimes a bit too much oily romantic portamento to suit the baroque. There was also a misinterpretation by the first viola of her part in the second movement of the first concerto. The repeated two-note refrain to the shepherd's plaintive violin melody is supposed to be the yap of his dog, not the vibrato-sodden love song she made of it.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=NAC+Orchestra+makes+best+of+musical+cliche&amp;author=Lloyd+Dykk%2C+Sun+Music+Critic&amp;issn=08321299&amp;title=The+Vancouver+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-25&amp;spage=C.12.FR&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: NATIONAL ARTS CENTRE ORCHESTRA
With conductor and violinist Pinchas Zukerman,
Oct. 23 and 24, Chan Centre
As part of its continuing 11-city Canadian tour, the National Arts Centre Orchestra, our light, classically-sized, 46-member orchestral ship of state, pulled into port for two sold-out weekend concerts at the Chan Centre.
Touring, nationally and internationally, is one part of the mandate of the Ottawa-based orchestra, which is 30 years old this season. It's celebrating this anniversary and, no doubt as well, the recent profile-enhancement of attracting to its helm an American icon like Pinchas Zukerman, particularly since the orchestra has spent the last few seasons without a musical director after the departure of Trevor Pinnock.
Zukerman's celebrity is an undeniable boost to the orchestra's image, even though his commitment to the NACO is only eight weeks a year, plus tours and recordings. The Israel-born violinist and conductor retains many ties elsewhere in the United States.
In view of Zukerman's celebrity, the Chan concerts, which were filled to the choral lofts behind the stage, were a sure thing. Even so, extra insurance was taken out with a no-miss program: Vivaldi's The Four Seasons and Mendelssohn's "Italian"Symphony. You might wonder what is served by featuring a cultural cliche like The Four Seasons, which is beautiful, certainly, but something that everybody knows by heart and has been recorded about 150 times. The agenda of this tour is multiple, however. It includes, aside from student workshops and interactive web events on the NAC website, even a commitment, vis-a-vis The Four Seasons, to "the environment and climate change," according to the publicity. This seems a bit of a stretch, something knocked up by a committee. But if anyone spares a tree after listening to Vivaldi (whose music has been around for 250 years) I'll take it all back.
The musicians at least seemed to believe in the music. Zukerman took the customary dual role of solo violinist and conductor, conducting with his bow, and quite loosely, often strangely --at times his style seemed more like choreography than conducting. Hardly anybody in the string orchestra looked at him for a cue, and why should they? By now they can play this program in their sleep. But they didn't play sleepily, at least after the Spring concerto, which felt automatic. On his 1670 Guarnerius violin Zukerman played with virtuosity, fire, lots of ornament, a sensitive line and sometimes a bit too much oily romantic portamento to suit the baroque. There was also a misinterpretation by the first viola of her part in the second movement of the first concerto. The repeated two-note refrain to the shepherd's plaintive violin melody is supposed to be the yap of his dog, not the vibrato-sodden love song she made of it.
Mendelssohn's sunny, infectious, almost literally mercurial Italian Symphony (its rapid, light, breathless pace evokes the beat of winged heels of Mercury, the god of message) was just about all it should have been: racing strings balanced by wonderfully fleet woodwind chords (it's been a while since we've heard horn playing like that from the Vancouver Symphony Orchestra) and a general feeling of vivacity.
The other piece was modern-Canadian, the Polish-born composer Peter Koprowski's Epitaph, a dark, harmonically rich dirge that evokes a cross between Frank Bridge and Debussy, which was gravely and purely played by the NACO string section. The encore, Beethoven's The Creatures of Prometheus, was just as stylish.

Kyoto deal has positives: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/kyoto-deal-has-positives/docview/244809596/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In the energy industry study released to the federal government and obtained by the Herald, the fearmongers are once again clamouring that we mustn't dare take out even a modest insurance policy against the growing threat of climate change for fear that it will devastate our economy. Setting aside for a moment the Quebec ice storm, Manitoba's Red River flood and Calgary's warm winter and cool summer of 1999, I wonder if their study looked at the many fringe benefits of domestically reducing use of fossil fuels.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Kyoto+deal+has+positives&amp;author=&amp;issn=08281815&amp;title=Calgary+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-24&amp;spage=A.16&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Re "Gasoline, heating bills would soar under Kyoto: study," Calgary Herald, Oct. 15.
In the energy industry study released to the federal government and obtained by the Herald, the fearmongers are once again clamouring that we mustn't dare take out even a modest insurance policy against the growing threat of climate change for fear that it will devastate our economy. Setting aside for a moment the Quebec ice storm, Manitoba's Red River flood and Calgary's warm winter and cool summer of 1999, I wonder if their study looked at the many fringe benefits of domestically reducing use of fossil fuels.
These would include reduced levels of acid rain, smog -- which causes 18,000 premature deaths in Canada annually in people with asthma, bronchitis and emphysema -- as well as lung cancer due to vehicle exhaust.
We'd likely see a more physically fit populace, as some people may opt for walking to the corner store instead of automatically hopping into the car to drive three blocks. Streets would be safer with less traffic. We might even get to know our neighbours better while we meet them on the bus or cleaning the front yard with a rake rather than a noisy gas-powered leafblower. And we could position ourselves for competing in the global marketplace for renewable energy.
The individual and societal benefits of relying less on fossil fuels are many and far-reaching; if we listen only to the energy industry, however, we'll ignore these benefits because they are not economically quantifiable, at least not by the fox who wants the gate left off the chicken coop.
Roger Gagne
Calgary

Title: Kyoto deal has positives:   [Final Edition ]

Publication title: Calgary Herald; Calgary, Alta.

Pages: A16

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1999

Publication date: Oct 24, 1999

'70s brings change and growth: [Final Edition]

Author: Porter, Thomas

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/70s-brings-change-growth/docview/344975142/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The 1970s were a period of great growth for the city of Prince Albert said local retailer Herschel Davidner. In his recollection, the city's business community evolved with the arrival of new people. Aside from changes to downtown, [Davidner] said Prince Albert experienced a period of significant growth during the seventies, after seeing the development of the pulp mill and the burning of the last bonded debt in the '60s. "The pulp mill had a huge impact on the city of Prince Albert," said Davidner. "The mill poured money into the development of this city and provided jobs for countless people. They have been, and continue to be, a great community member."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%2770s+brings+change+and+growth&amp;author=Porter%2C+Thomas&amp;issn=08395381&amp;title=Prince+Albert+Daily+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-23&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   As the year 2000 approaches, the Prince Albert Daily Herald is looking back at the history of this region.
Today Herald reporter Thomas Porter goes back to the 1970s when the Raiders came to life, and the building of a mall changed the look of the city forever.
Each month this year the Herald examines the important moments in the history of the region as 1999 moves toward 2000.
- - -
The 1970s were a period of great growth for the city of Prince Albert said local retailer Herschel Davidner. In his recollection, the city's business community evolved with the arrival of new people.
"The downtown used to be a vibrant, thriving retail centre. There were no vacancies," said Davidner. "When the South Hill Mall was built in '72, the retail space in the city was effectively doubled."
It was the beginning of change in the retail sector, said Davidner, one that spelled the end for the downtown shopper.
"When they were talking about building the thing (the mall), I asked myself `Do they expect to have twice the sales with twice the space?"' said Davidner.
Many businesses took to the mall, leaving empty storefronts in the downtown core. Despite the changing face of the city's center, Davidner's Clothing and Western Wear enjoyed favourable sales throughout the '70s.
"We noticed lower sales totals than the previous year for about a year after the mall came in," said Davidner. "Sales gradually returned allowing us to expand our location at the time."
Some retailers didn't weather the climate change as well, said Davidner, with some moving into the new mall and others disappearing all together.
"We used to have three shoe stores on Central Avenue and three high quality menswear retailers," said Davidner. "You can see what's happened to the downtown area. Things have changed and efforts to revitalize have had limited success."
"You didn't see lawyers and insurance agencies on the ground floor," said Davidner. "Second Avenue has become the main drag now and downtown locations that were once prime business locations are going for less money."
Aside from changes to downtown, Davidner said Prince Albert experienced a period of significant growth during the seventies, after seeing the development of the pulp mill and the burning of the last bonded debt in the '60s.
"The pulp mill had a huge impact on the city of Prince Albert," said Davidner. "The mill poured money into the development of this city and provided jobs for countless people. They have been, and continue to be, a great community member."
With an increase in population brought on by new jobs and new money, existing buildings became crowded and translated into a need for a additional space.
Carlton Comprehensive High School was built onto the Technical High School on Sixth Avenue East in the mid-1970s, said Bill Smiley of the Prince Albert Historical Society, along with other familiar landmarks of today.
"A second police station was built where the Northcote Manor stands in 1974," said Smiley. "The new fire hall was also built on 15th Street East."
The J.M. Cuelenaere Public Library took shape in 1972 shortly after the construction of the Comuniplex and new subdivisions like Crescent Heights, said Smiley.
"There was a lot of new development in the 1970s," said Smiley. "The city was growing rapidly and needed new facilities like schools, high rises and the mall."
Former Herald managing editor Wayne Roznowsky, who worked in the sports department at the time, recalls the decade as devoid of economic worry; a time when employment was plentiful.
"If you didn't like the job you had, you just went and found another one," said Roznowsky. "It was a time of great optimism, you didn't need to worry about what you did or didn't get out of university."
He recounted the opening of the mammoth Carlton Comprehensive as the day the high school dynamic changed for good.
"It (Carlton) pretty much absorbed four other schools and brought the total from five to two; St. Mary and Carlton," he said.
Illustration Black & White Photo: Herald file photos / BOOM TOWN -- The 1970s will be remebered by many Prince Albert residents as a time of great growth and change. With low interest rates and a growing population, construction and development was on the rise. With a new mall, schools and housing erupting from the ground, the city skyline would be forever changed. ;
Credit: Daily Herald

Energy levy 'will hit aluminium industry': [CITY Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/energy-levy-will-hit-aluminium-industry/docview/326520569/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Government's climate change levy would increase the aluminium industry's costs by 30 per cent, a major conference was told yesterday. Dr David Harris, secretary of the Aluminium Stockholders' Association, said it represented another blow to manufacturing. Talks were held earlier this week between the aluminium industry and Trade and Industry Secretary Mr Stephen Byers, along with representatives of the Environment Department and the Treasury, about the levy.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Energy+levy+%27will+hit+aluminium+industry%27&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Birmingham+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-22&amp;spage=21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Government's climate change levy would increase the aluminium industry's costs by 30 per cent, a major conference was told yesterday.
Dr David Harris, secretary of the Aluminium Stockholders' Association, said it represented another blow to manufacturing.
Energy consumption has to be reduced by 20 per cent to win a reduction in the levy.
The Government is expected to collect pounds 1.75 billion from the levy but it will reduce National Insurance contributions by five per cent.
Talks were held earlier this week between the aluminium industry and Trade and Industry Secretary Mr Stephen Byers, along with representatives of the Environment Department and the Treasury, about the levy.
The big users of energy in the manufacturing sector would be badly hit by the levy while service industries would virtually escape and still get the benefit of lower NI contributions.
"We do not yet know what the levy will be," said Dr Harris. "All we have is a document from Customs and Excise. The problem we face is that the levy will not be applied in other European countries."
Members of the association from throughout the UK held a meeting at the Marriott Forest of Arden Hotel at Meriden to debate the future of the UK manufacturing industry.
Ms Helen Southworth, chairman of the Commons all party aluminium group, said the Government was recognising the changing needs of industry through its cross-department strategy.
Mrs Sue Battle, chief executive of Birmingham Chamber of Commerce and Industry, and Mr Bill Morris, general secretary of the Transport and General Workers' Union, both said there was a need for more training and higher education had to play a greaterrole in securing the future of the manufacturing industry.
Mr Morris said 50 per cent of his union membership was working in the manufacturing sector.
It was essential for interested groups to come together and create a training culture to ensure that economic stability was not disturbed.

People: Morris, Bill

New energy tax would cost pounds 26m a year, warns union: [CITY Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/new-energy-tax-would-cost-pounds-26m-year-warns/docview/326570074/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mrs Carol Puddephatt, horticultural policy adviser for the NFU East Midlands, said: "Growers will be hit hard if this levy is imposed. They have already made every effort to reduce their use of energy. Many small growers could be forced to leave the industry and jobs will be lost for rural locations. In the report, The Impact of an Energy Tax on British Farmers and Growers, the NFU states that farmers and growers should be exempt from the tax, which is proposed to start in April 2001. Launching the report, NFU deputy president Mr Tony Pexton said: "This levy would have a severe financial impact on farmers and growers. But more than that, it simply would not achieve its environmental targets.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=New+energy+tax+would+cost+pounds+26m+a+year%2C+warns+union&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Birmingham+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-21&amp;spage=25&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A proposed Government tax on energy would cost British farmers and growers pounds 26 million annually while failing to deliver significant environmental benefits.
The protected horticulture sector - one of the most energy efficient in the world - would be worst hit by the Climate Change Levy, a National Farmers' Union report says.
It will result in an average cost of pounds 12,500 per hectare for every grower.
A survey of NFU members shows the increase in energy costs of growing produce would be up to 54 per cent under the new tax, with the extra costs ranging from pounds 1,000 to pounds 1 million per business.
Mrs Carol Puddephatt, horticultural policy adviser for the NFU East Midlands, said: "Growers will be hit hard if this levy is imposed. They have already made every effort to reduce their use of energy. Many small growers could be forced to leave the industry and jobs will be lost for rural locations.
"For horticulture there is an estimated yearly loss in the profit of pounds 12,500 per hectare."
In the report, The Impact of an Energy Tax on British Farmers and Growers, the NFU states that farmers and growers should be exempt from the tax, which is proposed to start in April 2001.
Launching the report, NFU deputy president Mr Tony Pexton said: "This levy would have a severe financial impact on farmers and growers. But more than that, it simply would not achieve its environmental targets.
"Businesses would be forced into ruin or move abroad, simply exporting the problem. Our industry is already one of the most energy efficient and we believe there are other, more effective measures which could achieve the Government's aims."
The NFU opposes the levy because, it says, British growers are already some of the most energy efficient in the world.
With an increase in energy efficiency of 25 per cent over the last ten years, the NFU believes that a further reduction in emissions can be achieved through present and planned measures without the tax.
The levy would cost the industry pounds 26 million while only leading to a reduction in National Insurance Contributions of pounds 9 million.
This financial impact would be too great to be absorbed by the industry, which is made up of hundreds of small businesses, with many farmers and growers forced out of business.
The NFU maintains the tax would not achieve its aim of benefiting the environment.
Many horticulture businesses would move abroad to climates more suitable for growing greenhouse crops while others may switch to using cheaper but less environmentally friendly energy sources.
More than 40,000 people are employed in horticulture, excluding seasonal workers.

Weird weather watch: [Final Edition]

Author: Bueckert, Dennis

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/weird-weather-watch/docview/360966451/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area. Using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to? Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Weird+weather+watch&amp;author=Bueckert%2C+Dennis&amp;issn=&amp;title=Nanaimo+Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-19&amp;spage=A.9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Gordon Toole remembers the day it was so cold that spit froze before it hit the ground.
Toole was one of about 20 men working at a military airport at Snag, Yukon, when the temperature plunged to the deepest low ever recorded in North America.
"You can't breathe through your nostrils in air that temperature, it's almost as though they're blocked or frozen," he says from Snag, about 320 kilometres northwest of Yellowknife. "You have to breath through your mouth but they're shallow breaths.
"You cannot take deep breaths because they just catch in your throat, you're gasping."
Toole recalls the crystal clarity of sounds. He could distinctly hear the barking of dogs in a village some five kilometres away. An airplane overhead was deafening.
As an experiment, the men flipped a saucer of water into the air at waist height: it landed on the ground in hard pellets.
The temperature was so low it couldn't be recorded on a mercury thermometer. Even in an alcohol thermometer, the liquid sank below the lowest temperature mark, settling into the bulb at the bottom.
They marked the point with a small file -- a pen wouldn't work -- and sent the thermometer to Toronto. Days later the reading was confirmed: --62.2C.
The record set at Snag on Feb. 3, 1947, still stands. For Canadians who seemingly never get tired of talking about the weather, it's a classic tale, especially since there were no casualties and no real damage.
Recent years have seen many weather extremes that were not so benign.
Few residents of Eastern Canada have forgotten the ice storm in 1998, when millions shivered in darkened houses or sought refuge in crowded public shelters.
Few residents of Manitoba have forgotten the flood of 1997, when the Red River turned into a vast sea, driving some 28,000 people from their homes and submerging whole towns.
The Saguenay Flood of 1996 caused massive damage, as did the Calgary hail storm of 1997. The current drought in the Prairies has helped to reduce Canada's breadbasket to a basket case as the year 2000 nears.
The Insurance Bureau of Canada estimates that insurance payments for extreme weather events across the country have been doubling every five to 10 years. Payouts last year reached $1.5 billion.
"The weather in Canada has been frightful," says Paul Kovaks, a spokesman for the bureau.
Munich Re of Munich, Germany, one of the world's biggest reinsurance companies, estimates global losses from weather-related natural disasters have increased to $90 billion annually from $7 billion to $8 billion annually in the 1980s.
Part of the rising toll results from population growth -- there's simply more property to be damaged, and more of it is built in risky areas like flood plains and coastlines.
Paradoxically, modern technology can also increase vulnerability to extreme weather since vital energy and communications systems across large areas are intertwined.
"The disaster is the event plus our vulnerability," says federal climatologist Roger Street.
"Higher dependence on networked energy, higher dependence on telecommunications, makes us more vulnerable."
Climatologists would like to come up with an extreme-weather index so they could measure the apparent rise in instability. One of the problems is they can't agree on how to define an extreme weather event.
The great ice storm, for example, did not involve record-setting precipitation. It resulted from multiple factors, none of which would in itself be ranked as highly unusual; it was the combination that was unusual.
The favourite scapegoat for strange meteorological goings on in the 1990s has been El Nino, a periodic warming of the southern and eastern tropical Pacific Ocean, or its twin sister La Nina, a cooling current in the same area.
Climatologist David Phillips says in his recent book Blame it on the Weather that El Nino has become the scapegoat for everything from whale beachings in China to increased crime in Saskatoon.
But using the El Nino as a scapegoat merely pushes the mystery to a deeper level. Why has El Nino been causing so much more trouble than it used to?
Records show El Nino events have been getting stronger since the late 1970s, with the most severe events on record occurring in 1982- 83 and in 1997-98. The longest El Nino on record lasted from 1990 to mid-1995.
Is El Nino a messenger for something much bigger?
Few credible scientists dispute that the climate is warming: the finding has been confirmed by countless studies.
There has been great debate about whether the trend is due to natural variability or human influence. Now that debate also seems to be settled.
Over the past century the average temperature of earth has risen by close to one degree Celsius. Now the big controversy concerns how fast future warming will occur and how it will affect different regions.
Much of the research is done with computer models simulating the atmosphere -- programs so complex that they push existing hardware to its limits. The models have many warts but they're getting better.
"There's some uncertainty, but I feel much safer, much more confident making a weather forecast for the year 2090 than I do an economic or a demographic or a political kind of forecast," says Phillips of the federal Environment Department's atmospheric environment service.
For example, the models show a balmy future for Toronto.
"What Kentucky has now is what in fact Toronto will inherit in terms of its climate probably a hundred years from now.
"We've warmed up Toronto by maybe 0.6 of a degree and now we're talking about warming in the summertime of four to five degrees, five to nine degrees in the winter. There's going to be really a leap forward in terms of the climate."
Canadians may welcome the prospect of milder winters, but what about the prospect of more frequent and severe droughts, floods and storms?
"It is extreme events that will be the biggest impact on Canadians from climate change," says Gordon McBean, assistant deputy to the federal environment minister.
At the 1997 Kyoto climate conference, Canada pledged to reduce its greenhouse emissions by six per cent from 1990 levels by 2008- 2012.
But since then, emissions have continued to rise, and there is still no plan for reversing the trend.
Cutting emissions would require major changes in the way Canadians use energy, and would have an impact on virtually every industry. Disruptions in the near term might be high.
"The cost of action to reduce the risk is today, and the cost of inaction is in the future and hence there's a tendency for discounting the costs of inaction," says Henry Hengeveld, a senior adviser on climate change in the Environment Department.
The fossil fuel industry and the Alberta government have denounced the Kyoto commitment as unrealistic, and skeptics continue to find platforms for their views.

People: Toole, Gordon

____________________________________________________________

Insurers the greens of business;: [Late Edition]

Author: Elias, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurers-greens-business/docview/363395109/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The world's insurance giants are joining forces to urge society to toe the environmental line.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurers+the+greens+of+business%3B&amp;author=Elias%2C+David&amp;issn=2203580X&amp;title=The+Age&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-16&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Headline in Early Edition: Insurers in push for ecology
The yachtsmen who battled the huge storm that claimed six lives during the Sydney-Hobart race last summer saw it as a freak of nature.
Likewise, the owners of the 20,000 homes damaged in Sydney's first big hail storm for eight years might well regard it simply as one out of the box.
So what if it is discovered that mankind had a hand in these events? What if, as a consequence of global warming, storms like these become more commonplace?
Some of the world's biggest insurance companies are asking these questions but, as the frequency and speed of the big winds increase and the cost of claims reaches $US15billion ($A22.8 billion) a year, they say they don't have the time to wait for answers.
The insurance industry has found itself in the eye of an environmental storm and it is preparing to assume its new role as the world's ecological police.
Nearly 90 big underwriters in 27 countries have indicated they are willing to use their financial clout to push industry towards the use of renewable energy sources.
These are the first signatories to a United Nations-sponsored insurance industry initiative, and next year UNEP, the United National Environmental Program in Geneva, will begin a drive to sign up insurance companies in Australia.
Mr Bruce Thomas, the Australian head of non-life reinsurance with the European giant, Swiss Re, says the insurance companies, especially the reinsurers, are feeling the impact of an upswing in severe weather events.
Since the 1960s the number of great natural catastrophes has risen by a factor of three, with economic losses, after adjustment for inflation, rising by a factor of nine and insured losses by a factor of 15.
"We don't know whether it is the result of man-made or man-influenced climate change or whether it is part of a cyclic movement but it will be too late if we wait to find out what is happening and why," Mr Thomas says.
By any measure, 1998 was a disaster. The mean average temperature rose 0.61 degrees and the number of natural catastrophes, which normally ranges between 530 and 600, rose to 700.
The economic loss worldwide from extreme weather reached a staggering $US93billion and insurance companies paid out $US15billion on claims for storms, hurricanes and major flooding on three continents.
In Australia three reinsurers, GIO Re, Reinsurance Corporation Australia and the failed New Cap Re felt the impact of severe storms at home and overseas. They have confirmed losses of more than $1.4billion over the past two years and the latest estimates indicate the insurance bill for the Sydney hailstorm will reach $1.5billion.
The insurance industry says in its UNEP statement that it is well placed to act as facilitators and enforcers of the December 1997 Kyoto protocol.
This international document sets the framework for reductions in greenhouse gas emissions across the world through government policies and international flexible market mechanisms. The chief of these will be emissions-trading and clean-development mechanisms.
Since the Kyoto conference the insurance industry has set down its own framework document that serves notice on the business world, especially manufacturing industry, that it is prepared to judge it by its approach to sustainable development.
It suggests insurers will reward organisationsswitching from carbon fuel to renewable energies and to sanction those who don't. They will include environmental criteria in managing risk when fixing premiums and investment policies.
It points out that as much as one third of investments in global stock markets with a total capitalisation of more than $US15 trillion are managed by the insurance industry and pension fund managers.
"It is therefore in the interest of the industry to understand better the investment opportunities and challenges which will arise from measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and to reduce the vulnerability of society," the UNEP insurance paper says.
In Europe and Japan, where insurance executives live under a thickening cloud of smog, underwriters have been eager to sign the UNEP document and lead by example.
Swiss Re, which writes $19billion of business a year, is developing services and insurance products aimed at managing environmental risks and reducing environmental damage, and increasingly it is including environmental criteria in its investment policy and property portfolio.
Swiss Re has also imposed a strict internal regime that requires its 9000 staff worldwide to cut carbon dioxide emissions by 10per cent by 2001. Where possible staff must teleconference rather than travel by air to meetings and they must separate and recycle their waste.
Its German-based opponent, Munich Re, has gone a step further and set up its own ecological consulting business to carry out environmental-risk analysis for industrial clients. It too insists on practising what it preaches and pays its staff a subsidy to park cars and use public transport.
Yet the farther south one travels towards the hole in the ozone layer the less concern the insurance industry shows.
Only one company in each of Australia, Argentina and South Africa appear as signatories to the UNEP insurance industry statement of Environmental commitment. New Zealand has two signatories and Chile none.
Also the insurance industry is conspicuously absent from the Greenhouse Challenge, a cooperative effort to abate greenhouse gas emissions set up by the Federal Government with industry groups that include many key organisations in the energy, transport and manufacturing sectors.
The Insurance Council of Australia, according to a spokesperson, is conducting a survey of its members for the Federal Government's Greenhouse office. She agreed it was the first step in the process of engagement and might lead eventually to an industry commitment.
Mr Aiko Bode, the coordinator of the UNEP insurance initiative, says the organisation is planning a meeting with insurance and financial service industries in Melbourne early next year.
"We think the time is ripe for Australian insurance companies and banks to come from an observer position into the circle of active players. Whether it is green funds, investment partnerships, the question of good company management and reporting; all of these elements support sustainable development.
"Enough evidence exists to suggest that good management and good environmental performance go hand in hand with above average economic returns. Environmental awareness is less a burden, it is much more a serious business opportunity," Mr Bode said.
"Personally I am convinced that those who care today will be the winners of tomorrow. I hope a few Australian companies are among them."
Mr Raymond Jones, the general manager of the QBE Insurance group, Australia's sole UNEP insurance signatory, says he is hoping that after the meeting his company will no longer be a voice in the wilderness.
He says he has been frustrated by the lack of interest of other insurers in Australia. "We got involved four years ago because we wanted to be a good corporate citizen and we wanted to understand the environment as part of a good risk assessment process."
Mr Jones says it is a slow and frustrating process and so far his company has been unable to take any step beyond public awareness to positive action.
"The marketplace does not allow us to deny cover to a client because of their record of carbon emissions. If we don't cover an errant company there are plenty of other insurers who will," he says.
Mr Phillip Toyne, a partner in the industry environment consultants EcoFutures, says Australian industry is in denial over global warming and still showing few signs of any real awakening.
A former chief executive of the Australian Conservation Foundation, Mr Toyne says he has been trying to interest Australians in green investment funds. He says by way of comparison these funds, which base decisions on strict environment and climate-influencing criteria, have taken off like a rocket overseas and are worth $US1 trillion. Here they are negligible.
He says insurance companies could play a key role. "If they perceive higher risk in terms of environmental issues and adjust premiums accordingly it would send a message to their customers that these are matters of great concern."
However, Mr Alan Tait, the head of Ecos Corporation, which advises business on ecological issues, says he has noticed an awakening of climate-change issues in the past 10 months. He believes the Australian insurance industry has been jolted by its own losses in Sydney and on Hurricane Mitch in Central America into a belated awareness of global warming.
"Until then it lived in its own comfort zone, reinforced by the attitude the Government took to Kyoto; that Australia was special and should be granted easier targets than the rest of the world," he says.
Illustration Caption: Photo: Stemming the storm damage: Might insurers lead the way? Illustration by SPOONER
