NO CANDU Canada would trade one problem for another

Author: Lynch, Claudy

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/no-candu-canada-would-trade-one-problem-another/docview/412716712/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It was pointed out to him, unsuccessfully, that nuclear is not a renewable source of energy in quite the same category as wind or water power and, of course, the safety considerations are always uppermost in everyone's mind. Candu reactors produce no greenhouse gas emissions but leave behind radioactive waste that remains harmful for 10,000 years.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=NO+CANDU+Canada+would+trade+one+problem+for+another&amp;author=Lynch%2C+Claudy&amp;issn=08216983&amp;title=Daily+Gleaner&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-15&amp;spage=&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: "No can do," said Norway's environment minister, Guro Fjellanger, to our prime minister. She didn't add that he had a nerve, but I will say it. How dare he try again to have the sale of Candu reactors declared a credit against our obligations to reduce the production of greenhouse gases. We promised to reduce these by six per cent from 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012. We shouldn't try to weasel out of that.
He keeps trying this. He did it in Kyoto, where we made our pledge.
He did it during subsequent negotiations and he did it again this week, at the international forum on climate change. This attempt to push nuclear energy as green power is not only shoddy, it is downright irresponsible.
Prime Minister Jean Chretien has also insisted that Canada should gain emission credits for exporting the technology to countries such as China. It was pointed out to him, unsuccessfully, that nuclear is not a renewable source of energy in quite the same category as wind or water power and, of course, the safety considerations are always uppermost in everyone's mind.
Meanwhile, the United Nations is studying whether it is possible to measure the contribution of the world's forests to greenhouse gas reduction. Forests absorb huge amounts of carbon dioxide, the primary greenhouse gas. So, Canada, with some of the world's largest forests, has much at stake. That would be a much worthier contribution to reducing the harmful gases. But, then again, the Chr tien government, to outdo the right wing agenda and opposition, might decide that our forests must also be sacrificed to "comprehensive development of our natural resources."
You'd think his misadventure with putting trade ahead of human rights in the case of Indonesia and Suharto would ring a bell in his head and make him avoid trying to push Candu sales ahead of the environment. But, no. He is a repeat offender. Perhaps we should be thinking of a three-strikes-you're-out measure for politicians who try to fool all of the people all of the time. If it was only embarrassing to him, we would just cringe at one more manifestation of his strange knack to do the unexpectedly stupid, but he speaks for us and the country.
I would think it would be embarrassing to his environment minister, David Anderson, whose reputation as an environmentalist is not one to fool around with. I can't believe he would be sold on this particular expediency, no matter how practical he has to be as a politician. But, even he said that, although he understood the safety concerns, he still wanted to put nuclear power forward as part of the solution. Shame. Candu reactors produce no greenhouse gas emissions but leave behind radioactive waste that remains harmful for 10,000 years. So, Canada's position would replace one environmental hazard -- greenhouse gases -- with another, that being nuclear waste.
You'd also think that the recent Japanese nuclear misadventure would at least get his spin doctors clamoring no no, Nanette. Back in 1997, environment groups were outraged by his response to questions raised in connection with a leaked cabinet document concerning Candu exports to Turkey and sales to China. Both countries are shaky in the human rights area. The government was also giving short shrift to the proper environmental assessment. The commitment of the Chr tien government to overseas nuclear sales at all costs was considered breathtaking, at the time. It seemed ready to risk breaking Canada's laws as well as $1.5 billion to do a deal with Turkey.
That's our money folks. Almost the equivalent of what wannabe prime minister Paul Martin is piking out of the unemployment insurance fund, the so-called surplus, created through reducing access to the benefits and throwing people on the provinces' welfare rolls, which they, in turn, with Frank McKenna in the lead, have thrown out onto the street.
Moreover, because of our Candu sales to India, we now have a potential military abuse of nuclear power in that volatile part of the world, where India and Pakistan coexist testily.
What is it that Mr. Chr tien wants? Does he want to give Foreign Affairs Minister Lloyd Axworthy a few more hot spots in the developing world to flex his leadership ambitions on? Maybe his plan is to have Paul Martin and Lloyd Axworthy at each other throat, or falling over each other as to who is going to sell his soul the fastest -- by ignoring human rights or our environment -- while filling the government's coffer with surplus which come so handy to win elections.
We should help developing countries to avoid making the same errors that developed countries have inflicted on our planet. Instead of trying to pawn off our Candu reactors, we should be helping developing countries to choose clean air and water processes for manufacturing and urban development. These will cost more, but that's where we, of the developed world, should be putting our money.
What's more, we should be putting great efforts in developing our pollution control and prevention industry. Let's increase trade in those, instead of selling more reactors which can fall in the wrong hands at the wrong time and which depend on dangerous substances to function.
Meanwhile, Atomic Energy of Canada should be making a mammoth contribution to ensuring safer nuclear power, a safer process and a minimum of waste material to dispose of, in Canada and everywhere in the world. There is still so much left to do to achieve this. That would be an honourable way to exercise our considerable Canadian gray matter in matters nuclear, environmental, political, humanitarian and developmental.
It is shameful enough that the Liberal government in Ottawa is continuing with its agenda to buy our votes with the dough they wrenched out of our hides in unemployment insurance and other safety net benefits, but to try to make themselves look good in terms of environmental protection is beyond the pale. * * * (Claudy Lynch is a former member of Parliament. She writes each Friday from Albert.)

Subject: Alternative energy sources; Environmental protection; Sales; Prime ministers; Nuclear energy; Human rights; Environmentalists

RESEARCHERS GIVE BROADER MEANING TO `BIODIVERSITY' UW-MADISON SCIENTISTS EXPAND THE TERM TO INCLUDE THE ABILITY OF A SPECIES, AMONG A DIVERSE POPULATION, TO SURVIVE TOUGH TIMES.: [ALL EDITION]

Author: Ron Seely Science reporter

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/researchers-give-broader-meaning-biodiversity-uw/docview/391108852/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: For example, [Anthony Ives] said, in a lake damaged by acid rain, the real value of biodiversity might not necessarily just be the numbers of algae species in the lake. Instead, he said, it could be the presence or absence of a species that survives acidification of the lake. By protecting the biodiversity of the lake's algae population, you increase the chances of at least one species surviving, Ives said, so encouraging biodiversity remains crucial.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=RESEARCHERS+GIVE+BROADER+MEANING+TO+%60BIODIVERSITY%27+UW-MADISON+SCIENTISTS+EXPAND+THE+TERM+TO+INCLUDE+THE+ABILITY+OF+A+SPECIES%2C+AMONG+A+DIVERSE+POPULATION%2C+TO+SURVIVE+TOUGH+TIMES.&amp;author=Ron+Seely+Science+reporter&amp;issn=0749405X&amp;title=Wisconsin+State+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-15&amp;spage=3.C&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Quick, define "biodiversity."
Yes, it's one of those words we see all the time, one of those words that seems to roll off the tongue of just about every environmentalist as easily as a quote from Thoreau.
But what, really, is important about biodiversity?
Writing today in the journal Science, a group of UW-Madison scientists has enlarged upon our understanding of biodiversity.
Most of us think of biodiversity as referring simply to the number and variety of living things in a particular place. We think of rain forests, for example, or ocean reefs as being incredible repositories of biodiversity.
But these UW-Madison researchers have expanded upon that theme. Using a complicated mathematical model, they showed that it's not just brute numbers of species that are important. They found that an equally important component of biodiversity is the ability of a particular species within that diverse population to survive tough times.
"What's ultimately important," said Anthony Ives, a UW-Madison associate professor of zoology and the lead author of the study, "is whether you have some species that are tolerant."
Ives worked with Kevin Gross and Jennifer Klug, both co-authors of today's article and UW-Madison graduate students, to fine-tune the mathematical model that was the basis for the findings.
That model, which Ives described as "a formalized mathematical description of a general eco-system," allowed the researchers to study the changes various populations of organisms underwent when placed under environmental stress. Gross compared the model to an analysis of the stock market which looks at the ups and downs of various stocks over time. This model, however, looked at species populations and how they fared when stressed.
In real life, Ives said, those stresses could be everything from climate change to acid rain.
For example, Ives said, in a lake damaged by acid rain, the real value of biodiversity might not necessarily just be the numbers of algae species in the lake. Instead, he said, it could be the presence or absence of a species that survives acidification of the lake.
"If you have a lot of species that are all essentially identical, having more of them doesn't help at all," Ives said.
But by protecting the biodiversity of the lake's algae population, you increase the chances of at least one species surviving, Ives said, so encouraging biodiversity remains crucial.
"This furthers the idea that biodiversity does serve as an insurance policy," Ives said. "When in doubt . . . you're really prudent to hang on to everything."

People: Ives, Anthony

CBI calls for rethink on energy tax Industrialists warn chancellor that levy will damage business

Author: Cowe, Roger

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cbi-calls-rethink-on-energy-tax-industrialists/docview/245440139/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Confederation of British Industry will today attack the chancellor's energy tax plans, which have caused the biggest row between government and business since Labour took office.[QQ] The industrialists will tell Gordon Brown that he must rethink the climate change levy if it is to achieve the objective of cutting pollution and avoid damaging British business.[QQ] Peter Agar, the CBI deputy director-general, said the tax on industrial energy was `fundamentally flawed' and would hit businesses of all types and sizes.[QQ]

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=CBI+calls+for+rethink+on+energy+tax+Industrialists+warn+chancellor+that+levy+will+damage+business&amp;author=Cowe%2C+Roger&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-15&amp;spage=27&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Confederation of British Industry will today attack the chancellor's energy tax plans, which have caused the biggest row between government and business since Labour took office.[QQ]
The industrialists will tell Gordon Brown that he must rethink the climate change levy if it is to achieve the objective of cutting pollution and avoid damaging British business.[QQ]
Peter Agar, the CBI deputy director-general, said the tax on industrial energy was `fundamentally flawed' and would hit businesses of all types and sizes.[QQ]
`Besides being potentially damaging to business, the tax is poorly designed to cut carbon emissions - the very thing it is supposed to do,' he said.[QQ]
The CBI makes its pleas in a special submission to the chancellor, separate from its main comments ahead of the budget report next month.[QQ]
The tax was published by the chancellor in this year's budget, but will not take effect until 2001. It is based on recommendations from a special inquiry under British Airways chairman Lord Marshall. But despite the lengthy consultation and a plan to refund all the money raised through lower national insurance charges, industrialists remain adamant that it will be disastrous for business.[QQ]
In today's letter to the chan cellor, the CBI says it supports international efforts to cut pollution but suggests the energy tax has been designed more to cut labour costs through the national insurance reduction.[QQ]
The business leaders call for six key changes, which they want the chancellor to flag in his pre-budget statement next month. The most significant is to reduce the pounds 1.75bn target the tax is intended to raise.[QQ]
The CBI wants such high-energy industries to be exempted from as much as 95% of the tax, much more than the government's intended 50% discount.[QQ]
Industry also wants Mr Brown to put more money into promoting energy efficiency, use the tax to boost renewable energy sources such as wind and sun, and pay greater attention to emissions trading.[QQ]
A pilot system for trading pollution permits is due to be launched later this month, but critics say this will allow industry to escape controls on emissions, while the government believes this is a longer- term solution which cannot be introduced quickly.[QQ]
Despite the intense anger in industry over the climate change levy, and the threat to the government's business-friendly image, ministers are not expected to be swayed by the CBI's pleas. Mr Brown is likely to announce minor concessions next month but is thought unlikely to accept significant alterations to the structure and implementation of the tax. [QQ]

THRONE SPEECH HIGHLIGHTS: [FINAL EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/throne-speech-highlights/docview/269969145/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Government is committed to prudent fiscal management. It will never let the nation's finances get out of control again. It will keep the ratio of debt to GDP on a permanent downward track. It will deliver on the commitment it made at the beginning of this Parliament to devote half the budget surplus to debt repayment and tax relief, and the other half to investments that address the social and economic needs of Canadians. ... As the nation's finances have improved, the Government has begun to deliver broad-based tax relief -- totalling $16.5 billion over three years. As the nation's finances continue to improve, the Government will further reduce taxes to increase the disposable income of Canadians, enhance innovation and risk taking, and create a more robust economy ... For Canada to generate jobs, growth and wealth, it must have a leading, knowledge-based economy that creates new ideas and puts them to work for Canadians. To do this, it is essential to connect Canadians to each other, to schools and libraries, to governments, and to the marketplace -- so they can build on each other's ideas and share information. Achieving this objective will require new types of infrastructure.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=THRONE+SPEECH+HIGHLIGHTS&amp;author=&amp;issn=11899417&amp;title=The+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-13&amp;spage=A.15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Excerpts of the Throne Speech delivered in Parliament yesterday by Governor-General Adrienne Clarkson .....
CHILDREN AND YOUTH: OUR LEADERS FOR THE 21ST CENTURY
Because of the changing nature of the world economy, the prospects for a high quality of life in any country will depend -- as never before -- on having a population that is adaptable, resilient and ready to learn throughout life. The foundation for this is laid in the very early years. No commitment we make today will be more important for the long-term prosperity and well-being of our society than the commitment to invest our efforts in very young children. Parents and families have the primary responsibility for the care of their children. But all of society must work together to ensure that our children develop the abilities to succeed.
The Government will extend and make more accessible Employment Insurance benefits for parental leave, to help parents take more time from work to spend with their children ... Through further tax relief, it will put more dollars in the hands of families with children. And ... it will work to reform family law and strengthen supports provided to families to ensure that, in cases of separation or divorce, the needs and best interests of children come first.
Federal, provincial and territorial governments are developing together the National Children's Agenda. As part of this work, it is the Government's objective to reach an agreement among governments by December 2000 on a national action plan to further support parents and families.
... To make it easier for families to break the cycle of poverty, the federal, provincial and territorial governments also established the National Child Benefit. The Government of Canada is already investing an additional $1.7 billion annually in low-income families with children, while the provinces and territories are investing in complementary services. ... by 2002, the Government will make a third significant investment in the National Child Benefit...
YOUNG CANADIANS
... The Government will:
- draw on the expertise of young Canadians to help connect rural and urban communities to the information highway, by hiring them to put in place additional Internet access sites for public use;
- create a single-window service -- Exchanges Canada -- to give 100,000 young Canadians every year the chance to learn about another part of the country;
- ensure that younger Canadians -- from age 13 -- are given an opportunity to apply their creative abilities, by providing them with a chance to produce their "first works" using traditional approaches and new technologies in the arts, cultural, digital and other industries;
- actively engage tens of thousands of young Canadian volunteers to participate in community and national environmental projects and to help others improve their literacy skills; and
- enable young Canadians to apply their energy and talents overseas, by participating in international internship programs and helping developing countries get connected to the Internet ....
A DYNAMIC ECONOMY FOR THE 21ST CENTURY
In the global, knowledge-based economy, the advantage goes to countries that are innovative, have high levels of productivity, quickly adopt the latest technology, invest in skills development for their citizens, and seek out new opportunities around the world.
... The Government will continue to build a better environment for economic growth and enhanced productivity by reducing the debt burden, cutting taxes, and making strategic investments. Such investments will help small businesses grow, encourage trade, support citizens in developing the skills they need, and ensure that Canada has modern infrastructure.
The Government is committed to prudent fiscal management. It will never let the nation's finances get out of control again. It will keep the ratio of debt to GDP on a permanent downward track. It will deliver on the commitment it made at the beginning of this Parliament to devote half the budget surplus to debt repayment and tax relief, and the other half to investments that address the social and economic needs of Canadians.
... As the nation's finances have improved, the Government has begun to deliver broad-based tax relief -- totalling $16.5 billion over three years. As the nation's finances continue to improve, the Government will further reduce taxes to increase the disposable income of Canadians, enhance innovation and risk taking, and create a more robust economy ...
In its next budget, the Government will set out a multi-year plan for further tax reduction.
INCREASED TRADE AND INVESTMENT
... To build on Canada's advantage, the Government will increase its trade promotion in strategic sectors with high export potential - - sectors ranging from biotechnology and environmental and information technology to tourism, culture and health. It will also continue to support innovation and the development of new technologies in leading export sectors such as agriculture, agri- food and natural resources.
It will launch Investment Team Canada -- a co-ordinated effort by all governments and the private sector to make the international community more aware of the unique opportunities for investment and growth in Canada. The Government will also modernize legislation to make it easier for global corporations to locate their headquarters in Canada ...
SKILLS AND KNOWLEDGE FOR THE 21ST CENTURY
A skilled workforce and a capacity to innovate continuously are crucial building blocks of a successful 21st century economy ...
Over the next two years, the Government will work with its partners to:
- enable skills development to keep pace with the evolving economy. This work will be led by the Sectoral Councils, which bring together representatives from business, labour, education and other professional groups to address human resource issues in important areas of the Canadian economy;
- make it easier for Canadians to finance lifelong learning; and
- provide a single window to Canada-wide information about labour markets, skills requirements and training opportunities -- on the Internet, over the telephone or in person in communities across the country.
INFRASTRUCTURE FOR THE 21ST CENTURY
For Canada to generate jobs, growth and wealth, it must have a leading, knowledge-based economy that creates new ideas and puts them to work for Canadians. To do this, it is essential to connect Canadians to each other, to schools and libraries, to governments, and to the marketplace -- so they can build on each other's ideas and share information. Achieving this objective will require new types of infrastructure.
... The Government will introduce the legislation necessary to create the Canadian Institutes of Health Research. These institutes will provide a model for world-leading research, bringing together for the first time all the researchers who have an impact on health to undertake shared research priorities.
...The Government will:
- increase its support to the Granting Councils, enabling them to forge new partnerships with our universities to attract the best research minds in the world through an innovative program of 21st Century Chairs for Research Excellence;
- foster greater international research collaboration by Canadian universities and institutes and expand Canadian expertise in such areas as genomics, climate change, and advanced engineering; and
- find new markets for new products and services ...
PHYSICAL INFRASTRUCTURE
... The Government will work with other levels of government and the private sector to reach -- by the end of the year 2000 -- agreement on a five-year plan for improving physical infrastructure in urban and rural regions across the country. This agreement will set out shared principles, objectives and fiscal parameters for all partners to increase their resources directed toward infrastructure. It will focus on areas such as transport, tourism, telecommunications, culture, health and safety, and the environment.
HEALTH AND QUALITY CARE FOR CANADIANS
... The Government will continue to move forward with its provincial and territorial partners and the health care community on common priorities.
With its partners, the Government will support the testing of innovations in integrated service delivery in areas such as home care and pharmacare, working toward a health system in which all parts operate seamlessly.
THE QUALITY OF OUR ENVIRONMENT
.... The Government will introduce legislation and stewardship programs, working with provinces and territories to ensure that species at risk and their critical natural habitat are protected. The Government will also continue to extend Canada's national parks system.
In its own operations, the Government will make itself a model of environmental excellence. It will do more to clean up contaminated federal sites. It will strengthen its capacity for conducting environmental science research. It will also explore new environmental clean-up technologies.
PROMOTING SAFER COMMUNITIES
... The Government will combat drug trafficking while helping communities aid those most affected. It will also launch a program of restorative justice to help victims overcome the trauma of crime and provide non-violent offenders with a chance to help repair the damage caused by their actions.
... The Government of Canada will also continue to work closely with the Government of the United States to modernize our shared border for the 21st century.
Illustration Caption: Photo by Canadian Press Governor-General Adrienne Clarkson delivers the Speech from the Throne in the Senate on Parliament Hill in Ottawa yesterday.

Leaders: Labour's blind spot Be progressive, include the environment

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leaders-labours-blind-spot-be-progressive-include/docview/245460044/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Amidst the New Labour triumphalism, there is one yawning gap which makes a mockery of all Mr Blair's claims to being a progressive: the environment. This is New Labour's blind spot. It is where all its optimistic rhetoric about the key words such as modernisation, radical, change and the future , begins to unravel. Modernisation has to include an environmental agenda: otherwise promising a rosy future is culpable fantasy-mongering. Nor can Mr Blair plead ignorance; the United Nations Environment Programme's predictions for the 21st century (the GEO-2000 report) was published just before the conference season. It laid out what is the most serious political challenge facing the globe. Yet it was barely mentioned in Bournemouth; Mr Blair seems to think he can fob the environmentalists off with a fox hunting ban.[QQ]

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leaders%3A+Labour%27s+blind+spot+Be+progressive%2C+include+the+environment&amp;author=&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-11&amp;spage=17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Amidst the New Labour triumphalism, there is one yawning gap which makes a mockery of all Mr Blair's claims to being a progressive: the environment. This is New Labour's blind spot. It is where all its optimistic rhetoric about the key words such as modernisation, radical, change and the future , begins to unravel. Modernisation has to include an environmental agenda: otherwise promising a rosy future is culpable fantasy-mongering. Nor can Mr Blair plead ignorance; the United Nations Environment Programme's predictions for the 21st century (the GEO-2000 report) was published just before the conference season. It laid out what is the most serious political challenge facing the globe. Yet it was barely mentioned in Bournemouth; Mr Blair seems to think he can fob the environmentalists off with a fox hunting ban.[QQ]
There are three issues over which the government can prove - or lose - its environmental credentials before the next election and all of them are pressing in the next few weeks. Firstly, New Labour's most important manifesto commitment on the environment was a 20% cut in carbon dioxide emissions by 2010. It was a bold target exceeding that agreed in Kyoto, and it delighted environmentalists because it upped the stakes globally, increasing pressure on the US. But what was once a clear commitment has slipped into cautious aspirational language after pressure from the treasury and the department of trade and industry. Environmentalists are outraged and are gearing up for a major campaign. A key point comes later this month at a climate convention in Bonn where the fear is that UK backsliding , once seen as the global leader, will slow momentum.[QQ]
The second issue is the climate change levy proposed by Gordon Brown in his budget last March. The principle of shifting taxation from employment (cuts in national insurance) to pollution was laudatory. But energy-intensive industries are currently waging a vociferous lobbying campaign, threatening dire consequences for British competitiveness, to get generous exemptions; the danger is the policy ends up like Emmenthal cheese, riddled with holes. Thirdly, whether a wildlife bill gets into the Queen's Speech next month is hanging in the balance; if it fails there is a chance the government could go into the next election without a piece of specific environmental legislation to its name. There is widespread agreement that existing legislation protecting wildlife is not strong enough, the question is whether the government will prioritise it for parliamentary time.[QQ]
The truth is that New Labour has never been interested in the environmental agenda as a Fabian pamphlet published this week points out. It regarded it as being anti-business, and feared a collision with Middle England's love of cars and consumerism. The matter has been left to drift; environmentalists describe an alarming degree of ignorance and indifference in Number Ten. But Mr Blair should learn his lesson from the clobbering he got over genetically modified organisms. If he does not, he risks giving ground to Charles Kennedy who has already spotted his weakness; nor is it beyond the realms of reason for the Tories to capitalise on their record under John Gummer and snatch an environmental agenda from under New Labour's nose. The next few weeks are crucial; there is still time for New Labour to remedy its mistake. Mr Blair needs to get to grips fast with the fact that the environment is a crucial part of being progressive not an optional extra. [QQ]
Illustration Caption: article-leader1

People: Blair, Tony

Company: Labour Party-UK

Minister backs off after pondering gas-guzzler tax: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/minister-backs-off-after-pondering-gas-guzzler/docview/433536631/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Insisting that his comments were misinterpreted, [David Anderson] said he has no intention of bringing to the cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on other gas-guzzlers. "I don't propose to target drivers of sport-utility vehicles," he told reporters. He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax - a tax on fuel consumption. After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs might face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse-gas emissions. American research shows the average SUV produces about one-third more carbon dioxide - a major greenhouse gas - per kilometre than the average passenger car. In Canada, sport-utility vehicles face the same fuel-efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Minister+backs+off+after+pondering+gas-guzzler+tax&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=03841294&amp;title=The+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1999-10-09&amp;spage=G.12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Environment Minister David Anderson tried to put the brakes on a furor that erupted yesterday after he warned that a tax on sport- utility vehicles might someday be needed to address climate change.
The comments sent shivers through the auto industry and unleashed a barrage of criticism from motorist and family groups who accused Anderson of attacking their beloved SUVs.
The rookie environment minister, who has quickly earned a reputation for his tough-minded approach to issues, called a late- day press conference yesterday to pour cold water on the suggestion that he favoured a tax on gas-guzzling vehicles.
Insisting that his comments were misinterpreted, Anderson said he has no intention of bringing to the cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on other gas-guzzlers. "I don't propose to target drivers of sport-utility vehicles," he told reporters. He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax - a tax on fuel consumption.
In Vague Terms
After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs might face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse-gas emissions. American research shows the average SUV produces about one-third more carbon dioxide - a major greenhouse gas - per kilometre than the average passenger car.
A transcript of the interview shows Anderson heavily qualified his comments and gave no indication that such a tax proposal was now on the table.
Still, the minister clearly miscalculated public reaction to any attack on the popular SUVs, especially one that raised the spectre of more taxes.
Anderson's comments blindsided auto industry officials who accused the minister yesterday of "political wrecklessness."
"The minister seems to think, quite myopically, that if you address trucks and SUVs you're going to solve the problem but nothing could be farther from the truth," said Mark Nantais, director of the Canadian Vehicle Manufacturers' Association, which represents the country's major car builders.
The government's own transportation panel, charged with offering policy options to politicians, has recommended against the idea of a gas-guzzler tax, he said.
An Inefficient Way
The transportation panel concluded the tax would be an inefficient way to reduce greenhouse gases, costing between $400 and $500 per tonne of emissions. "The government really has to be a little more innovative about how they go about this otherwise they're going to restrict economic expansion," Nantais warned.
A gas-guzzler tax would be applied to the sticker price of a vehicle based on its fuel-efficiency rating. Ontario used to impose a similar tax based on engine size while Britain now has in place incentives to encourage the purchase of smaller cars.
In Canada, sport-utility vehicles face the same fuel-efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.
The popularity of SUVs has skyrocketed in North America and they now make up about 25 per cent of new vehicle sales. Insurance companies have complained the vehicles are so heavy that even fender- benders they're involved in can be expensive to repair and rates may go up as a result.

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/gas-guzzler-tax-sparks-outcry-auto-industry-cries/docview/352476043/se-2?accountid=10226

Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.

Full text: OTTAWA -- Environment Minister David Anderson tried to put the brakes on a furor that erupted Friday after he warned that a tax on sport utility vehicles might someday be needed to address climate change.
The comments sent shivers through the auto industry and unleashed a barrage of criticism from motorist and family groups who accused Anderson of attacking their beloved SUVs.
The rookie environment minister, who has quickly earned a reputation for his tough-minded approach to issues, called a late- day news conference Friday to pour cold water on the suggestion that he favored a tax on gas guzzling vehicles.
Insisting his comments were misinterpreted, Anderson said he has no intention of bringing to cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on SUVs or other gas guzzlers.
"I don't propose to target drivers of sport utility vehicles," he told reporters.
CARBON TAX
He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax -- a tax on fuel consumption.
After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs may face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse gas emissions.
American research shows the average SUV produces about one third more carbon dioxide -- a major greenhouse gas --per kilometre than the average passenger car.
A transcript of the interview shows Anderson heavily qualified his comments and gave no indication that such a tax proposal was now on the table.
Still, the minister clearly miscalculated public reaction to any attack on the popular SUVs, especially one that raised the spectre of more taxes.
Anderson's comments blindsided auto industry officials who accused the minister Friday of "political wrecklessness."
"The minister seems to think, quite myopically, that if you address trucks and SUVs you're going to solve the problem but nothing could be farther from the truth," said Mark Nantais, director of the Canadian Vehicle Manufacturers' Association, which represents the country's major car builders.
The government's own transportation panel, charged with offering policy options to politicians, has recommended against the idea of a gas guzzler tax, he said.
The transportation panel concluded the tax would be an inefficient way to reduce greenhouse gases, costing between $400 and $500 per tonne of emissions.
INNOVATION
"The government really has to be a little more innovative about how they go about this otherwise they're going to restrict economic expansion,"Nantais warned.
A gas guzzler tax would be applied to the sticker price of a vehicle based on its fuel efficiency rating. Ontario used to impose a similar tax based on engine size while Britain now has in place incentives to encourage the purchase of smaller cars.
In Canada, sport utility vehicles face the same fuel efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.
The popularity of SUVs has skyrocketed in North America and they now make up about 25 per cent of new vehicle sales. Insurance companies have complained the vehicles are so heavy that even fender benders they're involved in can be expensive to repair and rates may go up as a result.
Judy Ciufo, government affairs manager for the Canadian Automobile Association, said motorists already pay enough taxes.

Full text: OTTAWA - Environment Minister David Anderson tried to put the brakes on a furor that erupted Friday after he warned that a tax on sport utility vehicles might someday be needed to address climate change.
The comments sent shivers through the auto industry and unleashed a barrage of criticism from motorist and family groups who accused Anderson of attacking their beloved SUVs.
The rookie environment minister, who has quickly earned a reputation for his tough-minded approach to issues, called a late- day news conference Friday to pour cold water on the suggestion that he favoured a tax on gas guzzling vehicles.
Insisting his comments were misinterpreted, Anderson said he has no intention of bringing to cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on SUVs or other gas guzzlers.
"I don't propose to target drivers of sport utility vehicles," he told reporters.
He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax -- a tax on fuel consumption.
After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs may face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse gas emissions.
American research shows the average SUV produces about one third more carbon dioxide -- a major greenhouse gas --per kilometre than the average passenger car.
A transcript of the interview shows Anderson heavily qualified his comments and gave no indication that such a tax proposal was now on the table.
Still, the minister clearly miscalculated public reaction to any attack on the popular SUVs, especially one that raised the spectre of more taxes.
Anderson's comments blindsided auto industry officials who accused the minister Friday of "political wrecklessness."
"The minister seems to think, quite myopically, that if you address trucks and SUVs you're going to solve the problem but nothing could be farther from the truth," said Mark Nantais, director of the Canadian Vehicle Manufacturers' Association, which represents the country's major car builders.
The government's own transportation panel, charged with offering policy options to politicians, has recommended against the idea of a gas guzzler tax, he said.
The transportation panel concluded the tax would be an inefficient way to reduce greenhouse gases, costing between $400 and $500 per tonne of emissions.
"The government really has to be a little more innovative about how they go about this otherwise they're going to restrict economic expansion," Nantais warned.
A gas guzzler tax would be applied to the sticker price of a vehicle based on its fuel efficiency rating. Ontario used to impose a similar tax based on engine size while Britain now has in place incentives to encourage the purchase of smaller cars.
In Canada, sport utility vehicles face the same fuel efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.
The popularity of SUVs has skyrocketed in North America and they now make up about 25 per cent of new vehicle sales. Insurance companies have complained the vehicles are so heavy that even fender benders they're involved in can be expensive to repair and rates may go up as a result.
Judy Ciufo, government affairs manager for the Canadian Automobile Association, said motorists already pay enough taxes.
"We're already paying humongous taxes on gasoline, on licenses, on tires, on air conditioning. It goes on and on," she said, adding a fuel efficiency tax would unfairly target families that need large vehicles.
Environmentalists, meanwhile, welcomed a tax on SUVs and other gas guzzlers as "long overdue." Gerry Scott, climate change campaign director for the David Suzuki Foundation, said a gas guzzler tax could be revenue-neutral with money generated used to subsidize the purchase of fuel-efficient vehicles.
The trend since 1984 toward heavier, more powerful vehicles has offset most of the environmental benefits realized from improved car emissions technology.
With more and larger vehicles on the road, the fuel efficiency of an average vehicle today is only marginally better than what it was in the mid-1980's.

Full text: OTTAWA -- Environment Minister David Anderson tried to put the brakes on a furor that erupted Friday after he warned that a tax on sport utility vehicles might someday be needed to address climate change.
The comments sent shivers through the auto industry and unleashed a barrage of criticism from motorist and family groups who accused Anderson of attacking their beloved SUVs.

Insisting his comments were misinterpreted, Anderson said he has no intention of bringing to cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on SUVs or other gas guzzlers.
"I don't propose to target drivers of sport utility vehicles," he told reporters.
He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax -- a tax on fuel consumption.
After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs may face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse gas emissions.
American research shows the average SUV produces about one third more carbon dioxide -- a major greenhouse gas --per kilometre than the average passenger car.
A transcript of the interview shows Anderson heavily qualified his comments and gave no indication that such a tax proposal was now on the table.
Still, the minister clearly miscalculated public reaction to any attack on the popular SUVs, especially one that raised the spectre of more taxes.
Anderson's comments blindsided auto industry officials who accused the minister Friday of "political wrecklessness."
"The minister seems to think, quite myopically, that if you address trucks and SUVs you're going to solve the problem but nothing could be farther from the truth," said Mark Nantais, director of the Canadian Vehicle Manufacturers' Association, which represents the country's major car builders.
The government's own transportation panel, charged with offering policy options to politicians, has recommended against the idea of a gas guzzler tax, he said.
The transportation panel concluded the tax would be an inefficient way to reduce greenhouse gases, costing between $400 and $500 per tonne of emissions.
"The government really has to be a little more innovative about how they go about this otherwise they're going to restrict economic expansion,"Nantais warned.
A gas guzzler tax would be applied to the sticker price of a vehicle based on its fuel efficiency rating. Ontario used to impose a similar tax based on engine size while Britain now has in place incentives to encourage the purchase of smaller cars.=
In Canada, sport utility vehicles face the same fuel efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.
The popularity of SUVs has skyrocketed in North America and they now make up about 25 per cent of new vehicle sales. Insurance companies have complained the vehicles are so heavy that even fender benders they're involved in can be expensive to repair and rates may go up as a result.
Judy Ciufo, government affairs manager for the Canadian Automobile Association, said motorists already pay enough taxes.
"We're already paying humongous taxes on gasoline, on licenses, on tires, on air conditioning. It goes on and on," she said, adding a fuel efficiency tax would unfairly target families that need large vehicles.

Gerry Scott, climate change campaign director for the David Suzuki Foundation, said a gas guzzler tax could be revenue-neutral with money generated used to subsidize the purchase of fuel-efficient vehicles.
"We cannot continue to jeopardize public health and our climate by allowing our atmosphere to be a free dumping ground,"he said.
The trend since 1984 toward heavier, more powerful vehicles has offset most of the environmental benefits realized from improved car emissions technology.
With more and larger vehicles on the road, the fuel efficiency of an average vehicle today is only marginally better than what it was in the mid-1980's.
Anderson, since taking over the environment portfolio in August, has made it known within the department that he intends to forge a green legacy for the Liberal government. He has already vowed to introduce strong legislation to protect endangered species that invokes the government's criminal law power to protect crucial habitat.
And on the long-stalled federal climate change strategy, Anderson has expressed his frustration at the lack of action and has made it clear he will impose government solutions if voluntary reductions are not stepped up.
As part of an international treaty, Canada agreed in Kyoto, Japan to reduce its greenhouse gas emissions by 6 per cent from 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012.
Since most of Canada's greenhouse emissions come from the burning of coal, oil and gasoline, it means reaching the target will require a major shift in energy production and use.

Full text: OTTAWA -- Environment Minister David Anderson tried to put the brakes on a furore that erupted Friday after he warned that a tax on sport utility vehicles might someday be needed to address climate change.
The comments sent shivers through the auto industry and unleashed a barrage of criticism from motorist and family groups who accused Anderson of attacking their beloved SUVs.
The rookie environment minister, who has quickly earned a reputation for his tough-minded approach to issues, called a late- day news conference Friday to pour cold water on the suggestion that he favours a tax on such vehicles.
Insisting his comments were misinterpreted, Anderson said he has no intention of bringing to cabinet a proposal that would increase taxes on SUVs or other gas guzzlers.
"I don't propose to target drivers of sport utility vehicles," he told reporters.
He also repeated his vow not to support the introduction of a carbon tax -- a tax on fuel consumption.
After a speech Thursday, Anderson suggested in vague terms that SUVs may face new taxes designed to discourage their popularity as part of the effort to control greenhouse gas emissions.
American research shows the average SUV produces about one third more carbon dioxide -- a major greenhouse gas --per kilometre than the average passenger car.
A transcript of the interview shows Anderson heavily qualified his comments and gave no indication that such a tax proposal was now on the table.
Still, the minister clearly miscalculated public reaction to any attack on the popular SUVs, especially one that raised the spectre of more taxes.
Anderson's comments blindsided auto industry officials who accused the minister Friday of "political recklessness."
"The minister seems to think, quite myopically, that if you address trucks and SUVs you're going to solve the problem but nothing could be farther from the truth," said Mark Nantais, director of the Canadian Vehicle Manufacturers' Association, which represents the country's major car builders.
The government's own transportation panel, charged with offering policy options to politicians, has recommended against the idea of a gas-guzzler tax, he said.
The transportation panel concluded the tax would be an inefficient way to reduce greenhouse gases, costing between $400 and $500 per tonne of emissions.
A gas-guzzler tax would be applied to the sticker price of a vehicle based on its fuel efficiency rating. Ontario used to impose a similar tax based on engine size while Britain now has in place incentives to encourage the purchase of smaller cars.
In Canada, sport utility vehicles face the same fuel-efficiency standards as light trucks, requiring them to achieve a minimum fuel efficiency level of 11.4 litres per 100 kilometres. Passenger cars must meet a standard of 8.6 litres per 100 kilometres.
The popularity of SUVs has skyrocketed in North America and they now make up about 25 per cent of new vehicle sales. Insurance companies have complained the vehicles are so heavy that even fender benders they're involved in can be expensive to repair and rates may go up as a result.
Judy Ciufo, government affairs manager for the Canadian Automobile Association, said motorists already pay enough taxes.
"We're already paying humongous taxes on gasoline, on licenses, on tires, on air conditioning," she said, adding a fuel-efficiency tax would unfairly target families that need large vehicles.
Environmentalists, meanwhile, welcomed a tax on SUVs and other gas guzzlers as "long overdue."
Gerry Scott, climate change campaign director for the David Suzuki Foundation, said a gas-guzzler tax could be revenue-neutral with money generated used to subsidize the purchase of fuel- efficient vehicles.
"We cannot continue to jeopardize public health and our climate by allowing our atmosphere to be a free dumping ground,"he said.
The trend since 1984 toward heavier, more powerful vehicles has offset most of the environmental benefits realized from improved car emissions technology.
With more and larger vehicles on the road, the fuel efficiency of an average vehicle today is only marginally better than what it was in the mid-1980's.
